The Red Page
Modern Plays
Arnold Bennett is a disappointing
dramatist. He can occasionally give the
public what it wants; he has humor, some
sense of characterisation, and some ideas;
hut one feels that he regards his play-writ-
ing as play. He does not devote to the
drama that fine seriousness and careful
craftsmanship that he puts into his great
novels; rather his plays belong to that
Arnold Bennett who can and does with a
cynical light-heartedness write “billy-
boilers” in the intervals between his serious
works. And his essavs for the stage show
no evidence of an improving dramatic tech-
nique. In What the Public Wants he gave
us a clever satire upon the half-penny
press; in The Honeymoon he relapsed into
a mechanical and unconvincing comedy; in
Milestones he, aided by Edward Knoblauch,
developed an original idea —the authorship
of which is not stated- —in a convincing
way; but now, in The Great Adventure
(Methuen), he becomes again merely the
“slick” writer for a not too critical audi-
ence. This play is apparently founded on
an early novel of his, Buried Alive, and if
looks like the usual dramatised novel. With
the exception of two characters all the per-
sonae are of the traditional conventional
stage kind. And to tell his story Bennett
requires four acts, each divided by the
dropping of the curtain into two scenes.,
Practically, therefore, he needs eight acts.
There is no dramatic objection to this de-
vice, of course, but the modern dramatist
who shows so little control over his material
lays himself open to the charge of clumsi-
ness.
Ilam Carve, an illustrious English artist,
is so shy that he is unable to correct the
mistake made by the doctor called in to
treat his dying valet that the sick man is
the artist. And as the valet Carve allows
the valet’s body to be buried in Westminster
Abbey. It is not only his relief at this easy
way of escaping the adulation of the public
that keeps Carve silent: it happens that he
meets a charming girl. As the valet he
marries her, and lives happily in a suburban
cottage, sustained on the joint income
afforded by his wife’s small dividends and
a small annuity. The matter-of-fact Janet
says: “What good has it ever done him (the
alleged dead Carve) to be celebrated and
make a big splash in the world? Was he
any happier for it? For all I can hear he
was always trying to hide, just as if the
police were after him.”
Janet him to fool about with
paints, as it keeps him out of her way when
she’s tidying the house. Then come the
inevitable farcical complications. An art
dealer recognises the hand of the dead
master in some of his recent paintings, sold
for pocket-money by Carve to a local frame-
maker. Also the dead valet’s wife arrives,
and identifies him as her absconding hus-
band of 26 years ago. And the art dealer,
who has been selling to an American col-
lector the recent work of the artist, is sued
by the collector for fraud. And both sides
are determined to produce Carve in the N
witness-box. But a whisper comes from
“influential quarters” that, as the English
nation has buried a valet in the Abbey, the
matter must be hushed up. So London is done
out of an amusing trial, and Bennett allows
us to see that trial practically take place in
private. By the disclosure of the hackneyed
old mole that has been on the back of the
neck, and occasionally on the left arm, of
every hero and heroine of melodrama, the
identity ot Carve is established, and we
leave him wondering: “Last time they
buried me in the Abbey—what will they do
with me next time?”
In the character of Janet, Bennett has his.
sole excuse for this comedy. And she is a
fine excuse. The charming, matter-of-fact
woman has seldom before been so delight-
fully put upon the stage. Her solid sense
gives reality even to the fantastic plot.
Janet supplies a convincing argument
when her husband is “identified” by the
valet’s wife as her bloke. “There’s no doubt
the poor creature once had a husband who
did run off. And it seems fairly clear his
name was Albert Shawn, and he ,went away
as a valet to an artist. But then, on the
other hand, if there is one thing certain in
this world, it is that you were never
married before you married me. That I
will swear to.”
But what is to be said for a modern
dramatist who thinks it necessary to pro-
vide a “curtain” at this cost? Carve is
awkwardly folding up a pair of trousers —
this is before their marriage—-while he asks
her to lunch with him at his hotel on the
morrow.
Janet (timidly): I should like to.
(Suddenly) Here, give me those trousers,
do! (She takes hold of one leg, Carve re-
taining the; other.)
(Enter Cyrus Carve.)
Cyrus: Oh !
Curtain.
The latest Shaw appears in Overruled, a
dramatic study in the May issue of that ex-
cellent magazine, Tiie English Review.
As usual, in this one-act play,. Shaw does
not worry to invent a plot. He just snatches
the nearest one. It happened that Mr. and
Mrs. Lunn determined to take a holiday sep-
arately. On the same day that Lunn starte.d
to go round the world in one direction, his
wife set out, with the same intention, in the
opposite direction. Curiously enough, Mr.
and Mrs. Juno had the same idea. And,
strangely, Mr. Juno travelled all the way
with Mrs. Lunn, while Mr. Lunn had for
travelling companion Mrs. Juno. By a re-
markable coincidence Lunn and Mrs. Juno
arrived in the lounge of a seaside hotel on
their arrival in England on the same day
that Juno and Mrs. Lunn happened—
amazing that such things could be! —to
reach the same lounge in the same hotel.
And —so romantic is this life of ours—
Gregory Lunn was in love with Mrs. Juno,
and Juno was infatuated with Mrs. Lunn.
(In the background one can imagine Shaw
shaking the hand of the long hairy arm of
coincidence.)
Some samples of the latest Shaw: —
“Do you know why half the couples who
find themselves situated as we are now be-
have horridly?”
“Because they can’t help it if they let
things go too far.”
“Not a bit of it. It’s because they have
nothing else to do, and no other way of en-
tertaining each other. You don’t know
what it is to be alone with a woman who
has little beauty and less conversation.
What is a man to do? She can’t talk in-
terestingly ; and if he talks that way himself
she doesn’t understand him. He can’t look
at her; if he does, he only finds out that
she isn’t beautiful. Before the end of five
minutes they are both hideously bored.
There’s only one thing that can save the
situation: and that’s what you call being
horrid. With a beautiful, witty, kind
woman, there’s no time for such follies. It’s
so delightful to look at her, to listen to her
voice, to hear all she has to say, that noth-
ing else happens. This is why the
woman who is supposed to have a thousand
lovers seldom has one; whilst the stupid,
graceless animals of women have dozens.”
J*
Here is the other pair:—
Juno: We are the most romantic people
in the world, we English. Why, my very
presence here is a romance.
Mrs. Lunn (faintly ironical ) : Indeed?
Juno : You’ve guessed, of course, that
I’m a married man.
Mrs. Lunn : Oh, that’s all right. I’m a
married woman.
Juno : Thank Heaven for that! To my
English mind, passion is not real passion
without guilt The tragedy of my life
is that I married, when quite young, a
womaa I simply couldn’t help being very
fond of. I longed for a guilty passion: for
the real thing: the wicked thing; and yet
I couldn’t care twopence for any other
woman when my wife was about I
felt my youth slipping by without ever hav-
ing had a romance in my life; for marriage
is all very well; but it isn’t romance.
There’s nothing wrong in it, you see
You get so sick of being happily married.
It’s always the happy marriages that break
up
Juno: But you? Are you really as in-
sensible as you say you are?
Mrs. Lunn : I never said anything of
the kind. I’m not at all insensible by
nature; but (I don’t know whether you’ve
noticed it) I am what people call rather a
fine figure of a -woman.
Juno (passionately) : Noticed it! Have
I been able,to notice anything else since we
met?
Mrs. Lunn : There you go. like all the
rest of them! I ask you, how do you ex-
pect a woman to' keep up what you call her
sensibility when this sort of thing has hap-
pened to her about three times a week ever
since she was 17. It used to upset me and
terrify me. at first. Then I g.ot rather a
taste for it. It came to a climax with
Gregory: that was why I .married him.
Then it became a mild lark, hardly worth
the trouble. After that I found it valuable
once or twice as a spinal tonic when I was
run down: but now it’s an unmitigated
bore. I don’t mind your declaration: I
daresay it gives you a certain amount off
pleasure to make it. I quite understand
that you adore me; but (if you don’t mind)
I’d rather you didn’t keep on saying so.. . .
Juno : Oh, I can’t believe that the voice
of love has ever thoroughly awakened you.
Mrs. Lunn: No, it sends me to sleep.
It’s no use. Mr. Juno: I’m hopelessly re-
spectable: the Jenkinses always were. Don’t
you realise that unless most women w’ere
like that, the world couldn’t go on as it
does?
Then, of course, the husbands and the
wives meet each other, and calmly discuss
the situation with the usual Shavian bril-
liance, and they all go comfortably into
dinner. A delightful bit of fooling, with
Shaw’s hatred of the shams of cbnvention
lighting it as the arc light outside the
darkened lounge lights the faces of his
characters.
A*
Lady Gregory’s New Comedies (Putnam.
per Dymock’s Library, Sydney) are inter
esting contributions to the Irish drama. In
each of these five brief comedies there is that
beautiful speech made familiar to us by the
author, Yeats, Synge, and the others of the
Irish renaissance. Even the chimney sweep
in The Bogie Men can say: “What signifies
chimneys? We’ll go up in them till we’ll
take a view of the Seven Stars! It is out
beyond the hills of Burren I wdll cast my
eye, till I see the three gates of Heaven!”
And when Delia in Darner’s Gold wants the
miser’s gold to buy herself a guinea-hen, she
finds poetry in poultry: “I am no way
greedy, I would ask neither food nor bite,
I would not begrudge turning Sunday into
Friday if I could but get my hearrs desire.
Such a thing now as a guinea-hen would
be bringing fashion to the door, throwing
it a handful of yellow meal, and it in its
speckled plumage giving out its foreign
call!” And Delia has higher flights of
ambition: “What’s goats and what is
guinea-hens? Did you ever see yoked
horses in a coach, their skin shining out
like shells, rising their steps in tune the
same as a patrol of police!” But
McDonough’s skill nvith the pipes lifts the
Irish mind to splendid hyperbole: “He is a
great one to squeeze the pipes surely....
His father was better again, they do be
saying.... I heard tell, he to throw the
pipes up on top of the rafters, they would
go sounding out tunes of themselves....
There is no person can be safe from him
the time he will put his ‘come hither’ upon
them It’s likely he’d play a notion
into the skulls of the pampootied boys from
Aran, they to be kings of France or of
Germany, till they’d lift their heads to the
clouds and go knocking all before them. And
the police it is likely laughing with them-
selves, as if listening to the talk of the
blackbird would be perched upon a blessed
bush.” Then McDonough’s wail for his
dead wife is the purest of poetry: “Is it
'that you are gone from me, Catherine, you
that were the blossom of the branch!....
I to bring you travelling you were the best
traveller, and the best that ever faced the
western blast, and the waves of it blowing
from you the shawl! Ito be sore in the
heart with walking you would make a smile
of a laugh.... What way did it fail me to
see the withering of the branches of every
bush, as it is certain they withered the
time laughter died with your laugh?”
Jt
Lady Gregory says that she has been
forced to write comedy because it was
wanted for the Irish theatre, “to put on at
the end of the verse play's.” But she thinks
tragedy is easier. “Tragedy shows humanity
in the grip of circumstance, of fate, of what
our people call ‘the thing will happen,’ ‘the
Woman in the Stars that does all.’ ..
Well, you put your ac-tor in the grip of this
woman. Once in the grip you know what
the end must be. You may let your hero
kick or struggle, but he is in the claws all
the time, it is a mere question as to how
nearly you will let him escape, and when
you will allow the pounce. Fate itself is
the protagonist, your actor cannot carry
much character, it is out of place. You do
not want to know the character of a
wrestler you see trying his strength at a
show.”
An interesting theory, but disputable.
For it is the character of your hero that
decides whether your play is to be comedy
or tragedy, whether he is to win or lose the
victory in his struggle with fate. Where
would the tragedy of Hamlet be if he
were not Hamlet?
The Bogie Men and Coats are but farcical
comedy one-acters. Darner’s Gold is a
character cqmedy in two acts. The Full
Moon is finer stuff. In this one-act play
the atmosphere of eeriness is thrillingly
adumbrated and embodied by the influence
of the full moon on a eroup of characters
intensely superstitious. A harmless dog
runs down tLe street, and through the sug-
gestions of a mad woman one after another
of the “sane” characters believes that he
has been bitten by a mad dog. and shows
all the signs of his madness. In the end
the sanest of them all is accused and bound
by his superstitious friends, but by counter-
feiting madness this character. Hyacinth
Halvev, who has appeared in an earlier play
of Lady Gregory, makes his escape. And
the theme of McDonough’s Wife is enough
to make any play. McDonough, the great
Irish piper, returns to his home to find his
wife dead, and no one to “wake” her. or
carry her to burial. McDonough pipes the
whole village in to her funeral.
Bengali Poems
On the seashore of endless worlds children
meet. The infinite sky is motionless over-
head and the restless ivater is boisterous.
On the seashore of endless worlds the chil-
dren meet with shouts and dances.
They build their houses with sand and
they play with empty shells. With withered
leaves they weave their boats and smilingly
float them on the vast deep. Children have
their play on the seashore of worlds.
They know not how to swim , they know
not hov: to cast nets. Pearl fishers dive for
pearls, merchants sail in their ships, while
children gather the pebbles and scatter them
again. They seek not for hidden treasures,
they know - not how to cast nets.
The sea surges up with laughter, and vale
gleams the smile of the sea beach. Death-
dealing 'leaves sing meaningless ballads to
the children, even like a mother while rock-
ing her baby’s 4 cradle. The sea plays with
children, and pale gleams the smile of the
sea beach.
On the seashore of endless worlds chil-
dren meet. Tempest roams in the pathless
sky, ships get wrecked in the trackless
water, death is abroad and children play.
On the seashores of endless worlds is the
great meeting of children.
On the slope of the desolate river among
tall grasses I asked her, “ Maiden, where do
you go shading your lamp with your
mantle? My house is all dark and lone-
some —lend me your light!” She raised her
dark eyes for a moment and looked at my
face'through the dusk. “I have come to the
river,” she said, “to float my lamp on the
stream when the daylight wanes in the
west.” I stood alone among tall grasses
and watched the timid flame of her lamp
uselessly drifting in the tide.
In the silence of the gathering night I
asked her, “Maiden, your lights are all lit—
then where do »"»>»* go with your lamp? My
house is all dark and lonesome —lend me
your light.” She raised her dark eyes on
my face and stood for a moment doubtful,
“f have come,” she said at last, “to dedicate
my lamp to the sky.” I stood and watched
her light uselessly burning in the void.
In the moonless gloom of midnight I
asked her, “ Maiden , what is your quest hold-
ing the lamp near to your heart? My house
is all dark and me your
light.” She stopped for a minute and
thought and gazed at my face in the dark.
“I have brought my light,” she said, “to
join the carnival of lamps.” I stood and
watched her little lamp uselessly lost among
lights.
f
The time that my journey takes is long
and the way of it is long.
I came out on the chariot of the first
gleam of light, and pursued my voyage
through the wildernesses of worlds, leaving
my track on many a star and planet.
It is the most distant course that
nearest to thyself, and that training
the most intricate which leads to the L U
simplicity of a tune. utter
The traveller has to knock at ever,, «v
door to come to his own, and one <
wander through all the outer world * 1°
reach the innermost shrine at the end l °
My eyes strayed far and wide before r
shut them and said. “Here art thou!’’
The question and the cry, “Oh w he rP m
melt into tears of a thousand stream* *
deluge? the world with the flood of the assur
ance I ami’ sur '
I was not aware of the moment when i
first crossed the threshold of this life
What urns the power that made me ooen
out into this vast mystery like a bud in til
forest at midnight!
When in the morning / looked upon the
light I felt in a moment that I was n
stranger in this world, that the inscrutable
without name and form had taken me in iu
arms in the form of my own mother.
Eten so , in deeith the same unlcnowu will
appear as ever known to me. And because
I love this life, I know I shall love death ox
tv ell.
The child cries out when from the right
breast the mother takes it away, in the veru
next moment to find in the left one its con-
solation.
From “Gitanjali,” by Rabindranath
lagore (Macmillan).
“A whole people, a whole civilisation
immeasurably strange to us, seems to have
been taken up into this imagination; and
yet we are not moved because of its strange-
ness, but because we have met our own
image, as though we had walked in
Rossetti’s willow wood, or heard, perhaps
for the first time in literature, our voice as
in a dream.”
Thus W. R. Yeats, in his delicate intro-
duction to Gitanjali (Song Offerings) by the
Bengali poet. Rabindranath Tagore, a col-
lection of prose translations made by the
author from the original Bengali. And
indeed, in this brown poet Yeats has met
his soulmate. The same naive simplicity,
the same pellucid atmosphere of poetry, the
same quiet care of words, are found in this
Bengali poet. We learn that Tagore is the
most famous of Bengali poets: the Ben-
galis. call this the epoch of Rabindranath.
He is also a musician, and Ins songs are
sung from the west of India into Burmah
wherever Bengali is spoken. “He was
already famous at 19 when he wrote his
first novel; and plays, written when he was
but little older, are still played in Calcutta.”
He is also a saint. “Every morning at
three he sits immovable in contemplation,
and for two hours does not awake from his
reverie upon the nature of God.” In the
original these lyrics are said to be full of
subtlety of rhythm. of untranslatable
delicacies of color, of metrical invention.
This volume of 103 poems contains trans-
lations of “Song Offerings” from three of
his books of poetry. Somehow it seems to
us that it is a pity that not more of our
Australian poets are not saints; and if the
daily of _ motionless contemplation
from 3 a.m. to 5 a.m. can produce poems
such as these, our bards are cordially pre-
sented with the recipe.
There is only one competitor for the vacant
English Poet-Laureateship. What is wanted
is a poet who can be depended upon week
after week to produce his poem on passing
events: he must write poems short enough
and simple enough to be understood by his
employer: and he must be able, at a mo-
ment’s notice, to perpetrate a poem about
Any Old Thing. As will be seen by any
careful reader of our “Answers to Corres-
pondents” column, there is only one poet
in the Empire who fulfils all the conditions.

From “Jeames”: —
Alfred Austin, the late British poet laureate, was
the son of a well-to-do Leeds merchant. He went
to the Bar originally, but soon drifted into journal-
ism. Almost from the beginning he seems to have
aspired to be a poet, in much the same spirit as a
comedian often hankers to play tragedy. He was
one of the Standard’s leader-writers for many years,
and as a slashing assailant of Liberalism there were
few to surpass him. But as a poet he was banal.
It has been said of that confirmed cynic, Lord Salis-
bury, that he was never guilty of a bitterer action
than when he made Alfred Austin the Laureate.
With Swinburne, Edwin Arnold and Morris in the
field, the appointment was, of course, grotesque.
Swinburne was ruled out by reason of some of his
earlier verse, both erotic and political; but the other
two big men were aspirants. Punch published a
cartoon showing Austin, Arnold and Morris striving
after the bays—Austin, if I remember aright, in the
garb of a ballet-dancer. It so annoyed him that he
never spoke to Burnand, the then editor, again.
Austin was a great flower-grower, and did most of
his writing at his country home in Kent. “The
Garden That I Love” was about the most human
thing he ever achieved.
The Laureate’s official duties are to compose odes,
lyrics and epic poems in honor of the births of fresh
royalties, or the winning of battles by Britain’s war-
riors. Austin supplied a few works of the sort at
the beginning of his term, but they aroused such
derision that the authorities kindly but firmly re-
quested him to desist. However, the divine afflatus
doggedly refused to be quenched, and he continued,
from to time, to publish poems which elicited shrieks
of delight from England’s enemies. The Boer war
one about the “women and children” in Ladysmith
was appraised by loyalists and disloyalists alike as
the most deplorable thing of the sort that had been
done within the memory of man.
The Laureateship is not worth much even as a
sinecure. The remuneration is, or was, a cask of
sack and £5O per annum. Austin, like Pye, and, I
fancy, some other Laureates, compromised for a
small cash sum every year, in lieu of the sack —-a
horrible concoction which nobody drinks nowadays.
I see that Kipling is mentioned as a possible suc-
cessor. He would be an appropriate choice, as by
the law of averages Britain is now in for a run of
feeble bards. Up to Southey’s time the most hone-
less poet of the day always got the job. Kipling is
uproariously loyal and imperialistic, and his later
poems are as bad as anything of Cibber’s or Rowe’s
or even Nahum Tate’s.
If incompetence is not a prime essential, some of
the Australian verse-writers might have a look in.
Harry Lawson would be an interesting figure about
the Court. And then, speaking in a serious spirit,
there are David McKee Wright, and “Kodak,” and
A. H. Adams and others—all miles above the Austin
standard. The only trouble in the case of these
writers would be the exiguous nature of the stipend.
There has never been a Wages Board for Court
Poets in the United Kingdom. In fact, they haven’t
even got a union. Hence, possibly, the starvation
basis on which they are remunerated.
From a Maoriland cleric (and The
Bulletin has to take his word for it, hav-
ing no inside information of its own) :
All the ecclesiastical world now knows that H. W.
Cleary, the only man in “last, loneliest, loveliest”
Auckland who signs his name with a Roman cross, is
to be offered the placid Archbishooric of Adelaide
when the present occupant, Dr. O’Rcily, retires, which
is expected to be very soon. Of course, all along it
was intended by everyone (except Archbishop Red-
wood) to give Cleary the tiresome job of holding on
a mitre in the windy old town of Wellington. But
arch-orator Redwood has just persuaded the proper
poople to allow him to hand over his shiny shoes, his
buckles and his purple stockings to V.-G O’Shea.
Hence, Adelaide may expect soon to see the clean-cut,
slight, upright present Bishop of Auckland striding
along King William-street. Dr. Cleary is a man of
vast and deep reading, a gentleman, a hater and
destroyer of bigotrv wherever found. Also, he is a
real tiger for flinging round eoiscooal titles ; to-day
Auckland has three mons’gnori, two vicars-general,
four deans, one archpriest, one archdeacon and one
chancellor.
“Jeames”: Lord Charles Beresford take
his Toryism seriously. When he says i u tho
House, or elsewhere, that Radicals should
he boiled he means it. Most Tories don’t
Beresford’s latest feat, according to the
London papers, is to advocate a social bov
cott of all those who disagree with hin
politically; an internecine conflict between
Britain s leading party-givers, so to sneel
Said he bitterly the other day : peak '
We denounce the Radical leaders, and call th
traitors, but in private we retain friendly relaS
and associations with them, which are inconsisw
with sincere, genuine feeling. sistent
This shaft is probably launched nrimariln
at Winston Churchill, who has raked the
earnest, but somewhat wooden-headed old
seaman (or should one say politician?
Admiral Percy Scott has laid it down that
Beresford is no seaman) fore and aft Of
course the furious old man has no hone of
getting his way. If Conservatives began
cutting Radicals socially, English society
as at present constituted, would disintegrate
or bust. One of Churchill’s greatest friends
for example, is his cousin, the Duke of
Marlborough—a Tory of the bluest variety
Another intimate friend is E. E. Smith who
should lead the Conservative party in due
course. The Wimborne family is half Torv
and half Radical; and 20 * others might
readily be mentioned which are in a similar
position. It is just possible that Lord
Charles’s outburst was a mere political gag
so absurd does it seem on any sort of
scrutiny. This view is strengthened by the
fact that he was one of the guests asked bv
Lord Rothschild to Tring recently to meet
the arch-Radical Churchill himself.
About an old genius and his work:
I should think this was unprecedented in literature
—the Australian Journal, with this June number
has begun the publication of Marcus Clarke’s “His
Natural Life” for the fourth time, with Tom Car-
rington’s original illustrations. In the book form
Clarke cut down the story by about one-third, on
Charles Gavan Duffy's advice. The original includes
a description of the Eureka Stockade rebellion, Lalor
being wrought into that part of the yarn. Publisher
Massina told me of the awful trouble he had to drag
the M S. from Clarke, who wrote merely from hand
to mouth, giving the printer nothing ahead. Mas-
sina sivore he would never employ another genius—
and he never did. Clarke's expenses having been paid
to Port Arthur, to get the right “color,” he seemed
to think that ended the business. So harassed was
Massina that he had thoughts of stopping the serial.
But when it was finished there were clamors to have
it over again : so Massina published it the second
time. When Clarke died, it was run through for the
third time. Now a new generation gets the fourth
boiling.
There being quite a number of Freetraders
in the sporting community, The Bulletin
has had a dream of a Freetrade Melbourne
Cup. There would be no tariff to prevent
the young and inexperienced horse being
smothered by its better established compe-
titor. The idea of putting extra weight (or
duty) on one horse (or worker) to bring
him to a level with another horse (or toiler)
would be absolutely abandoned. The blessed
idea of free competition would be strictly
adhered to. And then sport would boom, or
perish, or something. Most probably it
would perish. Joe Cook should look into
this matter. Wherever else the curse of
Protection rages let our Melbourne Cup be
based on pure Freetrade principles.
[James Edmond, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.)

The American Ring Pulveriser
is designed for
Crushing and pulverising limestone, lime, coke, ore,
brickbats, sewer pipe, terracotta, glass, quartz, phos-
phate rock, sandstone, gravel, tile crockery, flint,
manganese ore, oyster shells, stove lining, shale and
all other refractory materials.
Made in Six Sizes.
The various sizes are made with a view of meeting
the requirements of the small, medium and large
consumer.
Crushing and Pulverising to Fines.
Those materials of a refradory nature can be fed to
the machine in 3in. to 6in. cubes and smaller.
g
hfg
The several degrees of fines are secured by installing Grate Bars containing interstices of various widths. A measured
degree of fines is secured by running the materials over a given mesh screen, returning the rejections to the Pulveriser. The
coarser the grind the greater the tonnage capacity, while the finer the grind the smaller the tonnage capacity. The more
dry the material is made, the faster and finer the machine will pulverise.
Strength and Durability.
Careful observations, based upon adtual experience, have resulted in adding to and making extremely strong all parts
of the machine. Extended research and grinding tests year in and year out have brought about the use of Manganese Steel,
a metal that will best resist abrasion and withstand the greatest impafiL

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Dream of Silence.
I reck not of the purple maid.
Nor her whose eyes of bine
The flaxen lashes coldly shade
o’er cheeks of peachen hue;
My taste in girls, I am afraid,
Is heterodox and l new.
Give me the one whose eyes are green,
Whose scarlet lips are hot.
Whose hair is yellow with the sheen
Of gold I have not got—•
A girl who ought to be a queen.
But, happily, is not.
I want one somewhat like a saint,
Whose measured speech is such
She wears the lilies of restraint,
And never says too much.
I want a presence subtle, faint,
That yet is good to touch.
Our introduction need not be
Particularly long,
For such a girl would take to me
As housemaids take to song,
Or little oysterlings agree
With pepper on a prong.
When I have known this dimpled maid
One short half-hour or so,
Together down some sylvan glade
In silence we shall go,
Where kindly vegetables shade
The grassy turf below.
And I shall lie upon the sward
Beneath a pleasant tree,
My head, because the ground is hard,
Upon her silken knee,
As idle as an ancient bard
Who loafed in Arcady.
And there, I think, that dreams will come,
Such dreams as" Sappho knew,
Of love that sets the bees a-hum
And paints the peacock blue,
And in the end gives preachers some
Delightful jobs to do.
I think that I shall see again
The simple vision clear
Of angry persons who got slain
For sweethearts that were dear,
Or tangled up in mazes vain
Of mingled love and beer.
And her green eyes shall shine above.
And her red lips shall glow.
The while her little tender glove
Shall wander to and fro
About my rumpled hair, and love
Shall hid old Time go slow.
She shall be happy as a bird,
And peaceful as a log,
And we shall never say a word
To stir the cheerful fog
Of fancies, pleasant or absurd,
But go the whole sweet hog.
The whole sweet hog of pleasant dreams
Beneath a quiet tree,
While all the world of that which seems
Creeps softly over me,
Like furry surf that whitely streams
Out of a sleepy sea.
We shall not reck of pleasures flown
In all our simple days,
The inner world shall be our own
Far from the talking ways—
I, lying indolently prone.
She, upright in her stays.
N.B.W.
GEORGE STREET.
Sydney.
Lady of Splendor, circled round with hills.
About whose fragrant feet the sunlight
spills,
Set between sea and amorous amber sky,
Queen of the Countless Loves, your love
am I.
Am I not yours, as you, O Queen, are
mine?
The bearded rocks and jutting, age-old tree,
The brown walls washed with light, the sun-
set-wine
That floods across the sky’s fine napery,
The blinding, wrinkled water shot with
skiffs.
The high, white villa-walls and ruddy eaves
That hang upon their little clovered cliffs,
Hidden, like half-seen birds, among the
leaves.
I think that some day, while I overpass
One of these drowsy headlands summer -
hushed.
I’ll find, one windless noon, within the grass.
The print of some god’s body lightly-
crushed ;
And I shall stoop above the perfect thing.
And touch the yet-warm spot where he had
lain
A moment since ; and watch, all-marvelling.
The bruised blades bending slowly up again.
There is a place I know of, where the shade
And sun that cross and merge from rock and
tree
To rich-grassed deeps beneath, have met and
made
Flecked gloom and shadow-pools and mys-
tery—
Where one may glimpse (stealing with toe-
tip tread
No louder than his heart’s hushed diastole),
Among the shifting sun-spots diamonded,
White-bodied Oreads slip from bole to bole.
Yonder, a strip of steel, the seaway lies,
And there, the level drone of hurried men
Hums from the hidden water-lanes, where
plies
The world’s wayfaring back and forth again;
Yet here are clean slopes tumbled from the
sky,
And sheer rocks—weeping vines that, strand
on strand,
Swing down to touch the little bays that lie
Like jewelled fingers laid upon the land.
Lady, whose satraps are the shining hills,
About whose gloried feet the sunlight spills,
Palaced in palm and roofed with amber sky,
Of countless loves your latest love am I.
N.S.W.
J. ALEX ALLAN.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Uusiness letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements. etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thihg to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, Tim Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The perron may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice,
Insufficiently stamped letter; arc liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of. Political, Sociul or other Articles, Verse. Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, SOOO words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs.
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed, ll'hen MS. is forwarded with-
out stamps for return it will be understood that the
copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Personal
applica!ions for return of MS. cannot receive atten-
tion. The Bulletin desires, further, those sending
MS. to distinctly understand that it is not respon-
sible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

Anyone supplying .to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash, of
sufficient merit and interest and adapted for direct
reproduction, or any interesting photograph illus-
trative of a current event, will be paid for same on
acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea, but
unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, JUNE 19, 1913.
THE AWKWARD POSITION OF
JOSEPH COOK.
The final election returns were not to hand
Avhen this paper went to press, but, appar-
ently, Liberalism has a majority of one in
the House of Representatives, and the Refer-
enda questions have been negatived, as it
-were, by the skin of their teeth. The
tragedy of Joseph Cook’s position, as Prime
Minister, is to be found chiefly in the latter
circumstance. He is, amongst other un-
pleasantnesses, in the situation of a man
who, having opposed an increase in salary
in a certain job, finds, to his astonishment,
that he has been pitchforked into the billet
himself. Assuming that a Liberal Adminis-
tration manages to adhere to office for the
next three years, it should acquire a reputa-
tation for ‘whole-souled inertness that will
amaze the world. It may be taken for
granted, on his statements during the late
elections, that Cook will not seek to put
any of his old principles into execution. For
all‘ practical purposes they may be regarded
as dead and buried. No Dreadnoughts wall
be offered to Britain. The naval policy
of “the disloyalists” vull be persevered with
There will be no casing off in the_ matter of
“conscription.” Freetrade, which Hob-
house lias termed “the heart of Liberalism,”
and which every great Liberal from Mill
and Gladstone, down to Asquith and
Mobley and Haldane, has regarded hitherto
as a first essential of the creed, is to be
turned down ; and conscientious Freetraders,
like A. G. Huie, will be requested hence-
forth, figuratively speaking, to go away and
drown themselves, if they intrude at the
Liberal headquarters in Sydney, or else-
where. On the other hand, there is no dan-
ger of anything being done in the direction
of rectifying tariff anomalies. In a fiscal
sense, the party has taken a dope, the effect
of which is guaranteed not to wear off for
36 months. Various prominent Liberals
have given their assurance, during the last
month or so, that the party will not abolish
the progressive land tax ; or exterminate the
Commonwealth Bank ; or do away with the
Baby Bonus; or abandon the Northern Ter-
ritory ; or drop the construction of the Trans-
continental Railway; or block the institu-
tion of the Inter-State Commission ; or re-
call G. H. Reid ; or jettison the White Aus-
tralia policy; or in any way attack any such
manifestations of Socialism as Old Age
Pensions. Certainly a few daring characters
have threatened to engineer the destruction
of the “Gag Act” —the inoffensive, futile, old
Gag Act which has gagged nobody. But
this is too cautious a programme, take it for
all in all, to satisfy a country like Australia.
The times call for something rather less
slumbrous.
There are about two dozen Federal mea-
sures which most intelligent citizens, Liberal
and Labor alike, are simply yearning for. A
Companies Act, for example, and uniform
Banking legislation: an effective Seamen’s
Compensation Act, and an Insurance Act
of the sort that is now in operation in the
United Kingdom. Liberalism, as a result of
its Referenda victory, will be unable to pro-
vide one of them. Take the question of an
Insurance Act —a measure to protect workers
against want during invalidity or unemploy-
ment. The Cook party has officially an-
nounced its intention of legislating in this
direction. Now the essence of such an Act,
wherever it exists, is a system of compul-
sory contributions on the part of employer
or employee, or both. A non-contributory
Insurance Act —meaning a system under
which the Treasury pays the whole of the
premiums—would be unthinkable in any
community that hadn’t declared definitely
for Socialism. Also, the cost would be un-
thinkable. So it may be taken as read that
Cook’s Insurance Bill, if it ever material-
ises, will insist on the employees, if not the
employers, adding their contributions to those
of the Treasury. But the High Court has
decided that, owing to the presence of the
Trade and Commerce clause in the Constitu-
tion, the Australian Parliament has no
power to legislate in regard to the internal
trade and commerce of any individual State.
In other words, Parliament is impotent to
apply an Insurance Act to a purely State
business. If, under a Federal Insurance
Act, the employees at (say) Lassetter’s, in
Sydney, were desired to make weekly pay-
ments to balance the contributions made by
the Commonwealth, those of them who hap-
pened to favor thriftlessness, or who, for
some other reason, disapproved of semi-
Government Insurance, would be in a posi-
tion to decline, and so assist to wrneck the
Act. Well over 90 per cent, of workers in
Australia are employed in purely State con-
cerns —that is to say, in concerns which the-
High Court has declared to be out of the-
ambit of the Federal Parliament. Any
number of them might decline to be bound
by the Insurance Act, -which the Cook
party talks of introducing, and as it is ner-
fectly certain that many thousands of them
would decline to be so bound, the House is
unlikely to waste its time over any measure
of the sort.
It may be said —in fact, the betting is a.
million to one that presently it will be said
—that the Liberal party is not opposed to
what has been called vaguely “reasonable
amendments in the Constitution” designed
to empower the Australian Parliament to
pass and enforce uniform Companies’ legis-
lation, and so on. Towards the finish of the
late election there was a perfect welter of
blither on the subject of reasonable amend-
ments. Nobody took the risk of formulating
a set of “reasonable amendments,” but all
sorts of people who wished to appear
tolerant and catholic-minded, while getting
there just the same in an anti-Referenda
sense, got into the habit of deploring
Labor’s “mad grab at power,” and following
up that complaint with the remark that if
the grab had been slightly less frantic and
comprehensive they would have approved of
it. And they mostly drifted from this
thoughtful and patriotic attitude into a
scandalised denunciation of the idea of hand-
ing over to Federal control “the affairs of
every little tobacconist’s shop, of every
hotel, of every store, of every hawker’s cart,
of every fruit-barrow and of every act of
buying or selling goods, or carrying them.”
(The words in inverted commas are those of
J. H. Carruthers, and they are typical off
the chief argument that was advanced in
opposition to the Referenda.) The point
which all these thoughtful patriots either
missed or ignored was that without giving
the Australian Parliament power over little
tobacconists’ shops and small stores and
hawkers’ carts it will be impossible to em-
power it to legislate in regard to big State
trading companies or banks. The crux off
the whole matter is the phrase “among the
States.” Eliminate those three words from
the Constitution, and the States’ control of
their internal trade and commerce auto-
matically goes by the board. The Aus-
tralian Parliament will be enabled there-
after to pass Insurance, and Companies, and
Seamen’s Compensation Acts and the New
Protection, and the Trade Marks Bill, and
so forth, and be as sure as any human in-
stitution can be of anything that these
measures will not be invalidated bv the High
Court. Incidentally, it will be able to inter-
fere fractiously with the bottle-vending
business; and it will be within its power
to fix the price of beer at 2s. per pint; and
ruin every tobacconist and fruit-barrow
proprietor in the Commonwealth. If
Joseph Cook comes to the conclusion, after
a year or so in office, that, the Australian
Parliament is not likely.* at anv time, to
start harassing petty traders —if. in other
words, he admits the wild improbability off
Australia ever electing a completelv mad
collection of Representatives and Senators
—he will propose, on his own behalf, the
constitutional amendment behind which his
party affected last month to see the most
hideous menaces lurking. In the other event
he will be able to do nothing whatever of
any legislative value. His entire job will
consist in sitting down and being a sturdy
Liberal at £2lOO per annum; a sufficiently
pleasant occupation for him. but ’one that
will arouse no enthusiasm whatever amongst,
the general body of electors.
[John Bede Dalley, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
DEPOPULATED NEW SOUTH WALES.
The State Government of N.S.W. has issued
plans and specifications for a heap of new
railways, which are to be built when it is
possible to borrow the money. Incidentally,
Minister Griffith has, or had a few days
ago, some new patent device for borrowing
the money. The new railways are to “open
up the neglected interior,” to “develop the
country,” to “tap great wheat areas,” to
“populate the inland districts,” and to do
all the other beautiful things of which the
State has heard for such a long, long time.
Of course these lines are all to lead to Syd-
ney, regardless of the fact that, in some
cases, the needless distances arising out of
the fact that the goods must be dragged
away from the port that is almost at the
farmer’s door, and dragged to the port that
is right over the rim of the horizon, are so
tremendous that the wheat will be to a great
.extent eaten up in transit expenses. Also,
they are all to lead to Sydney, regardless of
the fact that the lines thereabouts are already
so badly congested through the struggle to
drag everything to the edge of Darling Har-
bor that this year there are more millions
•of bushels of wheat lost, stranded, bogged,
delayed and bailed up at country stations
than ever before. For the length of a very
respectable lifetime N.S.W. has been build-
ing railways with borrowed money, on the
.Sydney congestion plan, to develop and
CONGESTION.
Despite the great increase in the population
of NS. W. between the years 1901 and 1911,

loan money and the construction of 1000 miles
if new railroad , the area colored black con-

cial year business is to be pursued with re-
newed vigor, according to the old principles,
unless Cohen objects and stops the sup-
plies. A
Some people actually begin to wonder if
the result is worth the effort. There is
plainly nothing to be gained by borrowing
an illimitable wilderness of money to settle
the interior, unless the interior is really
settled. If it isn’t settled then there must
be something wrong with the scheme, and if
a great part of it is becoming more unsettled
than it was many years ago, the scheme is
liable to fall into disrepute, not to say con-
tempt. N.S.W. consists of 141 counties,
and, between the years 3891 and 1901 the
State raised on its lOU a trifle of
£15,000.000, almost entirely for the promo-
tion of wheat and cows and inhabitants
in the inland country. This was a
period of economy and prudence as
compared with the raging financial in-
sanity of to-day, yet £15,000,000 was a fail-
amount of new debt to be incurred by such
a mere handful of people as the folk of the
Ma State. And in these 10 strenuous years
43 out of the 141 counties of N.S.W. lost
population. In other words, despite all the

aboriginal condition. Some counties lost
half their people. Some went even further,

•depression. And the slump in popula-

■and un-railroaded West. It spread

only drawback was, and’ still is, remoteness
from the octopus of Sydney, and lack of
facilities for doing a foreign trade through
ports which are much nearer than Sydney,
the curse of depopulation settled down hard
on the community.

The New Protection and the New
Foreign-Trade.
There are persons here and there
begin to have a suspicion that. pven ts.
the Australian manufacturer, at ai
is concerned, an excessive fuss is j us i
about the increased cost of hyng.
now a Board is sitting, with its rf
on the vexed question. Quite a (be
witnesses estimate that since u p
general price of being alive a* ® 8
fully 35 per cent. That see * special
favorite estimate, though in 5 deal
lines the amount is said to be gtatis-
more. On the other hand, -• • I qoo i the
tician Trivett alleges tha^, s l“ c the Dianu-
average rate of wages paid b• . turer is
facturers of his State (the ma colß .
the only man who has to supp . be go t
plete returns that the wage bd » wom en,
down to a fine point), counting , }
bo.vs, girls flapper*, louts ..
has risen from £S3 lls. ea. pei $
£lOO 7s. 2d. That is an increase <^ traliaD
per cent., so the man who m * , pays
goods with Australian materials,
wages to Australian trades-


apparently done his share to make things
easy for the people. Whether importers'and
shopkeepers and banks and insurance offices
have boosted their wage rates up 20 per eeht.
is another matter. There is a very strong
suspicion that they haven’t even dreamed of
doing so. One boot-dealer who appeared
before the Board last week said that the cost
of hoofgear had recently gone up no less
than 30 per cent., which is quite an excep-
tional rise. Of that extra amount he
reckoned that over 20 per cent, went in extra
cost of leather and other materials, and
rather less than 10 per cent, in extra wages
to the bootmaker. On this calculation the
manufacturer, though he probably, owing to
high prices, requires a larger capital with
which to finance his business, has got none
of the increase. The boot-making workman
has got some of it, and the primary pro-
ducers who supply the bullock hide which is
the basis of boots have secured most of i[.
The manufacturer has quite a lot of out-
standing merits. As already mentioned, he
uses Australian materials and pays wages
to Australian toilers, and his toilers, unlike
those of many other business folk, arc
mostly good unionists and consistent sup-
porters of the Labor ticket on election day.
Above all things, the manufacturer doesn’t'
send money out of the country to pay for
foreign goods, and thus drain the local gold
resources till a harried Ministry, in order to
prevent the community being cleaned out of
coin, rushes to the London market for
another £3.000.000 loan, and thus leaves
another £3,000.000 for the baby to pay. and
adds another £120,000 a year in the shape
of interest to the gold export to make extra
trouble in the future. He is really, on the
average, quite a decent sort of fellow com-
pared with, say. the banker, or trader, or
importer ; but when he is suspected of doing
his employee out of 2d. all the country hears
of it because the Trades Hall takes the
matter up and fills the air with dust and
fragments of argument. Meanwhile some
other people who are of much less use to
Australia can do their employees out of 55.,
and the world hears nothing about it, be-
cause the employees aren’t unionists and the
Trades Hall isn’t appealed to. Labor should
consider this aspect of the case very hard
when it gets its next chance to do a whole
hog amendment of the tariff. So far, it has
declared that it couldn’t give the Australian
manufacturer any better chance to manufac-
ture until it was able, under its New Protec-
tion scheme, to make sure that the employee
would get a fair share of the new profits. Yet
the evidence is fairly clear that trades-union-
ism has been strong enough to secure for the
manufacturer’s employee a constantly in-
creasing share of the profits even with affairs
on the present basis. And, meanwhile, the
importer, who brings in sweated labor and
colored labor goods, and drains the country
of money to pay for them, has been allowed
every facility for a stupendous increase of
his business without any stipulation that he
will share the extra profits with his em-
ployees. There has been a heap of talk about
the New Protection. Has any member of the
Fisher Ministry thought of mentioning the
need for a New Foreign-trade?
[James Edmond. The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
Victimising.
The latest strike of workers at the Vic.
State coal mine should hasten a final settle-
ment of the question as to whether Trades
Unionism recognises or denies the em-
ployer’s right to discharge a unionist for
breaking the contract under which he was
employed. Two men in the Wonthaggi
mine were suspended during the general
manager’s absence for “ceasing work before
the proper time,” whereupon 700 struck
work for the purpose of enforcing the re-
instatement of those two in their billets.
The general manager, on his return to the
scene of trouble, confirmed the act of sus-
pension by dismissing the two men, so the
700 others continued to strike, and 200 more
threatened to join them in idleness. The
900 men complained that the dismissal of
the two was “victimisation,” due to their
having been found smoking in their own
time. The general manager, on the other
hand, talked of “coercion” by the men, and
stated that the output of coal at the mine
had lately decreased 300 tons a day. although
the same number of hands as before
were employed and coal-cutting machines
had been introduced, which were consider-
ably more effective than the old pick. But
it matters nothing to the argument re those
two men whether the miners as a body had
been diminishing their labors or doing their
best. The output of the Wonthaggi mine
will be a matter for discussion in the next
working agreement, which has to be fixed
up before the end of this month. The ques-
tion of a manager’s right to discharge any
workers for committing a breach of the
regulations in any industry is the question
which Trades Unionism has raised so much
dust about of late. The term “victimise”
has been shouted on behalf of more workers
than this alleged pair of shirkers at the Vic.
State coal mine. It implies the new claim
of Unionism to discover a sinister reason
for what the boss asserts to be fair dealing.
It is one of the most misused terms in the
language. Every lazy or insubordinate
schoolboy complains that the masters have
a “derry” on him. Every unsatisfactory
.clerk in an office reckons himself the victim
of somebody’s “spite” when he gets the run.
Every bad actor who is told of his badness
In The Bulletin can give personal reasons
for the animosity of a critic who doesn’t
know him from a crow when he is off the
■sitap'e. To say that the officer who sus-
pended a couple of miners for knocking off
work before the proper time was victimising
them for enjoying a legitimate smoke, and
that the manager approved of the victimisa-
tion, is to charge the bosses with being con-
temptibly foolish persons who make trouble'
)by their sheer cussedness. To admit that
they doubtless thought they were doing a
fair thing by their job, which is the job of
insisting on the fulfilment of the miners’
working contract, would be to admit that the
hasty - strike was an unpardonable act.
Therefore the men don’t admit it. The doc-
trine that unionists can do no wrong except
by endangering the lives of other unionists,
or by working unduly fast, is the doctrine
whereof the complaint of “victimising” is a
recent development. The men never suspect
themselves of victimising an official whose
discharge they demand, but they always find
him guilty of victimising the odd worker or
two whom he wants to discharge. And they
give the boss the option of accepting their
vefdict or putting up with a strike. In this
Wonthaggi case the workers struck before
the head boss had been appealed to. They
denied the management the right to “sack”
anybody without their approval, and in all
industries unionism is doing likewise. The
disputed question of authority is ripe for
settlement.
State Rights versus Imperialism.
It was remarked in this paper more than
two years ago that the American Beef Trust
would only come to Australia with the
object of cornering the British meat supply.
To an institution which thinks in tens of
millions sterling and which has been accus-
tomed for years to arbitrarily fix the selling
price of meat for all America, the patronage
of the handful of people that inhabits these
shores can mean little. But the United
Kingdom is a different proposition alto-
gether. That is a market which is well
worth, exploiting. The Beef Trust has been
established more or less impregnably in
Argentina now, during close on a decade.
The British Government has regarded this
fact with growing nervousness for some time
past. Only last week, for example, the local
daily papers printed this cable message from
London: —
Mr. J. M. Robertson, Parliamentary Secretary to
the Board of Trade, in reply to a question in the
House of Commons last night as to whether any
action had been taken in regard to the operations
of the American Beef Trust in South America in
order to safeguard the British meat supply, said
that the Government was aware that apprehension
had been caused among the British firms engaged in
the Argentine trade, but he understood that the
Argentine Government was considering the situation.
Britain, he added, would view with satisfaction
any steps Argentina might take to secure unre-
stricted trade.
When it is remembered that the Beef Trust
has been particularly careful always to do
nothing in the way of extortion, or what-
not, that might exasperate the Argentinos
—on the principle that it is foolish to quarrel
with one’s bread and butter —it will be under-
stood that Britain’s chances of inducing the
Argentine Government to do anything more
drastic than “consider the situation” are
remote. The only great meat-producing
country in’ the world, besides the United
States and Argentina is, of course. Aus-
tralia. At present, small as its output is,
comparatively speaking, it acts as a very
effective buckler against the Beef Trust’s
efforts to corner the British beef supply. The
Trust is unable to fix the selling price of
meat in London, aS it does in Chicago, by
rea&cin of the fact that at any moment a
shipment .of Australian mutton or beef may
be put on the market. With the removal
of this possibility the Trust’s only difficulty,
aS" regards dictating to Britain what price it
shall pay for one of its staple articles of
diet, must disappear. The local Liberal
party lays claim to a sort of option on the
Imperialistic spirit in the Commonwealth.
It is its custom, more especially at election
times, to hint darkly that all who are out-
side the Liberal fold are “disloyalists.” The
arrival of the U.S. Beef Trust in Queensland
affords it a priceless opportunity to show
w’hether its Imperial protestations are hot
air or otherwise. That Queensland has no
intention of ousting the Trust is quite
obvious. And if Queensland did oust the
Trust it would simply move on to some com-
plaisant State like Tasmania and get busy
there. The business of dealing with this
concern—“in order to safeguard the British
meat supply,” as the Parliamentary Secre-
tary to the Board of Trade put it last week
—is essentially the affair of the National
Government. Sacrifices will need to be
made, of course ; and soon, if the Trust is
to be assailed before it has grown so vast as
to be as impervious to attack here as it is
in its country of origin. The first of them
is what a Sydney Telegraph writer termed
the other day “the right of each State to
control its own industries.” A question that
Liberalism will be driven to ask itself
shortly may be put in this form: “Is it
better for the Empire that the right of each
State to control its own industries should be
retained, or that the right of each Briton to
pay a reasonable price for his meat should be
sacrificed?” He would be a queer sort of
Imperialist who decided to put State rights
before the question of saving about three-
fourths of the inhabitants of the United
Kingdom from blackmail.
[John Bede Dalley, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
The Fool-Farmer and His Wheat.
The Vic. Royal Commission, appointed by
the late Bent or the deceased Pharaoh, to
report on what is known as F.A.Q. (fail-
average quality) wheat and the handling
thereof, has at last handed in a report, in
which it deliberately advocates the cultiva-
tion of more friendly relations with
that pestiferous beast, State Socialism,
and further advises the fool farmer
to vote “Yes” next time he gets the
chance. As to the setting up of the B’.A.Q.
standard, the Commission simply sits
amazed at the folly of the farmer in suffering
it. This standard is fixed each season by
the Cprn Trade Sectional Committee of the
local Chamber of Commerce, an arbitrarily
chosen section of a purely self-elected body,
whose interests as wheat buyers necessarily
clash with those of the farmers as wheat
sellers. A Wheat Standard Committee,
legislatively- appointed, is recommended. The
Commission further remarks that “the
farmers now know that they scarcely get a
fair deal along the long line of the wheat’s
progress to its final destination,” wherefore
it recommends (1) The handling of wheat
in bulk; (2) the organisation of an Inspec-
tion and Warehouse department; (3) a
permanent standard of grading; (4) a
grain survey board; (5) a uniform system
of contracts for the sale of wheat and the
regulation by license and bonds of all dealers
in grain; (6) the erection of country ele-
vators as advised by the Railways
Department, and of terminal elevators at
Williamstown, Geelong and Portland; and
(7) the establishment of a Grain Exchange.
The Commission of six included two Labor-
ites, and they seem to have had much influ-
ence ; but do the fool-farmers expect to get
any relief from a State Parliament like that
of Victoria? They listened to the mendacious
tale of the Argus about the Rural Workers’
Log—and voted “No.” They thought of the
man who does much of their work and is
threatening to ask for 40s. a week and
found—and voted “No.” But they forgot
all about the man who sits two or three
hours daily in a fire-lit, carpeted, cushioned
office and fixes the F.A.Q. standard—and
then buys and sells their wheat on that
standard, and makes his living by doing so.
If they didn’t forget they would vote “Yes”
every time.
[F. O. Jones, Elgar-road, Burwood, Victoria.]
For weeks prior to every election the Argus
and similar prints tell the old tale that,
owing to Labor organisation, the party’s
supporters vote solidly for the ticket, and
would do so even if a Quong was one of the
selected candidates, whereas the “Liberal”
vote is directed only by conscience and
brains. These yarns are considerably dis-
counted by the Senate figures for Victoria,
now practically complete. There is a dif-
ference of 8000 between the votes polled for
the highest Labcfite (Russell) and the
lowest (McKissock), whereas the difference
in the totals of the corresponding Fusion
candidates is only 4000. Yet (a) Renwick,
a Freetrader, is alleged to have split the
“Liberal” vote; (6) Mauger, a red-hot Pro-
tectionist and “extremist,” split the vote
some more, and (c) McColl, the highest
Fusion scorer, undoubtedly obtained a great
number of Yellow Pup votes from Labor
supporters, not that the latter loved Labor
less, but feared Rome more. Having regard
to these things, the gap between the total
of McColl, the first, and Mauger, the last
of the Fusion candidates, must have been
unusually large had not “Liberals” sunk
their principles in regard to Mauger on the
one hand and Renwick on the other, and
voted blindly for the men selected by the
Fusion caucus. The figures therefore show
that individual judgment was exercised to a
greater extent by the allegedly-bound sup-
porters of Labor than by the allegedly-free
“Liberals.” The same condition of affairs
is revealed in the Ma State, where there is
a difference of only 8000 votes between the
head and tail of the “Liberal” trio, whereas
16,000 separate the first and last of the
Labor three. Still these facts will not pre-
vent the Tory press blithering anent Labor’s
superior organisation, and its habit of vot-
ing by machinery, whenever there is a
democratic victory to be explained away.
[Albert John Owen, 129 Grey-street, East Mel-
bourne. ]
One day last week Labor Premier Scaddan
of Westralia got all the applause that was
coming to him when a meeting of Laborites
proposed to hang or drown or burn him in
effigy. The effigy being seized by the police
the gathering had to be content with a mock
trial, and a verdict that the Premier should
be put in a lunatic asylum. The cause of
the row was Scaddan’s alleged failure to
provide work for the unemployed. It would
be interesting to know exactly how much is
expected of the hard-worked Premier. West-
ralia has toiled more enthusiastically than
most States to provide land for the people
to settle on, and cheap money to help them
when they are settled, but the crowd which
made the recent fuss at Perth was not an
agricultural collection, and land didn’t in-
terest it. Successive Westralian Ministx-ies
have borrowed to the last gasp in order to
build public works, which help to keep the
people employed, but there is a point at
which even the last gasp stops. The gold-
fields are dying, but Scaddan isn’t God to
make gold, and he can’t be expected to be at
the salary. As for private employment in
manufactures, no country can both impure
its goods and make them locally. And if
Labor insists on filling its Federal Parlia-
mentary party with fiscal atheists or tariff-
sinkers, who devote themselves to doing the
dirty work of the foreign importer and help
to flood the country with foreign materials,
it can’t expect to get wages locally
for making these same materials. That is a
matter over which Scaddan, as a State
Premier, has no more control than any other
elector. One way and another it looks as if
that meeting of Westralian unemployed
might much more logically have hanged
itself in effigy. The one great needless
curse that is coming home to roost in the
fowl-yard of Labor is the curse of burying
the fiscal issue. When work is deliberately
sent abroad to the extent of some £76,000,000
per annum, what is the use of complaining
that the work has gone abroad and is con-
sequently not at home?
[James Edmond, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
♦
It was another case of taking the line of
least resistance when Prendergast, the
somnolent man under whose leadership the
V ictorian State Labor party never gets
within sight of hope or gunshot of office, sent
a notice to England that there is no room
in Murray’s State for more bottle-makers.
Australia makes a few of its own beer and
pickle receptacles, but not nearly enough to
supply its requirements. It imports the bal-
ance—a very big balance —because the tariff
isn’t high enough to hold the scale even
between the well-paid Australian bottle-
maker and the ill-paid English one. And
when money to pay for imported bottles and
things of the sort becomes scarce in Aus-
tralia this casual country floats a big, casual
loan in England. In this matter, as in other
matters of the kind, there are two courses:
open to. the Australian. One is to re-adjust:
the tariff so that the local bottle will have'
the advantage over the foreign article; to>
stop importing bottles ; to stop floating dis-
astrous loans and heaping up ruinous debts:
to pay for imported bottles; to start new
local industries; and to import bottle arti-
sans mot goods, but men—who will pay
taxes in Australia, and spend their wages ini
Australia, and help to defend Australia, and!
assist to fill up its empty spaces, and do»
their share in carrying the burden of itsr
debts. The other device is to warn bottle-
makers off the premises on the grounds that
Australia is a failure, and is content to im-
port and borrow and drift. The first idea
involves hard work in the way of improving;
the tariff, and establishing new factories,,
and re-organising the Bottle-makers’ Union*
which, incidentally, will mean so many more
votes for Labor on election day. The second
idea is one that any fat and lazy and un-
patriotic loafer is equal to. It is merely a
matter of sitting down hard and letting the
situation drift. And Prendergast is a great
drifter.
[James Edmond, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
The howls of the Tory press against the gag
clause in the Electoral Act of the Common-
wealth are still loud in the land, particularly
in New South Wales and Queensland. Be-
fore (and since) the Federal elections cam-
paign commenced, it was protested by these
literary dervishes that the signing of news-
paper articles was an invention of that habi-
tation of devils, the Labor Caucus, to stifle:
exposure of its wickedness, falsity and!
menace to a community which was hunger-
ing for information on these matters. The
Tory press of New South Wales decided that
the suffering community would have to go
unsatisfied to its doom. On the other hand,
the Queensland section of the Liberal organs
resolved to make an attempt to save the
country, even at the sacrifice of the mysteri-
ous “We.” And this is how it all worked
out (anyhow, up to time of writing) :

Instead of repealing the law which the press
supporters of that harassed politician,
Joseph Cook, say has “gagged” them (and
about the only policy Joseph has so far
breathed into Australia’s listening ear is the
wiping out of the signed article “outrage”),
it would seem a profitable move on Joseph’s
part to enlarge its scope, so that no political
articles, signed or unsigned, should be printed
and published during any future election.

“0.K.”: llenniker Heaton is back in
Australia, swelling with the new idea of
penny-a-word cables. Up to now Henniker is
temporarily immortal as the parent of penny
postage between Britain and Australia, but:
the size of his immortality will be vastly
increased if sixpenny cables come to pass
within his lifetime here below. Personally,
I cannot enthuse worth a cent over Hen-
niker and his ideas, although I have already
saved a few pennies in postage to Britain.
Seems to me that the importers and other
well-to-do persons who had most to gain by
the reduction in the overseas postage rate
were the very ones who should have been
taxed by a raising of it to 3d. To bring the cost
of commercial, financial and stockbroking
cablegrams down to a penny a word would
be a truly sinful piece of class legislation.
And as for the excuse that needy people
here and there occasionally use the cable
service for private, personal purposes—well,
there is no reason why a paternal Govern-
ment should not arrange a special price for
their benefit. Have never cabled to any
friends and relatives in Britain myself dur-
ing 30 years of separation from them. Still, I
might wish one day to send news of my
decease, at a penny a word. But that is no
reason why Shoddy and Co., the importers,
should receive a large annual concession
from the Government which transmits their
orders for foreign goods.

In respect to the Senate the Constitution has
utterly broken down. It is entirely useless for any
Lament and Curse.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(Wailed and sworn on June 12, being the fourth
day of heavy rain and black clouds.)
Lo ! I have done !
I cast from me my umbrella, and it falleth
like a bucket of water cast into exterior
darkness.
My goloshes shall cling no more to my feet,
and my overcoat shall hang like a
discarded shield in the washhouse.
For my patience is exhausted, O Lord, and
my socks likewise, and my linen.
I have ventured abroad in the storms, and,
bit by bit, my armor has forsaken me.
The tirewoman sitteth disconsolate by the
stove and looketh upon washing that is
not so;
For the rain and the wind make sport of the
labor of her hands ;
And the flapping of wet washing in the wind
woundeth her in her bosom.
The things that are dry we cling to as to a
brother.
For lo! a dry garment is more to us than
rubies,
And a pair of hot socks dear beyond all
reckoning.
The wind hath shaken our house, and the
rain hath drummed on our roof-tree.
We have lain down wearied at midnight by
the demons that seem to surround us.
And lo! with the dawn have we risen to hear
the same clamor and riot!
Lord, it is over the odds! Therefore —let’s
to our heavyweight cursing.
curse to be launched at the void.
Out of my heart —out of my heart and my
mouth,
This is my curse aimed at the rain-burdened
South.
May all the flames of the Pit —green, purple,
yellow and red—
Lick up the cloud-veils you wear round your
tempestuous head !
May the dull heat of Hell’s furnace stifle
your winds as they soar—
Stifle and kill them at sea, far from the
flower-decked shore !
Let them not play with the dust —sport with
the grass and the trees !
Keep them from tram-tickets, chaff, cinders
and pleasures like these !
Boil them, and bake them, and fry—grill
them, and devil and roast!
Kill them and keep them away from all
things they like blowing most!
Let them not harry the waves, driving them
north, terror-blind !
Jump on their ear with both feet and
savagely kick them behind !
Blister them! Shrivel them quite! Seal
their pestiferous breath !
Vanquish these furies in black whose icy
lungs whistle with death!
Let the warm winds from the north, mounted
on chargers of white,
Fly to do battle with them. Raiders of joy,
health and light!
Flame to their dwelling-place ! Flame! Steel
to their hearts and their—Eh?
Coming on heavier? D —n it! I’ve done my
best for it to-day !
N.S.W.

[S. H. Prior, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
This paper has been solemnly warned that
the June issue of the Sale (Vic.) Church
News will contain a list of the staff of The
Bulletin, living and dead, stating how long
the deceased members were connected with
the staff, and w’hat particular kind of “vio-
lent means” they died of. The Bulletin
expects a lot of new and startling informa-
tion fiom that Church paper. As regards
most of the departed staff, the coroners’
juries were unable to place the cause of
death, so, if the Rev. G. H. Smith, who
promised the forthcoming revelation, can
throw a flood of spiritual light on those aw-
ful mysteries, it may lead to several arrests
in the office. Moreover, the present mem-
bers of the staff will be glad to know where
their predecessors went to. By the way, the
Church News idea is stale even if the
yarn be new. Not very long ago
another “religious” paper published a
list of Bulletin men who had died
young, or middle-aged, and tw T o of them by
suicide. One of the suicides was also a con-
tributor to some eminently “respectable”
publications. Neither of them was ever on the
staff of this paper, and one had even left off
contributing and left the country years before
his death. Then the pious organ added, to
show the blessedness of the other side of the
case, a list of obscure parsons who had ob-
tained a very considerable venerability. But
it didn’t mention that The Bulletin can
show three healthy and vigorous contribu-
tors aged w r ell over 70. Nor did it allude
to the fact that the Founder of Christianity
“went out” by violence at the age of 33,
and that the Church claims that hundreds
and thousands of martyrs —including the
1.1,000 virgins of Cologne—died horribly
when quite young, and that an apostle
hanged himself. It finished its tale with
the w r ise remark: “By their fruits ye shall
know them!” Well, why not? Judas
Iscariot belonged to the Church—not to
The Bulletin.

The disgruntled Melbourne Age may now
sit down and consider the size of the “vic-
tory” it crowed so loudly about on the Mon-
day after the voting. It refused to support
the Labor party, though the leaven of Pro-
tectionist Labor from Victoria was the thing
Protection had most to hope from; and in
the scuffle that followed it lost the three
most out-and-out Protectionists in Common-
wealth politics—Lyne, Sam Mauger and G.
H. Wise. And, refusing to give the Lab-
orites a hand, it saw Scullin and McDougall,
two of the stoutest Protectionist advocates
in the Common-wealth’s Labor party, go over-
board and drown. If this was a “victory,”
Heaven save the Age from a defeat!
[F. C. Jones, Elgar-road, Burwood, Victoria.]
“D.”: Really, if only in the interests of
the collection plate, it is about time they
stopped discovering fresh Apocrvnha. The
latest one, according to a cable of last week,
describes the defeat of the Devil, the destruction
of the gates and bolts and bars of the inferno, the
extinction of the fires, the overturning of the caul-
drons and the liberation of Adam and Eve.
With the Devil defeated, and Hell utterly
ruined, the necessity for a whole lot of re-
strictions on the moral liberty of the subject
disappears. For my own part, I shall
henceforth commit, without a tremor, such
offences as plural voting, stealing from the
person, surreptitiously putting on the score
at billiards, jeering at defeated Parliamen-
tary candidates, champerty, maintenance,
and throwing the empty glass at the bar-
maid, so often as 1 shall have adjudged the
liquor therein (meaning in the glass, not in
the barmaid) to be bad. The discovery that
Gehenna has been a spent force or myth or
Socialistic tiger for some 1913 years gives
one to think mournfully of all the festivity
that has been given up, as it were under
false pretences, in that period. The world
wduld have been a far cheerier and less
gory place had the Gospel of St. Bartholo-
mew come to hand earlier. A matter for
especial regret, in connection with the great
find, is the way it cheapens one of the most
august figures in literature. The “destruc-
tion of the gates, bolts and bars,” “ the ex-
tinction of the fires,” “the overturning of
the cauldrons” :—it more suggests a riot in
a soup-kitchen than the final scrap of him
•whom Milton termed —
The strongest and the fiercest Spirit
That fought in Heaven.

The American (U.S.) vaudeville co., in-
troduced to Sydney by William Anderson
at the Palace Theatre on Saturday night,
had contracted to provide, in return for the
price of admission, a ‘‘musical scream, en-
titled ‘The Grafters.’ ” “The Grafters”
turned up, likewise the best part of the
musical, but the scream apparently took the
wrong turning. Which is just as well. The
show that is responsible for the A sharp
squeal of the soprano flapper, the harsh,
prolonged squawk of her stout mamma (the
writer has sat beside some mammas whose
false teeth rattled when they squawked),
the bray and splutter of the dairy-fed book-
maker or importer, is a public nuisance.
“The Grafters” is a cobweb of plot upon
which is hung a fair variety of light merri-
ment—that, and no more. Most of the
music is tuneful and, as in the song
“Wouldn’t That Be a Dream?”‘it is quaint,
and some of the character work is excellent.
Harry Burgess, the hayseed constable, and
Bert La Blanc, the Hebrew tramp, need no
press agent to explain what they are. As
for the dancers, the lady with the varie-
gated legs (Miss Eugenie La Blanc), the
afore-mentioned Harry Burgess and the sty-
lish Gertrude St. Clair are hereby invited
out to swing the axe. And there is a herd
of nice chorus girls. On the other hand, the
“gagging” is frequently spread too thick, as
the Carpenter remarked about the butter, and
the hustle for the scream is sometimes too
crude. Also, the advertisement in aid of
some exhibition boosters along the Pacific
Slope is somewhat rubbed into the whiskers
of the audience. Taking it all round the
show might be worse.
