HER MAJESTY'S ,
SYDNEY.
Victor Prince threatens to send the comic cook
(Phil Smith) to Siberia.
Sydney’s Little Theatre has got on to
“Bobby Burnit.” This comedy, by Winchel
Smith, the dramatiser of "The Fortune
Hunter,” of happy memory, deals with high
finance in a back-blocks town of the L .S.
Burnit, deceased, proprietor of the Burnit
Stores, left a curious will, the gist of which
was that, as his son Bobby had shown no
knowledge of business, he would not inherit
his father’s millions until he had convinced
his trustee, unnamed, that he could make
good on his own as a business man. Inci-
dentally, the deceased made a nice girl,
whom both Bobby and he meant Bobby to
marry, his trustee. And for further guid-
ance, he left with his head clerk a series of
sealed letters to be handed to his son at the
various crises of his life. One letter was to
be given to Bobby when he took over the
Stores ; another when he lost the Stores ; and
(as the deceased knew Bobby had
grit) he left a third letter to be given to
Bobby when he had won back the Stores.
Bobby takes to business as if it was polo,
and his infantile attempts to assume the
mantle of his father lead to delightful comedy
and to sudden ruin. In the clever scene in
the second act there is a company meeting
that is hilarious comedy, but suddenly
changes to gravity as Bobby realises that he
is ruined, and has also dropped the whole
of the nice girl’s savings. Henceforward
we see a new Bobby, strenuously engaged in
a life-and-death fight against the forces of
“graft” that have the town in grip. By
showing up the career of the grafter-in-chief,
he and his head clerk manage to drive him
from the town, and Bobby builds on his ruin
the foundations of a fortune. If possible,
“ WITHIN THE LAW," AT MEL-
BOURNE ROYAL.
A snapshot of the gentle art of persuasion in
Amur'ka.
the financial expert of this paper will be
prevailed upon later to draw up the annual
report of the Burnit Stores, with balance-
sheet attached. As far as can be seen,
however, Bobby does not show any business
sense at all. He fights a long, losing fight
till, part by accident, part by trick, he com-
pels a “shark” to hand over the small batch
of shares that aives him the controlling inter-
est in the company. And he buys at auction,
for one-tenth of what it is worth, also by a
fortuitous inspiration, and without any
knowledge of its value, the whole of the
shares of a monopoly. From the moment of
Bobby’s advent into business, till he is left
with a fortune, he has not created one cent
of value. He has merely gambled. How-
ever, that, in America, and possibly else-
where. is “business ability.”
* * * *
Hugh C. Buckler was quite captivating in
his cheerful ingenuousness ; and the change
in his face as, in a few minutes, he saw his
whole fortune crumble at the company meet-
ing, was a real piece of convincing drama.
His subsequent seriousness was equally effec-
tive. After the strain of Shaw, Miss Vio-
let Paget had an easy time, but she gave a
slight character its proper emphasis, and
in the second act attracted ,the keen atten-
tion of the audience by her “Futurist” gown,
evidently designed by a “Cubist” of the most
triangular tendencies. The finest bit of
character-acting among the smaller parts was
that of Kay Souper as the snappy, honest,
shabby clerk. Every gesture and tone
was accurate. Kenneth Brampton, as a
gymnasium owner, and the only person in
the whole group of financiers who actually
made money, took full advantage of his chance
for comedy. Alfred Bristowe, as the other
clerk, was entertaining, and the group of
grafters looked their parts. Special men-
tion must be made of Reynolds Denniston’s
forceful interpretation of the sinister grafter-
in-chief, Sam Stone. For a bustling, excit-
ing and hilarious comedy, “Bobby Burnit” is
awarded a little medal.
Rudolph Besier’s extremely talky comedy,
“Don.” was presented by the Sydney Stage
Societv, at the Palace Theatre, on Friday
evening. In the first act, after a very dull
opening, Stephen Bonington (otherwise
“Don”), a poet, the son of religious though
honest parents, brings home to the Canon’s
house the persecuted wife of a Wowser.
“Don” is quite unaware of the misconcep-
tions engendered in the minds of his parents,
of the girl he is engaged to, and of her
parents, by this, to him, quite natural action.
The woman who was being cruelly ill-treated
by her Wowser, appealed to “Don,” with
whom she was romantically in love, and he
carried her off. On the way, owing to the
girl's illness, they had to stop one night at
an hotel; and this romantic deliverer of
oppressed ladies saw no harm in them
occupying the same bedroom. The infuriated
husband arrives, and demands his wife,
spouting texts and flourishing a revolver.
But already the lady had discovered that
“Don” does not love her, and in the end
she consents to return to her husband, who
is tardily convinced that his suspicions have
no foundation. The play is occasionally
frankly realistic, but, unless propped up by
an actor of more personality than that
shown by Mr. Gilbert Nathan, it fails to
convince. Mr. Nathan failed quite to
gest the quixotic character of “Don,” thou/h
he. showed an intelligent grasp of its minor
points. Miss Gavin Brown, as the f Us <, v
mother of Don Quixote, was natural and in
character. As the Canon, Mr. Harris,™
Allen spoilt by his mechanical gestures an
otherwise excellent interpretation \j7
V ictor Cohen, as the ranting Wowser'
stressed that fanatic’s unpleasantness with
vigor; and other parts were more or less
—probably less —satisfactorily taken by Miss
Ross Brown, Miss Beatrice McDonald Miss
Eileen Hart and Mr. Adrian Stephen ’ Sliss
Cross, as the ill-used and hysterical wife
gave an astonishingly real performance of a
very emotional and difficult part. Her work
was on altogether a higher level than is usu
ally found in the ranks of amateur actor-
dom.

Joseph McCabe, the scientist who knows
more than the other men in a million and
can tell it more charmingly than the other
men in five millions, has now- arranged his
Sydney lectures. They will begin in the
Protestant Hall on July 1, and run over a
week or thereabouts. Carlyle Smythe is
managing the season; and if he can expand
the little Protestant Hall so that it will
hold all the people who ought to hear
McCabe, there is a great future before him
in Sydney-—especially at Easter Show-time.

The Overland Limited Express, loaded
with hilarity and sprinkled w r ith confetti, is
still running on time at Sydney Criterion.
The management ought to present a free
pass to the N.S.W. Raihvay Commissioner.
“Excuse Me,” by its quaintness of setting
and its realistic “sticking up” scene, has
caught on with the Sydney public. Its
chief fault is that that admirable comedian.
Fred Niblo, has not the opportunities that
were his in the previous productions.

The Flying Banvards, the half-dozen new-
comers at Sydney Tivoli, would make their
turn infinitely more attractive if they
occasionally scored a mishit and landed on
the back of the neck. As it is, the accuracy
becomes painful. But if it’s accuracy they
aim at, they do it remarkably well. Lucy
Gillet, the juvenile German acrobat, juggler
and furniture remover, still refuses to take
life seriously ; and De Mario and Lady, the
contortionists, the Quaint Q’s and Roosters
(both last nights) and the Kremkas and
Kebble Sisters all get their share of pro-
gramme space.
The life of Sydney Adelphi heroine this
time is to be short, but crowded. On Friday
night “The Girl Who Knew a Bit" will be
forcibly removed, and on Saturday
Anderson will put in “Life’s Shop Window."
No plot has yet been discovered among the
suffragettes to break it.

There is a good programme at Adelaide
Tivoli, especially on the musical side —with
Reynard, Sydney Bach and Rosella, the
harpist. Corson Clarke puts up a humorous
“sketch” with Miss Dale-Owen, and the
American cross-talk includes some worth-
while jokes. Madge Temple, too, has at
last been able to bring her frocks and her
songs to Adelaide. The King’s has a varied
bill, ranging from Mysto’s magic to the
Lessos’ juggling and shooting; from Desper-
ado’s funny acrobatic work to Rivenhall’s
really-good comic songs, without any red-
nose foolishness. Mr. and Mrs. Rumford-
Butt seem more popular at the Exhibition
the longer they remain, but it is desperately
cold barn for concerts in winter. The chief
thing in the picture-line is a big Italian idea
of Satan, Miltonic and modern.
sfc *
The other night Melbourne Town Hall
held a large and hearty audience when Mary
Conly shivered in an atmosphere that was
on the point of freezing. The buxom lady
gave of her best in Roger Quilter’s “Come
Back,” Schumann’s “Dem Helden,” and
Landon Ronald’s elegy to dear Adonais.
Later her finished exuberance turned “Er-
nani involani” from a trite and tattered
warble into a treasured recollection. Maggie
Stirling’s contralto softly thawed the frozen
chunk of air in a rich “Delilah” item, and
Louis Hattenbach, with the vertical hair
and disorganised coat-tails, warmed his
’cello in Gossman’s “Tarantella,” wherein
the ’celloist became a musical express with
the accompaniste toiling heavily in his wake.
“The Girl Behind the Counter” at Mel-
bourne Bijou adds no glory to the record of
the persevering amateur organisation that
ventured to rush in where “The Grafters”
had ceased to tread. It is the patchy
musical comedy in which Brett of yore used
to hanker after nuttiness and wherein Crom-
well made a huge success on the
first night by happening to split his pants.
The music is neither frequent nor very
catchy, except for the charming “Won’t You
Buy” duet and one or two other numbers.
In short, it is not a likely piece for any
amateurs to set the Yarra ablaze with.
When they are not brilliant, and the comedy
wants a lot more acting and staging than
they can give it, the performance needs a
friendly audience to keep it warm. In this
case the pretty duet suffered severely from
the want of a staircase exit for the prima
donna, and the chorus girls were mostly
handicapped in the first act by costumes
which Natpre had not built them to adorn.
In the second act, where W. Ottey, as the
waiter, gave a passable imitation of W.
■ Cromwell, the show was less dispiriting.
Miss Schnabel, as Winnie, displayed several
qualifications, but lacked the vim of self-
confidence. The baritone’s disqualifications
included a clumsy method of uttering his
song words. J. T. Moore was a crude old
General, and J. Baylis made a deplorable
Gushington. William Pope, as the office
boy who shakes a £5 note from the till and
spends the ill-gotten wealth in frivolous dis-
sipation with Susie (Miss Connolly), may
be said to have shared the bun with Ottey
aforesaid. But it was a very stale bun,
anyhow. The M.C.O.C. has hired the Bijou
for a whole fortnight, and will presumably
try a change of bill before the time is up.

The brightest of sundry new turns at Mel-
bourne Opera House is the athletic acro-
batic display of the two Bellclairs, who
stand for patterns of manly grace and do
fine feats of strength in a most picturesque
way. Tiebor, with his educated seals and
sea lions, goes one better than a previous
captain with a similar lot of marine flappers,
but the intelligence and balancing instinct
of these shiny and somewhat damp amphi-
bians has lost the charm of novelty.
Prince, the concertina expert, is back for a
few nights, the musical Elizabeth Clair is a
new-comer, and the cycling Jacksons, the'
O’Hana-San varieties, the De Bruns, George
Whitehead, Dale and Brookes are happening
as before.
David Bispham, the rich-voiced baritone
who is something more than a singer, will
come to pass in Melbourne at the Audi-
torium on Friday and Saturday this week,
and some nights in the week to come. His
reputation has come ahead of him by way
of Sydney, and the city is looking for David
to live up to it. At St. George’s Hall, across
the road, the Dandies finish their six weeks
session on Saturday night.
The return of the Bert Bailey Co. to Mel-
bourne King’s, last Saturday, supplies the
lately-felt want of Australians for Australia
in local bellowdrama. Albert Edmunds,
who is Bailey-cwm-Duggan in collusion, calls
the present outbreak a “novelty drama,
under the heading of “The Native Born,
and the matter answers to its description.
Bailey personally, in the opportune charac-
ter of a genial magician, hypnotist and won-
der-worker, supplies the novelties, whilst the
other people put in the melodrama. A sinful
land agent, abetted by two minions, has
grabbed all the water frontage in the Kos-
ciusko district, and covets also the selec-
tion where the hero abides with his serious

Aggie.
dad and volatile young
sister. The discovery of
gold on the coveted pro-
perty intensifies the vil-
lain’s anxiety to buy it,
and the desire of the vil-
lain’s gaudy sister to
steal the hero from an-
other girl serves to
thicken the plot. There-

to thwart the schemes of
the evildoers by means of
De Biereish devices and
pantomime effects. He
constructs an unaccount-
able temple of mystery
on the Kosciusko selec-
tion —a blackened stage
framed with electric
lights—and with a series
of apparitional tricks bor-
rowed from a William-
sonian panto he extracts
from the villain’s trem-
bling minion the name of
the person who committed
the murder, in much the
same way that Tweedle-
punch and Grace Palotta
induce the unlawful
owner of the island of
Florodora to confess his
fraud. Also, Bailey is apt
to pop up from nowhere
in a magicianly manner, to prevent the hero’s
arrest. If the villain is about to seize an
opportunity, or _ take a mean advantage,
Bailey converts it into an inkstand or a hat-
rack by a mystic movement of his hands.
A tablecloth, flung over the hero in the hour
of need, changes the concealed object into
Bailey himself, and where the baffled trans-
gressor is looking for his prey, he finds only
a bouquet of flags or a birdcage, maybe.
The only sort of trick that the resourceful
Bailey has overlooked is the liquor trick.
If he were to disguise himself as a book-
case, whilst the land agent was tempting
Ned Duggan to drink a bottle of chloroform,
he might bring down the house by changing
the fatal draught into a pint of beer.
Fun and fury chase one another with
great rapidity through the Albert Edmunds
miracle play, and there are but few dry
moments in its career. The dialogue is
good Australian, but, generally sneaking, the
jokes and the stage management are more
forcible than the acting. George Kensing-
ton, as the murderous minion, is hardly
more underdone (for bellowdrama purposes)
than Treloar, as the offender-in-chief. J.
P. Lennon, as the scared confessor, expresses
his genuine histrionic feelings rather too
quietly. The heroine (Miss Oakey) is a
colorlessly tepid actress, with an unmusical
voice, and Miss Bevan, as a conventional
old maid, is amateurish. On the other hand,
Guy Hastings is a pleasing hero. Duggan
does well enough as his Dad. Fred Mac-
donald and Harford are satisfactory in minor
comedy. Miss Roberts, as the young sister,
is a “hit of orlriaht,” and the distinctive
personality of Bailey is the chief bulwark
of the show. Dramatically, “The Native
Born” does very well, and scenic artists
Vaughan and Robins have done good work
for it.
* * * *
The sad and rather gruesome happenings
of “The Beggar Girl’s Wedding” appealed
to a moderate congregation at Melb. Prin-
cess’s last Saturday night. Bess Webster
is the heroine, and Bess’s early emergence
from poverty, on the arm of a strangely-
acquired husband, is attended by all the
mischief that a cheap and nasty villain can
bring to pass. He performs all the old
stunts, from chloroform-doping to theft and
homicide, their one object being to ruin Bess
and annoy her lawful husband. His wicked-
ness is aided and abetted by the husband’s
stupidity. It goes almost without saying
that the noble fathead’s name is Jack. Most
people apparently regard Jack as a medium
wherein George Cross may show off, in a
series of stained-glass attitudes illustrating
the emotions of a purse-proud hero. And
when this paper considers the matter, all
the other characters seem superfluous to a
tale which George so adorns with his beau-
teous posturings. George, after all, is the
entertainment. We have George getting tight
like a perfect gentleman; George distribut-
ing largesse to a tattered mob ; George huri-
ing the villain down eight flights of stairs ;
George perishing bravely in an iron-barred
cage; George spouting beatifically in his
high-heeled boots —in fact, George in all his
lovely incredibility. Beside such achieve-
ments, it should be sufficient reference to
the other members of the cast to state simply
and formally that they also spoke.

A five nights’ revival of “Miss Hook of
Holland” at Her Majesty’s (Melbourne), on
Saturday, is paving the way for Gen€e and
the Russian ballet. They will transpire with
unparalleled brilliance on the 21st. “Miss
Hook” is a pretty entertainment, and on
Saturday it had its quaint Dutch characters
demonstrated by a practically new cast.
Leslie Holland is a subdued and soft-spoken
Mr. Hook, trying to adapt his
strut to the waddle of the fat old distiller.
Frank Greene unconvincingly tenants the
uniform of Bandmaster Van Vuyt, and
Cannot, as Slinks in anew sham nose, is an
intermittent sound of revelry by night.
Bantock, in his element as the drowsy Van
Eck, is a picture of perfect rest, while Hig-
ginson resurrects his giggle to decorate the
amorous Captain Paap. Miss Olive God-
win’s tunefulness and cheerful comportment
as Sally Hook protect her from the wind
of inclement criticism, and Miss Jessie
Lonnen easily conceals her angularity in
Mina’s numerous “petties.” The show,
whereof the male chorus again is admirable,
occurs for the last time to-night (Thurs-
day). On Saturday Gen6e and the other
incomparables will happen as aforemen-
tioned.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Gimlet”: The tricks of melodrama are amusing.
Superior melodrama, I mean. Plain bellodrama has
no tricks. It merely resorts to audacities. But a
play like “Within the Law” is a bag of smart tricks,
and one gets good entertainment in watching them.
Observe, then, that the first object of the play is to
get the audience’s full sympathy for a girl who,
after her renaissance, lives luxuriously by wrong-
doing in an atmosphere of unmitigated criminality ;
who is supposed to nurse a revenge as good women
nurse a baby; and who entraps a feather-headed
fool of a boy into marriage by means so unaccount-
able that the drama never attempts to explain them.
How is sympathy to be won for a young woman like
her ? In the first place, she is presented as an inno-
cent, hard-working shop-girl, convicted on circum-
stantial evidence of another girl’s theft. After her
conviction, whilst being dragged to prison by a
policeman who has handcuffed her to his own wrist
because she tried to “make her away” (as though a
girl whilst travelling from courthouse to prison could
be seriously considered as a possible fugitive !), she
is brought to the office of her employer in order
that she may, whilst protesting her innocence, rail
at him and his system of sweating his employees.
He, seemingly, is a dull-brained millionaire who
honestly believes the girl to be a thief. The police-
man tells him that all such thieves assert their
innocence. The girl knows that he honestly dis-
believes her protests, yet she goes away—dragged
by the policeman—vowing to be revenged on the
boss who “ordered” her conviction, but was not per-
sonally responsible for her arrest. Her promise to
make the boss suffer for every hour of her imprison-
ment provides the climax of the first act. Before
the end of the second act she has married his fool
of a son, and then she makes the “situation” by
taunting the old boss with the success of her
policy of vengeance. “You gave me a number”
(prison number), she says. “Now I have taken
your name.” But, in the meantime, the playwright
has considered the fact that a girl, innocent when
she went to gaol, who had thought of nothing but
vengeance for three years, and then went right away
to join a community of thieves and blackmailers,
would be in danger of losing the audience’s sym-
pathy. So the playwright takes precautions. No
admirable character of any kind is permitted in the
story. The policemen are brutal dogs, the boss of
the emporium is a soulless ass, his son is a brainless
waster, his lawyer tries to out-crook the crooks.
Still the fact remains that a girl who walked out
of prison without a thought of ever being honest
any more would seem hardly a lovable heroine.
What does the dramatist do ? He informs the audi-
ence, per medium of Joe the Forger, that Mary did
want to go straight when she emerged from quod.
That she sought honest employment, but was
“narked” by the police every time (more cause for
sympathy!), and that in her despair of getting a
poor but honest billet, she sought a watery grave,
whence Joe the Forger rescued her. Then Joe nursed
her back to health, and led her gently into criminal
society. That’s how she came to be running the
blackmail boarding-house where the audience find her
in the second act, and thus she preserves their sym-
pathy. But—there are a lot more “buts” in the
later acts of the drama —if Mary’s ambition on leav-
ing prison was to return to the wretched, hopeless
shop-girl life she had been dragged from three years
previously, how about the vengeance she had promised
Poppa Gilder? How was she going to make him
suffer for every hour of her imprisonment, with 500
p.c. interest? As a humble toiler, known to the
police, she couldn’t have inflicted much slow ven-
geance on a millionaire. And how was she going to
hurt the old gentleman by committing suicide? She
never would have met Gilder, junior, in her fine rai-
ment, never could have told him fairy tales about
her luxurious domicile; never could have introduced
her “crook” friends to him as evidence of her un-
blemished respectability, if Forger Joe had not hap-
pened to see her when she was trying to drown her-
self. Had she committed suicide, as she intended,
her impassioned threats against old man Gilder, in
the first act, would have resolved themselves into
the merest skite. The tricks of melodrama are more
than amusing. They are interesting.
“S.T.S.”: As the Rheads and Sibleys of “Mile-
stones” seem now like people that we have known
all our lives, I rise to ask why didn’t Gertrude
marry Sam? The double engagement was broken off
on December 29, 1860, but within two years the
other pair married, with Sam’s approval. Gertrude
always cherished a tender feeling for Sam. He
“led a miserable life as a bachelor” till about 1882,
when he married his typewriter girl. I have no
grumble against this most charming of stories, but
a line would have been welcome to say why the two
failed to be united somewhere about a.d. 1864.
Further, why the arbitrary limitation of families
throughout the piece? There are five households
altogether. In three of them, one boy and one girl
are found ; in the other two, there is an only child.
From royalty to the crossing-sweeper, things were
not thus in the Victorian era.
“Props”: Bailey and Duggan’s play of “The
Native Born” was first called “The Trump Card,”
but they found that the Australian George Darrell
had a play of that name and had acted it. George’s
dramas included “The Four Fates,” “Back from
the Grave,” “The Sunny South,” “Hue and Cry,” and
several others. He got a show with “Sunny South”
in London, appearing himself as Mat Morley, but,
unluckily, an actor wounded him with a bowie knife
in cutting him loose from a tree. This spoilt the
run. A similar accident happened to Charles
Warner in “Michael Strogoff,” but he was replaced
by H. J. Byron. Nobody could replace the volatile
Darrell, one of the most engaging personalities of
the stage that I have met.
“Fontaine”: Died at St. Vincent’s Hospital, Mel-
bourne, on 11th inst. Andrew Howard, one of the
well-known Howard Bros., “telepathists,” who
caused a mild sensation when they first came to
Australia 10 years ago under engagement to Wirth
Bros. They remained' with Wirths for a consider-
able time, and afterwards had long bookings with
Rickards and Brennan. Another Howard, a nephew
of the brothers, will probably fill the vacancy, and
so keep the mysterious secret, gift or whatever it
is in the family.
It was of Rosina Buckmann, Maoriland’s sweet
soprano, that Melba said last year, “....One who
will surety gather the laurel wreath for herself, for
she has tenacity of purpose, courage, capability and
withal, an exceptional voice; I predict a great
future for her....” Rosina will get her chance in
1914. The influence of Melba and John McCor-
mack, plus her own talents, have secured her for
“Parsifal” at Covent Garden, in January, and for
the grand-opera season.

(accounts unaudited), Sara Bernhardt tells an inter-
viewer : “My ambition now is to be a great-grand-
mother.” Well, why shouldn’t she be one? The
Bulletin offers no objection.

To relieve the tedium of the long-drawn-
out counting of Senate and Referendum
votes, Melbourne arranged for itself a sen-
sation last week. This was an alleged con-
spiracy to murder a young married woman
named Florence King, wife of a railway
parcels clerk. Mrs. King’s narrative was
that an unknown young woman called on
her on June 7 with a parcel of clothing
alleged to have come from King’s sister at
Echuca. She invited the stranger inside and
offered her refreshment, pouring out also a
cup of tea for herself. She then left the
room, she said, for a few minutes, and on
her return noticed a whitish powder floating
on the tea and some spilt around the cup.
The liquid tasting bitter she left it, and sub-
sequently got it analysed. It proved to
contain enough strychnine to poison 10 per-
sons ! The police, being informed, inquired
as to the why and the who, and were told
that a young woman, allegedly “mashed” on
King, had pestered bim at his parcels office
window about a year ago, and, being re-
buffed, had gone out to Ascot Vale, where
she and Mrs. King had had a lively meet-
ing. The Law discovered this young woman
and her mate, the latter corresponding to
the description given of the woman who had
come with the parcel. They were arrested ;
but on presentment at court both were in a
state of hysterical collapse, and too broken
up to say a sensible word on the subject.
Last week a gentleman named Speed put
on a spurt in a Sydney drinkery, and just
about beat the record. He requested a perfect
stranger to clean his boots, and when he,
the now imperfect stranger, declined to
either clean Speed’s boots or drink his liquor
Speed tore out his pocket artillery and
started to make a crash. One of the bullets
cut the back of the neck of the gentleman
who would neither drink nor clean boots.
Several others smashed up the bar. The
last ended Speed’s swift career towards gaol.
Which suggests that there was a streak of
wisdom at the back of the shooter’s madness.
The streak increased in width at the inquest,
when it was found that deceased’s sole assets
were a cheap gun and a steamer ticket. He
had £l3O when he arrived at the hotel, and
gave it into the care of the landlord. He had
taken it all back in dribs and drabs, the last
trickle being £5 the night before the bom-
bardment took place. Presumably Speed felt
that when his money had gone his name
would change to Snail, or something of that
sort, and he preferred a wild and riotous
exit. Perhaps he was right!

PENFOLD'S

Man’s inhumanity to woman was power-
fully stressed in a Sydney police court last
week —also the utter brainlessness and lack
of a sense of proportion that characterise a
section of the local police. A 22-year-old
girl was charged with the criminal offence
of having abandoned her child so that its
life was endangered. What she actually did
was to take the child to the Waitara Found-
ling Home and ask the nuns if they would
look after it, as she was financially broke
and couldn’t. The nuns agreed, provided
she would spend the customary two weeks
with the ■ new arrival in the isolation ward.
There were reasons why she couldn’t. She
went away, and a while later the baby was
brought to the Home by someone else, with
a note from the mother imploring the nuns
to look after it. The infant was well-
clothed, healthy, and accompanied by a
bottle loaded with its customary fodder or
sustenance. The nuns took it in and pro-
pose to keep it. Now consider the unhappy
mother’s position. She was married at 17
and deserted at IS. After battling along for
some years she permitted herself to be sup-
ported by a n an who is at present in gaol
for assaulting her. She has a brother-in-law
AN OLD PRO-
VERB FALSIFIED.
"I told Jack that you refused to allow our
marriage, father."
" Well, what did he say?”
“He said he could wait.”
"Why, child, you will never marry a waiter."
who is willing to give her a home, but he
won’t stand the infant because it doesn't
belong to the man who deserted her. Hence
her visit to Waitara. Here is a girl who
has met with the most villainous ill-treat-
ment and misfortune. She can’t live what
is called honestly and keep her child. In a
frantic effort to obey the conventions and
get right with society, to a certain extent,
she allows herself to be separated
from the infant; but she takes all sorts of
care that it shall be well looked after. And
her only reward is that a fool-body of police
abandon the hopeless chase after the genuine
malefactors whom they so rarely overtake,
and try to get her gaoled. Of course the
magistrate dismissed the case. The ends of
justice would have been served even better
had he got up a testimonial for her in recog-
nition of her pluck, while leaping upon those
who were responsible for her arrest with
the heaviest boots in the wardrobe.
Melbourne’s City Coroner Cole —the
occasional husher-up of unquiet evidence —
was engaged last week in sitting on the
adjourned case of a married woman who
somehow descended from a third floor win-
dow at Oxford Chambers into the yard
below and was dying when the night watch-
man found her. The husband of deceased
suggests that she must have walked out of
the window in her sleep, being addicted to
somnambulism. The coroner decided to
throw the onus of decision upon a jury, and
committed the husband for trial. It.
appears there was another girl whose at-
tentions to the deceased’s husband had caused
a lot of unpleasantness all round. The at-
tentions were reciprocated, and the wife was
sadly unphilosophical. When her husband
asked her to “be sensible and let him see
the other girl three or four times a week.”
she declined to adopt that method of “re-
maining good friends.” She nursed her un-
happiness until it presumably grew too
strong for her and got her down into the
yard. Jealousy, as a rule, is pathetic when
it is artless, and. alas ! it is not often crafty.
The crafty little wife beats the other girl
at the finish every time. She runs a waiting
race, keeps a good position next to the
rails, and finally’ romps home with a lot in
hand. But the artless, jealous wife geis
pumped out in trying to catch her rival
when she is going strongest. Because she
can’t bear to think about the other girl she
is always thinking about her in the most
painful manner possible until the agony’ be-
comes unbearable. Then she walks out of
the third-floor window in her troubled sleep
or jumps out by way of protest. These re-
flections apply also to artless, hopeless,
jealous husbands. As for crafty, hopeful,
jealous husbands —well, there are none.
The S.A. Supreme Court has taken the
step, very unusual in that State, of striking
a lawyer off the rolls. It was what the
Chief Justice called a “dark and sordid
story.” Among other misdeeds gent, one
had deposited a lady T client’s shares as
security’ for his own overdraft; bad got
money for a loan that was not arranged,
and for shares that were not bought; had
misrepresented by £2OO the price of a house
be had bought for a client; and had been
guilty of plain, unfrilled misappropriation
of money’s received. After eight days before
one Judge, and three before the Full Court,
the latt°r institution ordered the name of
Judah Moss Solomon, senior, to be struck
off the roll. The last straw was a declara-
tion. “in justice to my’self.” by 7 Paris Xesbit.
K.C.. that, though he had (without fee)
conducted the defence, so that this member
of the profpssion should have everv fair
chance, yet it must not be supposed that his
own “grief and horror” at the disclosures
were exceeded by those of any one in the
community.
“Vic” : The Methodist Church in Victoria
advertises a fine property for sale at a sac-
rifice. This is the estate of Mintaro, at
Lancefield, 32 miles from Melbourne. It was
bought a number of years ago at the in-
stance of an enthusiastic man. Pastor Bick-
ford. who was very much interested in Girl
Reformation. To that purpose it was ap-
plied on a generous scale. The mansion is
one of the most luxurious in Australia, hav-
ing be<m built and fitted, reckless of ex-
pense, by a mysterious tar, Captain Gardner,
said to have been a whaler, or nrawner. or
sometoins’. Tho house is alleged to have
cost £26.000. It is an imitation of Mel-
bourne Government House, which in its turn
w *>s Wardell’s imitation of Osborne, in the
Isle of Wight. When Bickford died the re-
formatory business was carried on first by
Pastor Adamson, and since by Pastor
GOlocott. Fortunately, the supply of
reformatory girls has run out; factory de-
mand seems to have cut off the supnly. So
the Methodies lead their white elephant to
market.
Joseph Boyd, of North Melbourne, wan-
dered into the Victorian Divorce Court last
week and gut what be wanted. In 1905 he
married Evelyn. In 1909 she went north to
visit her mother, and after a reasonable in-
terval she left or proposed to leave for home
on the s.s. Bombala. Apparently there was
never any authentic record of her on the
Bombala, but she might be the casual, un-
booked passenger who flees on board at the
last moment, proposing to arrange for a
berth with the purser or chief steward, and
falls overboard before she has time to inter-
view anv responsible officer. Or perhaps the
fourth fireman recognised her as the girl
who had jilted him in early youth, and be-
coming a raving maniac on the spot, be-
auiiPd her downstairs to see the pretty coal-
bunkers. and then shoved her head first into
the furnace, and after that laughed a hor-
rible laugh and went away and joined the
Unitarian persuasion. Anyhow home never
saw her any more. Joseph, parents, and
have alike failed to find any trace
of her. Evelyn has become simply a name,
a mcmorv. She was divorced on the ground
of desertion, but. after all. it is ouite pos-
sible that she merely eloned with the Angel
of Death and fled to Asphodel.

“Kodak” does a think: —
The suffragette cause can’t possess many human
catapults like Elizabeth Davison, who projected her-
self at the King's horse, and caused that shocked
Derby animal to somersault and drag his jockey by
the stirrup. The fanatical ■ worm-thrower and the
rustling tea-room incendiary only need to be chased
and firmly grasped; but the rare and bewildering
horse-charger, or female unmounted dervish, has too
much dash to be suppressed by- ordinary police brawn.

don, until the word was flashed along, and they
could be headed down a broad thoroughfare to a
barricade of improvised bargain counters, manned
by bowing shopwalkers of the most elegant variety,
and piled high with insanely cheap treasures. Just
imagine the disorder it would create even in a steel-
willed suffragette’s mind, when she was keyed up to
shrieking pitch, to be courteously offered a real 20-
guinea Paris hat for fid. ! Why all the fuss, and
riot, and panic would be over and done with by the
time she had wrenched her old tile straight, told
the shopman that he was a contemptible wretch, and
warned him on his life not to deliver the purchase
at the wrong address.

The reverse of “Chinaman’s luck” attaches
to the pistol with which young man Chia
thought of murdering the Chinese Consul in
Melbourne some weeks ago. Chia’s connec-
tion with the weapon cost him his liberty.
Constable Calwell, who
arrested him at the
consulate, picked up
the revolver and ap-
parently kept it. He
soon got busy with his
new toy. When he
saw a man running
away from a suspici-
ous-looking melee in a
Melbourne street he
fired at the fugitive,
and missed him. The
incident was officially
ignored. Next time Cal-
well discharged his ill-
fated firearm it went
off in a struggle at
the Police Barracks,
where the awful young
man had been threat-
ening to “blow out the
brains” of a brother
John. The career of
the next person who
carries Chia’s auto-
matic shooter will be
watched with interest.
The Chinaman who
purchased it at a shop
is doing three years
for firing it at another
Chinaman, and the
Hop who took the
darned thing into cus-
tody has been dis-
missed from the force
and otherwise dealt
with for having
pointed it at another Hop.
Adelaide is the one city in Australia
where you don’t rub your eyes when you
drop on such a notice as this at your break-
fast :
The Friends of the late Mr. ETCETERA are in-
formed that his REMAINS will be Cremated at Hest
Terrace Crematorium on SATURDAY, arriving at
11 a.m. DITTO & SON, Undertakers.
So Adelaide leads Australia in another re-
form. And in another 20 years, being by
that time still more sensible, it will have
left off inviting its friends to drop in and
enjoy a morning of damp wretchedness at
the cemetery. It will perhaps still announce
briefly that it is dead, but its widow and
orphans will buy bread and butter and an
extra winter blanket with the couple of
pounds it now wastes on a procession of
funeral advertisements.
DIRGE OR WAIL (JUNE 11, 1913).
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Weeping skies and a wild, cold wind,
And my best umbrella gone ;
The week's work hopelessly behind,
And my brain like a sodden scone.
Fresh from a dripping, draught-swept tram,
With pains in my back and head,
I shut my office door with a slam,
And envy the peaceful dead.
Hundreds of cares and aches and ills
Have harassed me all to-day,
And now my cup of anguish fills —
I’ve the income tax to pay !

THE TURN OF THE TIDE.
Marriage and birth statistics of Victoria for 1912 were most satisfactory. —Daily payer.
Momba is in the market, and anybody
who wants to throw out his chest and say
he owns the biggest station in N. S. Wales
can have that joy—if he has enough money
to buy it. There is a mere trifle of 1,600,000
acres left now. There was a time when the
holding ran to 2,000,000 acres; but selectors
have had their cut, and when the Western
selector cuts, 10,240 acres drop off, as a
rule. Still, with the selections cut out and
the White Cliffs opal fields shorn off, there
is enough left in Momba to make another
Devonshire, or three Surreys and a half; or
you could drop the whole of the province of
Otago (Maoriland) into it; and Denmark
could be accommodated inside and space
enough left for two-thirds of Switzerland.
If anybody doesn’t want to feel cramped,
and has a quarter of a million or so lying
idle, there isn’t any place in N. S. Wales
The Bulletin would sooner recommend
than Momba. And when one has bought it
he can amble into Wilcannia on one of the
station camels and get lashings of excite-
ment by seeing the coaches lurch in from
Cobar and Broken Hill twice a week.
A pretty legal knot in maritime
law is to be untied in Fiji over
the American schooner Lyman D.
Foster. The L.D.F. was bound
for Australia from the Pacific
Slope with 700,000 ft. of timber
when it butted a hurricane and
went ashore in the Lau Archi-
pelago, near Fiji. The crew
escaped in the boats, and reaching
Suva, reported a loss of vessel
and cargo. A few days later, a
small coasting boat sighted the
wreck, plucked it off the reef and
towed it safely into Suva, under
the British flag. When the
American skipper saw with
amazement his vessel safe in port
he was slightly aggravated, but
when he spotted the Union Jack
where the Stars and Stripes used
to be he felt like a mad Injun
feels between drinks. To save
him from apoplexy the Customs
authorities pulled down the
British flag, but olaced the vessel
under arrest pending a legal deci-
sion on the question of salvage.
The skipper of the schooner ex-
plains that he left his tub on the
reef while he went to Suva for
shelter and offers of towage; so the
Admiralty Court has some choice yarn to
unravel and may expect some tall swearing.
The reformed Young Turk movement
leads to much unsuccessful war and much
successful assassination. Two grand viziers,
reckoned to be the two best soldiers that the
falling Unspeakable possessed, have gone in
quick succession. Mahmud Shevket, who
was supposed to rank very high in a mili-
tary sense, even according to European
standards, apparently looked more like a
tunnel than a field-marshal when the ass-
assins had done their work. He was a reck-
less, casual man in a fez, and when he saw
half the road in front of him torn up for
repairs, and the rest of it blocked by another
motor-car, he didn’t turn his petrol vehicle
back and flee for his life, but went straight
ahead. Then a number of persons (accord-
ing to one picturesque account they con-
sisted of sundry gambling-house keepers
and a lame publican) put their heads
into Shevket’s car and filled him with shot.
The gambling-house keepers leaped for their
vehicle and fled, but the lame publican
didn’t leap with sufficient velocity, and was
left. lie was captured while trying to con-
ceal his “gun” down a drain, which seems a
foolish business, considering the scarcitv of
drains in Constantinople. There was at
least this much to be said for the late
lamented Shevket. He wasn’t a Turk, but
an Arab from Bagdad way, and the Arab is
sometimes threatened with being a gentle-
man—which is the sort of thing that seldom
happens to the Turk. The fact that one of
Shevket’s assassins was a lame publican
may possibly be quoted as an evidence of
the horrors of drink, but this is a matter
upon which it is difficult to offer an opinion.
One of the strangest features of all the
current Turkish rows is the complete efface-
ment of the miserable old Sultan, who was
dragged from the gilded prison he had occu-
pied for 30 years and ordered to adorn a
throne. Apparently nobody takes any
account of poor Mohammed V. The dogs
scramble over the bones of his inheritance,
and he says nothing. Wars come and go,
and _ provinces drop away, and armies
mutiny, and Grand Viziers are murdered,
and Mohammed, drivelling peacefully in his
’ oth year, doesn’t put up the semblance of a
kick. Apparently the Unspeakable is near-
ing his dead finish.
Those who hold Liberal views about the
mingling of the races should carefully study
the sombre story’ of Annie Sophia Gordon,
sentenced in Sydney last week to three years’
imprisonment for manslaughter. Sophia,
when sentenced, was 36 years of age, and
she had borne 11 children. She had wrecked
her life at the beginning by marrying a man
of color. He knocked her about so that she
left him and went to live with his brother.
He, too, knocked her about, and possibly by
this time she had got down so low that she
deserved it. They were described as living
a cat and dog life. The negro proposed to
end it by leaving her and taking his furni-
ture to his sister’s place. He and the sister
commenced to dismantle the bed, and the
woman who was to be left in the stripped
house picked up a razor that happened to be
lying handy and slashed. The negro stag-
gered outside on to the grass and quietly
bled to death. Prompt aid would have saved
him, but it is doubtful if his passing is a mat-
ter for public regret. Sophia’s career, after
becoming the slave and chattel of two black
brothers, seems to have been a patchwork of
wallopings, drunks and baby-bearings, with
a background of general Sheol all the time.
From this paper’s Military Historian :
As far as I can trace in the records, Sydney has
been bombarded only once. The Are was from a
ship called the Three B.’s—so named from the initial
letters of the three owners’ names. The vessel caught
fire while moored in Sydney Harbor, and when it
was known that there was a loaded cannon on deck
there was a great scare. Governor Macquarie took
charge, and ordered various people; —among them
Dean Cowper, then a child—to flee into the bush
for safety. The charge did eventually explode, the
ball fell harmlessly in Br dge-street, and the future
Dean crawled out from his hiding place.
In the Perth City Council last week two
votes were cast for this motion:—
That the time has come to cease the practice of
supplying spirituous liquors ad lib. to councillor*
unless at their own expense, and that the money
thus saved, estimated at £4OO a year, be expended
on urgent works.
The time seems nearly ripe for dividing
drink-per-head statistics into two sections:
(1) Aldermen; (2) All others.

BALLADE OF THE PETTICOAT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Councillor Crespin said that he would not be sur-
pnsed in a few years to see girls walking down the
street in tights. It was unfortunate that the good
old petticoat had been done away with.— Daily
paper.
Encased in her narrow, silken sheath,
Gripping her tightly around the hips,
Nothing to hamper her underneath,
On high-heeled “patents” the Block belle trips.
Fine is the shape that is thus revealed,
Great is my joy as each curve I note;
Little there is from the view concealed —-
Oh ! for the glimpse of a petticoat!
We still get a sight of silken hose,
Neat are the ankles displayed to view;
Oh ! but its odor has left tfie rose,
This is a matter I needs must rue.
Where are those furbelows, fresh and sweet,
Whence Saracenical perfumes float,
Filling with fragrance the dusty street?
Oh, for the scent of a petticoat!
Gone all the delicate lingerie,
Silenced now its voluptuous speech,
Like the lap of the amorous sea
Kissing its lover, the brown-limbed beach.
Vanished the billows of silk and lace,
Whose rustling was as a siren’s note,
Subtle and sweet in its limpid grace—
Oh, for the swish of a petticoat.
L’Envoi.
Oh, for the lure of the lurking lace !
Oh, for its perfumes that cling and float!
Oh, for its swish in the crowded place!
Oh, for jthe thrill of a petticoat!
victoria.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
Weather Item from “ J.R.” : The Federal
Government has induced Professor Allen, of Mel-
bourne, to report on health conditions at Panama
for the External Affairs Department. The idea is
that a line may thus be got as to what to do in the
Northern Territory. One of the reasons why any
such information is more or less useless lies in the
fact that the climates of Panama and the N.T. are
quite different. F’rinstance, the mean temperature
of Northern Australia —summer, winter and annual —-
is lower than that of Panama. Also, temperatures
generally in the Southern Hemisphere are several
degrees lower, latitude for latitude, than in the
Northern. It was not necessary to go into elaborate
statistics to prove the deadliness of the Panama cli-
mate. Amiable innocents with very little experience
of the realities of life, and no outlook outside their
own narrow circle, like most of the Federal Mini-
sters, have let themselves be bluffed into believing
that all the places between the two imaginary circles
drawn on the surface of the terrestrial globe, and
called the Tropics, are exactly alike. Some of them
have made the voyage to Europe and back by differ-
ent routes, but they, seemingly, failed to notice that
there was any vast difference between the heat in
the Red Sea and that in the Pacific (say) about
Hawaii or Fiji.
“Snell” : His name was Danks. When I
first met him he had just entered into possession of
298 acres of so-called arable land at Back Creek.
The land of Mr. Danks was heavily encumbered with
rocks like bogged elephants, and monstrous gums
that stood over him in defiance, shaking curses from
their twisted limbs on his puny head. Danks was
sft. 4in., but he had a chest like an old colonial
oven, and a eye like a bit of bright steel.
He had also one axe much the worse for wear, one
hammer, one billy, two chops, and not a friend in
the world. He was grilling the chops on a bit of
wire. He offered one of them to me. He had made
a home for himself by splitting 20 slabs, and leaning
them against a tree-butt. This held his bed. He
had to sleep in a semi-circle, but Danks didn’t mind
that. I saw him again three years later. He now
had a four-roomed slab house neatly roofed with
shingles, a clearing planted with apple trees, and a
flourishing vegetable garden. He had two goats and
a cow, and a small sawmill, worked with a water-
wheel of his own contriving. He had about 50
acres rung and fenced, a flock of turkeys, and a
thriving army of hens. Danks had also a wife and
three children, two of them over five years of age.
I was not curious about the other things, but the
two elder children certainly excited some concern.
Later, Danks explained. A year ago, when married
and living in a bark humpy about as commodious as
a furniture van, he had adopted the two children of
Dan Hewitt, because Dan Hewitt was dead and
unable to provide for them. “Poor little beggars,”
Danks said, and confided to me that, as for himself,
he had never known what trouble was.

“McNortli”: Touching on this black
tracker question. “Surveyor” (B. 3/4/’l3) in-
stances a case wherein Binghi did some “wonder-
ful” work — i.e.j followed a trail through scrub, a
maize patch, grass paddock, etc. There are any
amount of white men, stockmen, prospectors and the
like, in the North, who would have no difficulty in
doing that. A man can’t travel through scrub with-
DESCRIPTIVE.
YOUNG BILL : “Seen our cow about 'ere, Mister?"
M ISTER: “ What's your cow like?"
YOUNG BILL : “ He's all black, on'y he's got a white spot every now an’ agen."
out breaking twigs and branches, thereby leaving a
plain indication of his course. In grass his trail
would be there for all to see, whereas a track in a
maize patch would appear like a main road to a
bushman. Binghi’s abilities as a tracker—and there
are hundreds of him in his “raw” state in my
vicinity (N.Q.) —don't impress me worth a cent. He
very often goes near to losing himself in the bush
through inability to find his own tracks home again.
But he can put up a very good bluff, which accounts
for a great deal of the “wonderful” tracking. The
nigs “Surveyor” mentions as receiving £1 per week
and return fare to Cooktown on the expiration of
their engagement would, in many instances, soon
after their arrival home, be looking for a job on
some of the Cape York Peninsula cattle stations at
10s. a month, and considered dear at the price. My
impression of the present-day Binghi’s skill at track-
ing is that it is on a par with his skill at spear-
throwing, and that is, in round figures, nil.
Lecturing to Melb. University Science
Club, Dr. Harvey Sutton, of the Vic. Education
Department, asserted that, as a general rule,
persons with blue eyes loved, and, if pos-
sible, dwelt in the country, while those with brown
eyes mostly gravitated to the cities. He said an
examination of town schools would show a large
minority of blue-eyed scholars, while in country
schools he has found frequently but one in 20 and
even 30 brown-eyed. The deduction he makes is
that Australia should seek its immigrants, not from
the Cold Land cities, but largely from the heredi-
tarily blue-eyed races —Scandinavians, etc.—who, by
the way, are sea-lovers as well.
“0.K.” eloquently but on his own respon-
sibility :
Birth statistics for England and Wales show the
calamitous result of the diffusion of the Malthusian
doctrine in pamphlet form with which the late
Charles Bradlaugh and Mrs. Annie Besant, his dis-
ciple, thought to benefit struggling humanity. In
1873, or thereabouts, the Bradlaugh-Besant booklets
were scattered broadcast at a penny each. The pub-
lishers expected that the poverty-stricken millions
would buy these pen’orths of warning and instruc-
tion and immediately reduce, or stop, their output
of ill-fated infants. Within the 40 years since 1873
the birthrate in England and Wales has declined 40
per cent., and the loss of physique to the race as a
whole has been immeasurably greater. Bradlaugh
and the misguided lady dreamed of an increase in
the national health and vigor. The families of the
poorest and worst-fed classes were to be restricted,
whilst the progeny of the people in comfortable cir-
cumstances were to come along as numerously as
ever and help to improve the average physical and
intellectual standard of Britain’s sons and daughters.
What happened was the reverse of this expectation.
The hopeless, reckless legions of the unfit were
mostly indifferent to the Malthusian doctrine, and
the slums continued to be populated at the same old
rate. But the idea of restricting the number of
their offspring, as was done in France, appealed to
the understanding of the more “respectable” classes.
Where Malthus had published his views in vain, the
practical social reformers (as they thought them-
selves) with their instructive penny pamphlet, full
of matter borrowed from an American doctor, made
a lasting impression. The effect of their teaching
was to bring human fecundity into disfavor and
even ridicule, in the homes of young parents who
possessed the means to rear and educate healthy
children, whilst the “masses” for whom the danger-
ous teaching was intended, remained in ignorance or
received the gospel with derision. The very name
of the publication, which spread like wildfire through
England in the early 70’s of last century, is now
almost forgotten, but the perverted accomplishment
of its purpose is told to every old compatriot of
Annie Besant in the announcement that the English
birthrate has dropped to 23.8 per 1000. Mothers
of large families, nowadays, are either “titled
ladies,” who are expected to provide heirs to the
blessed dignity and estates, or careworn women
whose children are born to ill-nourishment and all
other ill-conditions of life. The sturdiest section of
the community grows proportionately smaller every
year. The plants have ceased to multiply, but weeds
are overrunning the ground. And amongst the
reasons suggested for this steady deterioration in the
average manhood of Britain, the publication of a
popular incentive to race-suicide, 40 years ago, is
never mentioned at all.

THE WINNING OF DOLLY WEST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was up the Gippsland district, at the “Saw
and Axe” Hotel,
That I heard the facts that go to make the present
tragic tale';
Charley Withers, the “commercial,” who knew all
the parties well,
Spun the yarn, while we discussed a glass of Michael
Hogan’s ale.
We were sitting in the parlor. Through the door-
way, in the bar
We could see a cove, whose face revealed the marks
of care and strife.
“It is awful,” murmured Charley, as he lit a bad
cigar,
“How a pretty woman’s able to mess up a fellow’s
life I
Ste that mournful-looking Johnny? He’s a victim,
poor old chap !
Tis the old yarn, slightly twisted ; pretty woman,
two fool men ;
She was Dolly West, the barmaid at the pub at
Fisher’s Gap,
And the blokes were Jimmy Webster and one Wil-
son, first name Ben.
“Dolly was a well-made damsel, with a wealth of
red-gold hair,
Which, I m free to own, looked splendid when it
caught the sunshine’s rays;
And her teeth were white and gleaming, while her
eyes were like a pair
Of blue lakes kissed by moonlight, and she had nice,
taking ways.
“Jim and Ben were timber-cutters, and, of course,
the poor chumps fell
Deep in love with pretty Dolly, like the lunatics
they were!
Girls were rather scarce at Fisher’s Gap, and this
one was a belle.
For a time she would not give a hint for which she
most did care.
r; : .
“Jim was very sliv and nervous—not a bit like slap-
s' up Ben —
,And he didn’t make much progress with his courting
of Miss West ;
Wheras Wilson had nice manners and the cheek, at
least, of ten,
And in time ’twas plain to ev’ryone the lady liked
him best.
“When he put the question to her Dolly readily
said ‘Aye.’
Bentio went about triumphant, shouting drinks for
all the boys !
Poor*'old Jimmy was downhearted, and expressed a
wish to die,'
lor a time we thought that he would lose his mental
equipoise!
“It was fixed that in December Ben and Dolly should
be- wed.
’Twas about a week beforehand that the forest fires
broke out.
Palls of smoke hung o’er the township, and by night
the sky was red,
And the settlers on the outskirts by the flames were
put to rout.
“The.‘Saw and Axe,’ though out a bit, seemed safe,
until the wind,
Of a sudden, changed to north, and then a mighty
sheet of flame
Raced Tike mad acj-qss the tree-tops, leaving blazing
trunks behind—
With a roar of rushing waters down \ipon the place
it came! ,
“In a trice it was enveloped! Was there anyone
: ii- inside ?
S jimmy Blair, the licensee ; his missus, Ada, and the
; rest
AJI were safe, as were a honeymooning Johnny and
his bride ;
But a look around the crowd revealed no sign of
Dolly West!
“Jim and Ben were on the scene, and towards the
pub they wildly raced,
But the latter stumbled backwards, scorched and
blinded; Jimmy, too,
Hesitated for an. instant, but he quickly turned and
faced
The spitting flames, with lowered head, and gamely
battled through !
“For hours, it seemed —a year—an age—the fire hid
him from sight!
Though ’twas really but a minute ere we overcame
our qualms—
When, all scorched and burned and smoke-grimed,
faint and reeling from the fight,
Jimmy staggered from that furnace, bearing Dolly
in his arms !
“Well, the wind dropped, and we beat the fire. When
Dolly was brought to,
Up she marched to Jim and kissed him, right in
front of poor old Ben,
Who was feeling mighty sore, and made a bit of a
to-do,
\\ hereupon the damsel turned ou him and ‘chucked’
him, there and then!
“Later on, she married Jim, and Benno vanished
from the Gap.
It seemed mighty hard on Wilson, for he had not
funked the fire,
But the smoke had knocked him silly ! He had tons
of pluck, poor chap,
But he’d rushed in where the blaze was worst, and
just had to retire!”
Charley stopped. I took a look at that sad figure
in the bar.
There he stood, upon his face the ruthless marks of
care and strife!
“\es, it’s rotten,” Charles commented, puffing hard
at his cigar,
“How a blanky fickle girl can spoil a blankv fellow’s
life!”
“V\ ell, the cove was badly treated,” said I, glancing
through the door ;
“Poor old Ben, he seems quite broken up. I really
pity him!”
“Hang it all, man; that's not Wilson!” broke out
Withers, with a roar,
“Ben is doing well in Queensland! That poor vic-
tim there is JIM l”

“ J.R.”: What rot these overseas com-
mentators on Australian affairs do talk, to be
sure! Thus Lovat Fraser, in a recent issue of the
Daily Mail (London). [N.B.— Fraser tore fever-
ishly through a small part of Australia three or
four years ago.] :
Australia, not Western America, will be the scene
of the next phase of the interminable conflict with
Asia. The entire population could, be comfortably
and prosperously established in the Northern Rivers
district of New South Wales.
The Northern Rivers district of ’N.S.W. is 150
miles long, by an average of 30 miles wide from
the coast to the crest of the dividing range, accord-
ing to Lyne’s Water Conservation Commission.
There are 45 millions of people in the Common-
wealth, and that would give more than 1060 persons
to each square mile of the generally rugged and
precipitous country in question. Foreign trippers
and so forth get a casual look from a motor-car
at the alluvial flats of the lower Clarence and
Richmond Rivers, and imagine they are a sample.
They should try the road from Grafton to Glen
Innes, Grafton to Armidale, or the track from
Kempsey to Armidale up Five Day Creek, and over
Guy Fawkes Mountain. I have ridden over all of
them, and I know\ There are others, too. There
is another road from Kempsey to Arm’dale —nearly
all side-cutting, and that from Port Macquarie to
Walcha, and the old bullock-dray road from Port
Stephens to Walcha, crossing the Barnard and Little
Manning Rivers 21 times in less_ than 20 miles,
through the Dark Entry, and up Hungry Hill —a
solid six miles’ climb, which the old bull-punchers
used to take two days over, double-banking the
whole way. There are patches of fertile soil here
and there, among the most desolate rocky mountains
imaginable, and in some of the most inaccessible gul-
lies there is alluvial gold to be had for the dig-
ging. But it does not begin to be the sort of
country that will support over 1000 human beings
to the square mile.
» * * ♦
Gouger: A fearless and capable navigator
is the coastal Binghi of Northern Australia, among
the countless reefs and rocks of the periodically
hurricane-swept seas. A log, burnt out with fire
and trimmed with a tomahawk, having a stout
sapling lashed five feet away from its side to act
as outrigger, to prevent capsizing, and about 20ft.
long, is the limit of Binghi’s Titanic. Half a dozen
men in one of these (a boat will carry 14 men, or
more if necessary) will paddle from the coast to
miles out of sight of land, visiting, in the bird
season, numerous islands, collecting the eggs that
lie in countless thousands on the beaches and among
the rocks. It is from these tribes that the boche-de-
mer boys are recruited, and better seamen and better
divers cannot be found the world over. The Bloom-
field River natives constantly replenish their larder by
turtle fishing, making for the open sea at night-
time. A couple of boys paddle the canoe, while two
others attend to the harpooning apparatus. The half
of the spear is of heavy wood, at one end of which
a hole has been burnt out. Into this is jammed an
iron spike, a foot long, the point very sharp, having
on it a single long barb. On the end that fits into
the spear-handle is a hole, through which is a long,
thin rope. When a turtle is sighted, the canoe is
paddled silently towards it. The native in the bows
takes sure aim, and launches the spear with terrific
force. The turtle immediately dives straight down,
pulling the spear head out of the loosely-fitting
handle, and leaving the two natives hanging on
grimly to the straining rope. Often a turtle grows
to a hundredweight, and more, and the fight that
such a shellback will put up on a moonlight night is
well worth a box seat to witness. The pace is so hot
that often the natives have to paddle strenuously in
the direction the maddened turtle is taking to pre-
vent their canoe being dragged to pieces. But the
turtle must rise to take in more air, and when the
canoe catches up to him, two of the boys jump into
the sea, turn him on his back, and lug him aboard.
That is h's dead finish.

“Catamain” : It stands to reason that the
tubercular diseases must increase in the cow herds
owing to over-cultivation of the brutes to make them
produce milk in excess of a natural standard of flow.
This cultivation has been continued until only two
or three of the best milking breeds remain in prac-
tical use, and even these have had all but the most
prolific juice-producers eliminated. The whole aim
is to generate excessive milkers, and as the more
milk a cow yields the weaker she is physically, (can t
say the effect on her mentally), the time is ripening
when there won’t be a bull’s wife able to stand up
without a prop under each arm, with the result that
the mighty serious hold that tuberculosis has already
got on the goddess through inbreeding and
malnutrition will be increased. Incidentally, it is
generally overlooked that there is more danger to
human beings in the use of butter than the use of
milk. Milk can be boiled to a germ-destroying
degree, but butter doesn’t take kindly to boiling or
even grilling.

“Ginger”: I have heard many and
scornful aspersions cast at a recent aboriginally
anent a dingo pack rushing a Queensland hatter's
kypsv. I, for one, have no doubt of the veracity of
the reported item. Last Sunday in a Hay (N.S.W.)
pub I heard the paragraph challenged again, backed
with the assertion that the dingo was the most cow-
ardly mong’ on four legs in Australia. He certainly
slopes away to his lair when his belly is full, but
when he is hungry anybody can have my portion of
him. A pack of the brutes took after the Coolgardie-
to-Red Hill mailman one trip. He dumped the mail-
bags and put up the scorch of his natural life till he
got to the HiH. I was camped on Freestone Creek,
a few miles out of Bringolong (Vic.), when a pack
came and picketed the hut. Fortunately, I had a
rifle. Incidentally, I got enough skins for a dingo
rug before the secretary of the Wild Dogs' Union
declared the strike off.

"S.S.S.” (from Papua) : Ivypa. the Kiwai
carrier, went kava-kava (mad) and sang at the
top of his voice for three weeks without stop-
ping. At the end of the second week he ran up
a cocoanut tree and hurled cannon-ball fruit at the
hospital orderlies who tried to entice him down.
For three days and nights he howled in the tree-tops.
The morning of the fourth day a police boy passed
up the hill track carrying a strange sort of snake
slung across a stick. Kypa saw it, slid down the
tree trunk, snatched the snake from the - astonished
and sedate old Samarai sergeant, and ate it raw.
Then he went on singing. I gave him everything
known to science that he would take to compose him,
and still he sang. Then I called Kukane, the chief hos-
pital orderly, into consultation. “Kukane,” I said,
“what fashion that, all the time sing; my head all
the same sore along noise.” Kukane saluted gravely.
“Sinabada,” he said, with dignified composure,
“more better I hit him along head.” Which meant
that, if I approved, he would slaughter him. I
called him off till all other remedies had been tried,
and one of the Ori-kiva boys, under a bond of
secrecy (sorcery being an indictable offence in Papua),
made a pourri-pourri. After that, there was a great
and abiding silence.
“Jack Moses”: I was yarning to the
trooper about the generosity of the Blokes of the
Outback. “This is how it is,” he explained.. “I had
a Cove in the lock-up here doing 48 hours, and had
introduced him to the wood-heap and an axe. He
was belting away when a couple of bushmen hap-
pened along, and asked me what the Cove was doing
it for. I explained. ‘What was the fine?’ asked one,
diving his fist into a pocket. ‘Ten bob,’ I told him.
‘All right,' he says, ‘here’s the dough. Drop the
sliprails.’ When I sooled the Cove out the Blokes
were waiting. ‘Whalcher,’ says one casually, ‘come
andhaveadrink.’ And, of course, the Cove did.

Pick: Though almost upon the roof of
Australia —being situated some 60 miles, as the
aeroplane flies, north-east of Kosciusko the
Kiandra country holds more dampness than any
other place I have visited above sea level. On the
tops of the highest peaks
springs eject their icy
waters with a force that
sometimes spurts the
stream into the air like
a miniature fountain,
and on the lower lands
are the shaking bogs.
These quagmires, some-
times acres in extent,
are covered with a grass-
ancoated crust, about a
foot thick. Standing
thereon, with a few
steps of the devil’s horn-
pipe, a man can bounce
a bird off its nest at the
other end of the patch.
If a pole, or crowbar, or
any similar old thing be
shoved through the
crust, it will slide away
towards the centre of the
planet. And if a down-
country horse fools after
the enticements of the
greenery he makes a
sudden exit from the
landscape, although the
wild horses of those
parts gallop across the
bogs and jump the
waves created by the
vibration without break-
ing through. I once
saw a flock of fool
sheep, their fool shep-
herd and his foolish ass
of a dog wander out on
to the surface of a
Kiandra bog. The thing
nearly tipped up on
to its edge when the
last sheep and the dog
straggled aboard, and
most of the flock slid
back to firm ground.
But 200 of them went
over the upper edge,
and fell down into the
Nirvana of slush, and
the place that once knew
them knew them no more.

A Melbourne bookseller recently got an
inquiry from the backbloeks for a Bible,
and inasmuch as the inquirer seemed to
suppose that it was a rare publication he
was in want of the bookseller wrote back
for more definite instructions. He thought
that his customer might be referring to some
rare edition, such as the Wicked Bible, or
the Breeches Bible, or the Treacle Bible, or
the Bishop’s Bible, or the Great Bible. How-
ever. the answer to his letter was definite
enough. The back-blocker wrote :
The old and new Testament, or Bible. I believe
God is blamed for writing it, but no one is quite
sure. The Labor Party’s law re signing articles was
not in force, unfortunately, when this particular
book was written. I hope this will be sufficient in-
formation for you.
The Odd-Jobber of Freetrade London.
Some extracts from the London Standard
about life in the world's great Freetrade
metropolis, where regular work is precarious
by reason of foreign imports, and odd-
jobbing is the order of the day :
It is the failure in life who has to fall back and
live on the earnings of the women, and when you get
down amongst the lowest strata of society the pro-
portions of failures amongst the men increases. Many
of the men never get a fair start. As boys they take
the first job that offers —usually one that leads to
nothing. Some of them are not strong enough to do
a day's work ; others have lost their character, but,
whatever be the cause of their failure, they are
sunk in hopeless surrender; all their pride has left
them, and there is not a spark of energy or spirit
remaining.
When the man is a failure the work of feeding the
family falls on the woman. There seems to be a
wider demand for female labor, but it is shockingly
paid. What they earn and what they keep a family
on is terribly small, but, such as it is, it is the
woman’s earning. There is factory work for them,
which sometimes keeps them employed till nine
o’clock at night, during which time their children
are crying in the streets because they cannot get into
their homes —the father possibly being employed dis-
cussing horse-racing outside the publichouse.
Home-work, making paper-bags, or washing up in
restaurants, are other sources of income for slum
mothers. They go on working when, by aU the laws
of nature, they should be in bed, and the result is
diseases which make every hour of their lives an
hour of torture. Their men-folk are just an extra
burden to their lives —failures who will never do any
good. Such men as get into trouble never seem to
rally, and they are dependent ever after on the earn-
ings of their wives and mothers. If they could only
get off somewhere else where their offence was not
known they might make a fresh start,,but they come
back to their old friends—their bad companions
and their wives have got to keep them
Not only in South London is the slum mother rh
breadwinner as well as the homekeeper. In some nf
those parishes which lie off the Harrow-road th
women are doing all the fighting while the men idl!
around the street comers until it is time to go hom
—to supper. “The slum husband,” said a curatT
experienced in rescue work, “has no sense of his re’
sponsibilities. The wife keeps him without question'
Of recent years it has become the recognised thin?
If the man does an odd job and earns a few shilling
that is regarded as his own pocket-monev—somethin?
for beer or betting. A woman takes a husband often
just for the sake of respectability, and, after that
she has got to keep him. Sometimes he mav ?n
along all right for a year or two, but, when unem
ployment comes, the wife takes on the work of earn
ing money, and the man rarely tries again
There was a man who had a job on the railways
for 17 years. He was convicted of stealing a packet
of cocoa, and imprisoned. That was five years aeo
and he has done no work since. His wife has joined’
the great army of earning-mothers, and has managed
to keep him going ever since. See the loafers stand
ing at every street corner. You could count a' hun
dred in a ten-minutes’ walk. Who mends and darns
and makes for them? Their womenfolk: Whv the
women clean their boots and make their beds’ am]
tidy up after them, and then go out and earn monev
to feed them as well. This lack of pride in the
slum husband is growing to be a serious thin? it
is wrecking the health of the women, for whatever
their courage, they are not built for continuous
work, and it is filling the streets with slum children
whose mothers have no time to look after them.”

Owing to the high price which anvthine
with a bit of fur on it is bringing, native
game in Tasmania threatened to become as
rare as green elephants. The last Tas-
manian Parliament, faced with this danger
passed a Bill to protect for 12 months
opossum, kangaroo, black swan, and a few
other fish. This law. having just come into
force, has produced the usual crop of letters
from “Struggling Settler.” “Cakeful
Cockie,” etc., which when carefullv
examined, seem to smell of the hand of skin-
buying storekeepers mostly. A Forestry
League is also trying to stop some of the
great waste of Tasmanian timber, and to
do something in the way of replanting.
These measures, of course, are driving the
usual amount of capital out of the
When, in 1804, Governor Collins was in
Hobart (which consisted then of half a
dozen wattle-and-daub huts, one weather-
board house, and a tent for the hospital),
he issued ordnances protecting the black
swan during the breeding season, and for-
bidding the cutting down of trees until they
had been passed by the Government
carpenter. He also, by the way. fixed the
rate of wages and the price of provisions.
Unfortunately, the Hobart Mercury was
not then in existence, so there is no record
of the way capital rushed for the coast.
Peaceful Geelong (Vic.) is disgruntled and
out of sorts. At the recent election it con-
founded itself by helping to displace Labor
representative Ozanne. and then giving more
votes to Labor candidates for the Senate
than to Fusionists; also by giving a majority
of affirmative votes to the Referenda pro-
posals. Rut that isn’t what is worrying it.
It has discovered that it is too moral —that
the common or pestiferous burglar, house-
breaker and petty lareenist avoids it as if
it were flat beer. It doesn’t want the
burglar or his friends, but it wants the
common or perambulating John, whom the
burglar makes necessary. Bendigo, it seems,
requires 21 policemen to keep it in order,
and Ballarat needs 22, whereas Geelong, with
much the same population, has but a paltry
nine. Wherefore, Geelong deputationised
John Weary the other day, demanding to
know what he meant by this "injurious dif-
ferentiation?” as Bruce Smith said when
the railway refreshment-room handmaiden
gave him one large sausage, while the farmer-
passenger got two small ones, which, in the
aggregate, represented 25 per cent, more
sausage. Wdiary said Geelong ought to be
proud of its notorious absence of crime: and.
instead of giving it more, he had half a mind
to take some constables away, seeing that
wickeder parts of the State were very short
of them. Having thus failed to get its new
uniforms. Geelong isn't sure whether it hasn't
made a fool of itself. The modern criminal
is a discerning animal, who reads the
papers, and he will now doubtless visit
the moral town, and spy out its nakedness—
and when he departs it will be more naked
than before. Then it will want police in
dead earnest, and they won’t be any use
when it gets them.
