PERSONAL
ITEMS
One vanished Liberal, who will be little
regretted, is the ill-tempered Hedges, of Fre-
mantle (W.A.). Hedges staggered hu-
manity, in a manner of speaking, when he
defeated Carpenter, in 1906. by 169 votes.
He roused suspicions of lunacy' on the part
of the electorate when he won. in 1910. bv
nearly 1300 votes. But, in 1913. he has
fallen heavily, and Burchell’s victory runs
to some thousands of votes.
[James Edmond, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
PROFESSOR
BALDWIN
SPENCER ,
Victorian Aboriginal
Authority.
From an old Victorian pressman in
London: —
Died in London, at 80, David Boswell Reid, long-
time surgeon of Geelong Hospital. He built up
there a big reputation as a successful operator, and
cases came to him from all parts of Australia.
Originally he worked his passage as an ordinary
sailor from London, and was actually wearing sea-
man's rig when he appeared before the committee of
the Geelong Hospital. Some of them thought he
must be a fraud or a humbug, but his diplomas and
qualifications were of the best, and they decided to
give him a trial. He soon made good.’ Unluckily,
he went into the wool business with the Synnotts, a
family into which he had married, and a great mon-
etary burst ensued. That drove h ; m from Australia,
and he took an appointment in a West End (London)
hospital. His father was the supervisor of ventila-
tion of the British Houses of Parliament: and when
the subject cropped up again, the son gave evidence
before a Parliamentary committee, of which another
old Geelongite, J. F. Hogan, was a member. A son
and daughter of Dr. Reid went into the London
music halls, and under the name of "Paul and Vir-
ginia,” did a pretty good turn.
McGowen, who partially scuttled from
N.S.W. Labor Premiership last week, was
leader of the party 22 years ago, but Holman’s
story to the Herald, that "Jim” had had an
"uninterrupted leadership for 22 years,”
doesn’t quite fit the facts. Things were mixed
in the Labor party of 1891, and the roll
was never quite the same two weeks run-
ning. The party was supposed to be 36
strong at the start; but there was a time
when it got down to 16 or 17. It was at
this low-water mark that McGowen was
elected leader; but when, after a few weeks,
the split was glued up, it was on the under-
standing that Joe Cook should lead. W.
H. Sharp, from the Government Printing
Office, was the first chairman of the Caucus:
and he, with McGowen, J. D. Fitzgerald
and Gough (presently succeeded by Cann),
were the first committee, with Houghton
as secretary. There was no leader then or
for some time after. At the 1894 election
Toe Cook ran as an Independent- Laborite, so
McGowen was elected leader once more;
and he has had the job ever since. Of the
men who attended the first caucus in ’9l,
Arthur Rae. Edden, McGowen, Cann, A. J.
Kelly and George Black are still in politics,
but McGowen, Edden and Cann are the only
ones who haven’t dropped out for a while.
Holman, the new Premier, didn’t arrive till
1898.
Alban Hill, who has been most things in
the Commercial Bank of Australia, definitely
retired from the service lately after 31 years’
strict attention to business. After keeping
him for many years at the weary job of
taming the wild cats that the busted boom
left on the head-office premises, they made
an inspector of him, and then turned him
into chief manager in X. S. Wales, with
head-office in the lobster-salad building in
George-street. There or somewhere the
damp got into his bones, and rheumatism
tortured him into taking long leave, with a
regretful retirement at the end of it. Quit-
ting Sydney, he has settled down at Malvern
to take as much ease as the little devils in
his blood will allow him. If he shifted his
camp to Longreach. in Middle Queensland,
which is mostly inhabited by cured rheu-
matic patients—it was the boiling bore
water which cured them —Alban Hill might
wake up and start a new bank.
‘L mfaan" : A good Australian
is painter H. S. Power, a Vic-
torian domiciled in England for
purposes of tucker, and now in
the land of his birth for the pur-
suit of holiday and to paint a real
Australian picture for the London
Academy. He is son of old-man
Power, the Victorian artist of 87.
still coloring canvas for a liveli-
hood. but thinking of retiring
shortly. Power believes that Aus-
tralia is the best country in the
world for an artist to work in. but
alas! not to sell in. Speaking of
Australians working at the other
end. Longstaff, he says, has ex-
celled himself this year, sculptor
Parker cuts ice whenever he chips
marble, and Fred Leist is looked
the youngster with the brightest
upon as
prospects.
Amongst the string of old identities of
Maoriland who have been harnessed up
lately for the Home Station was Archibald
Kelly, who was one of the first Presidents
of the Australasian Institute of Marine
Engineers. He first sighted Australia’s shore
aboard the celebrated old Stormbird. in 1854,
out from Scotland to Melbourne. Proceed-
ing to Auckland in the Wonga-Wonga. he
remained three years, returned to Australia
and. throwing in his oil-can and spanner,
went off in search of the gold he never
found. After pursuing it for four years he
turned back, hunted up the oil-can and
spanner and tramped the seas again for a
couple of years. Thenabouts he joined the
Maoriland Government Service on the Wai-
kato River during the native troubles. After
that, the Panama Co., M.L. Shipping Co..
and others. He was 77 years old when
Death rang down. “Stop !”
Acting Premier Weary sent a thrill of
satisfaction through the Melbourne press
last week with a notification that his friend,
ex-policeman O’Callaghan, had written him
from Fiji to say he was enjoying his trip to
America. Melbourne also is feeling be L
for it, so everybody’s happy.
THE LISP OF THE JEW.
[For The Bulletin.]
Then the Cabineth broke up.
—lnspired mistake made by a daily paper.
The shadow of Israel is dark on the land,
And the sign of its pawnshop is glowing;
The N'oses have sniffed at our good note-of-hand,
And we’re not very sure where we're going.
We’ve a dim sort of feeling that all is not well—
That Calamity’s trotting behind us:
Still we're shuffling along, though no prophet can tell
Where the cold light of morning will find us.
We’re privately sober and publicly drunk
With regard to financial transactions;
Our Governments all in the loan-cup are sunk;
’Tis the vice of all parties and factions.
Ministerial tankers filled up with gold wine,
E’en the national credit are shaking;
And an echo we hear of a lisp and a whine
When we read of the “Cabineth” breaking.
x.s.w. FOE.
CLEMENT WRAGGE'S LONG OVERDUE
DROUGHT.
(It struck Sydney last week.)
Four Labor members now obtrude them-
selves in Vic.’s unpaid House of Fat Land-
lords. Adam McLennan was the pioneer.
He has been obtruding for nine years. For
three years he obtruded on his lonesome ; then
W. J. Evans joined him. For the next three
years the tough job of presenting the Labor
case in the Freezing Chamber fell on these
two. Then, in 1910, J. P. Jones arrived.
Now Fielding has come from Footscray in
place of Minister for Works Edgar.
McLennan, the pioneer, is a wholesale meat
merchant: Evans, a tremendous worker, is
see. of the Vic. Loco. Engine-drivers’ Union.
MILESTONES;
Or the latest Fantoid produced by the last
bottle of the Great Election Booze.
Joseph TV illiam Robinson, of Broadford
• lie.), holds a record. A week or two
back he celebrated his 52nd birthday by win-
ning his 52nd lawsuit —a County Court
claim for £l5O, reduced to £97. Robinson’s
various and varied legal bouts with his fel-
low citizens, all of which he has won. have
occurred in Police, County and Warden’s
Courts. His long success seems to have
persuaded him that he is invincible, so he
has now announced his retirement —any-
how. until he thinks someone is presuming
on his sworn declaration of peace.
"Melb" among the house-knackers:—
As I write, there is being knocked down, in a
literal sense, a business place intimately associated
with James McCulloch, four times Victorian Pre-
mier. It is the old bluestone warehouse of Dennis-
toun Brothers, at the corner of King and Little Col-
lins streets. McCulloch came to Melbourne as agent
for this great Scotch firm. He soon became a lead-
ing man in the Chamber of Commerce, and Gov-
ernor Latrobe hooked him as a promising Torv
nominee for the old Legislative Council. When the
new Constitution came in 1856. Mac held subordi-
nate office in two Cabinets. The two Ministries of
Haines, the three of O'Shanassy, Nicholson's one,
and the one of Heales foHowed with dazzling cele-
rity—seven in about seven years. Then came the
first stable kind, the original Fusion, with McCul-
loch as Premier. Fellows was in it for a little
while. He, McCulloch, Higinbotham and Michie
were Freetraders. Francis and Verdon were Pro-
tectionists. Heales was taken in from the Demo-
crats. This Cabinet startled the countrv with a
10 p.c. Protective tariff, and the fat was in the
fire so far as Collins-street and “the Lane” were
concerned. Under Higinbothain's fierv impulse
McCulloch developed into an advanced' democrat,
but afterwards fell', away, under the influence of the
wife, Miss Inglis, who had been sent out to him
from Scotland.

The Fog is going to lose Will Dyson, the
Australian cartoonist. A Chicago paper
lately offered him £l5OO a year. “Bill"
wasn’t anxious to move on, so replied that
nothing less than an offer of “over £2000”
would bring him. According to a letter just
received by elder brother Edward, the “over
£2000” offer has come, and the Australian
takes the Chicago job at £2lOO.
S. Aus. Geographical Society has got in
hand about £2OO towards a memorial to
McDouall Stuart. There is already a statue
of him in Adelaide; the new memorial is to
be an obelisk on the spot
( Chambers Bay. Northern
Territory) where he struck
the northern sea after his
transcontinental march from
Adelaide, in April. 1862.
Dashwood. when Resident
at Darwin, found that the
tree “blazed” by Stuart had
been burnt down, so he put
up a post to mark the spot.
Mitchell, a later Resident,
found the post destroyed by
white ants, and fixed up s
steel rail. The present idea
is to send along a concrete
obelisk about 13ft. high. The
cost of transit must be
heavy, but the Australian
Government has promised
£2O towards that, and subscriptions have
come in so well that now a marble column
is talked of.

ment had promised that bridge. He had heard
Henry Parkes promise it 25 years ago.”
THE ELECTION
JACK-IN-THE-
BOX: “ They're
telling me the
old, old story. "
Dead in Adelaide, at 80, E. W. W ebb, one
of Australia’s most experienced journalists.
After leaving the London firm of Waterlow,
he and his brother were in the plucky syndi-
cate which, 60 years ago, ran the Melbourne
Age on co-operative lines, before Syme took
it over. Then he drifted to Adelaide Regis-
ter. but joined the Advertiser at its birth
in ISSS, and finished his career still on it-
Latterly he had lived on a well-earned pen-
sion from the Bonythons. In private life-
yachting —mostly with the late Judge Bundej
—was his chief interest. He never married.
A RANK OFFENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
J. M. Barrie, creator of “Peter Pan,” has beer,
lowered to a baronetcy. —News item.
The lights of fairyland are burning low :
The camp fires of the Redskins pale and die;
The kiddies sad to Mother Wendy go
To find out why
They now must walk, where once they used to
fly!
The crocodile ticks slowly while it weeps
Tears insincere, and seeks for tasty bits
Of Hook, who, caring not how close it creeps,
In silence sits;
His dear infinitives no more he splits!
Hushed is the merry voice of Tinker Bell,
And in the tree-tops Peter’s silent, too,
There’s something wrong, I’m sure, for, sad to tell,
The pirate crew
Seem ordinary blokes, like me and you !
Alas! they now are calling Barrie names!
Dazed by the gaud, his pen he slowly plies,
And ev'ry time a fool remarks, “Sir James,”
A pained world sighs,
And somewhere, I feel sure, a fairy dies!

The half-year’s profits were better than usual, no
doubt because practically everybody’s overdraft rate
went up simultaneously, whereas the rate paid by the
bank for deposits only advanced when the deposits
fell due. The time will come when the deposit and
overdraft rates will be hauled down again. Then,
maybe, the bank will have to charge lower rates for
overdrafts while still paying for a while the higher
rates on some deposits, and the profits will then be
as much under normal as for the past half-year they
have been above normal. Also, owing to the rule of
the Commonwealth Bank not to charge anything
for keeping debit accounts or accounts wh'ch are
£5O or more in credit, private banks are beginning
to have serious arguments with many customers who
don’t approve of the charge; and though, of course,
they will hang on to it as long as they can, they
are understood to be amenable to reason in the
matter. Which means that some at least of them
are foregoing the charge rather than lose a cus-
tomer. A lot of rubbish is talked and written about
this charge. Certainly it is absurd for a bank to
charge a customer 10s. a year for having the use
of his money, if you put it that way; but it is
equally absurd of the bank not to charge a customer
for, say, his cheque-books, pass-book, and other sta-
tionery. Also, the labor involved in keeping, say, a
sharebroker’s account is out of all proportion to the
value of the credit balances which some brokers are
alleged to maintain. So it boils down to this: some
customers pay far too much when they pay 10s. a
year, and some pay far too little; and common-sense
suggests that the charge should be a matter of
arrangement, as it now threatens to be. The
trouble from the banks’ point of view is that,
whereas they may get nothing in some cases, they are
very unlikely to get more than the old 10s. in any ;
and profits are not going to look any better in con-
sequence.
On one account and another, the National of Tas-
mania owes £685.264 to the publ'c, and it has
£203,262 in liquid shapes. This is 29 p.c., and
especially at a time like this, 29 p.c. is probably not
enough. Take the figures:—
May, May,
1912. 1913.
Notes in circulation.. £7,976 £5,317 Dec. £2,659
Bills, etc 14,114 29,932 Inc. 15,818
Deposits at interest . . 309,085 300,968 Dec. 8,117
Free deposits . . 330,845 349,047 Inc. 18,202
Liquid assets . . 231,836 203,262 Dec. 28,574
Advances . . . . 622,105 678,898 Inc. 56,793
Premises, etc. .. .. 28,825 28,238 Dec. 587
Deposits which may be called for to-day. or to-
morrow total £349,047 ; money in hand to pay it
with, £203,262. The balance is out in advances.
Possibly some of the advances can be got in quickly;
but it isn’t a satisfactory position from the stand-
point either of the depositor or of the borrower. A
gibe flung at the banks lately has been that they
haven’t lent as much as they could, and should, have
lent. In this paper’s opinion, this and a good many
other banks have lent too much. No doubt, given no
iolt of any sort, and always assuming that every-
body will keep his head, things are quite right,
because the bank’s own funds are equal to nearly
£1 for every £3 of the liabilities. Deposits, etc.,
are therefore well protected, provided the bank
never has to realise hurriedly. But it isn’t safe to
assume an eternal absence of alarms and panics.

Thus in 12 months the surplus of liquid assets has
been reduced by £15,306. But the money hasn’t
merely fallen through a hole. This table shows
where it has gone:—

So in six months the machinery assets has sprung
from £24,926 to £58,561, the result of a new in-
stallation of printing gear at a cost of about £35,000.
Presumably the old plant will be thrown out of
commission, and the chances are that the greater
part of it will have only scrap value. Thereupon
the question arises whether it has been written down
sufficiently. If it hasn’t been, then the net profits
hitherto have been less than represented. This is a
pretty common trouble with machinery users. They
reckon on a plant running, say, 10 or 15 years, and
provide for replacement at that rate out of gross
profits. What they don’t provide for is the possi-
bility of having to throw out the comparatively new
plant much sooner and get in the very newest in
order to stave off threatened competition or meet
actual competition. In this case the competition
was only threatened. The Sun, of Sydney, talked
of going to Melbourne, and had some of the money
for the purpose. What it wanted as much as any-
thing was a Melbourne paper to share the cost of
its independent cable service. The Herald, finding
how the wind blew, offered to come in. It doesn’t
say what it paid for the right to come in, but in the
balance-sheet this new item appears—“ Shares in
other cos. at cost, £9500.” Probably that has some
connection with it; anyway, it was said in Sydney
that something in the neighborhood of £lO,OOO was
received by the Sun for organising the service. The
two cos. will “jointly control” the new service, and
the directors rightly think that this say in the cable
business is “essential in the event of having to
meet competition.” It is good to see the directors
thus expressly recognise the possibility of competi-
tion. In fact, competition is more than possible.
It is inevitable, if Melbourne keeps on growing. And
then time only will tell what the Herald’s good-
will is worth. At present it stands in the books at
£198,000, so that, though large profits have been
accumulated, there is still £65,291 of the capital
( £ 250,000) unrepresented by tangible assets, and
waiting to be made good out of undivided earnings.

The recent shrinkage in the profits is probably more
or less due to the fact that the co. gave up a fire
and marine insurance agency in order to stick more
closely to its proper trustee job. On the whole, it
is advisable that a trustee co. should be that and no-
thing else: there are few businesses that don’t carry
some risk, and the funds of a trustee co. shouldn’t
be risked in side-lines—the man who is making a
will and is appointing a trustee co. as his executor
should feel sure that the whole of the capital and re-
serves will be available as a fund guaranteeing the
right performance of his and other trusts. Still,
though the profits have eased off, they leave a very
fair amount to lay by after the thumping 15 p.c.
dividend is paid. For this result, however, interest
is more largely responsible than ordinary business
earnings. The latter are distinctly moderate: com-
missions last year amounted to £3669, while salaries
and other office exes., exclusive of taxes, ran to
£2589. It is the nearly 30 years’ accumulation of
surplus profits that gives the co. its present pull as
a dividend-payer. Capital on which the div. is paid
amounts to only £9375, while the total funds at
work for the shareholders are £23,255; and 5 p.c.
on that lot alone can pay a T2* p.c. div. As to the
nature of the investments, nothing of any conse-
quence can be said; £10,500 is vaguely described
as in “debentures,” and £12,223 is thrown into a
heap labelled “mortgages and leasehold and build-
ings.” Presumably the leasehold is being written
down, though there is nowhere any hint to that
effect.
The New York Life Insurance Co.; —
This mutual office still does a certain amount of bus-
iness in Australia, though the pursuit of policies has
eased off since the American law set a limit on the
amount of new business and the money to be spent
in getting it. While the grossly exaggerated yarns
were being told about the manipulation of the big
Yankee insurance funds, business was not only hard
to get, but large sums were allowed to lapse. The
scare is, however, as dead as Pontius Pilate now,
and the figures of this and other cos. show a steady,
healthy expansion. The New York Life’s total in-
come, for example, has moved this way:—
1907 £20,861,878
1908 21,008,853
1909 21,401,462
1910 22,099,262
1911 .. .. .. 23,152,124
1912 24,449,797
The new business taken in 1912 was the maximum
allowed by law ; and it was got for 91 p.c. of what
the law fixes as the maximum expense rate.
Under United States law it is necessary to re-
value securities every year, debiting the accounts
with any shrinkages and crediting them with any
increments. In the foregoing these movements have
been disregarded ; all that has been done has been
to debit or credit income, as the case might be, with
profits or losses made on actual sales. Taking the
fluctuations in value into account, things look like
this :
Shrinkages. Increments.

1911 .. 178,277 159,670
1912 . . 405.539 53,104
Judging by the pretty steady excess of shrinkages,
money has been tight in other places besides Aus-
tralia.
Still taking the net movements in the market-value
of the assets into account, the past year’s opera-
tions pan out as follows:

The “cash bonuses” paid to policy-holders amounted
to £2,350,084, or equal to about 13 p.c. of the pre-
miums. But the American plan isn’t the same as
the Australian. In the American office the “cash
bonus” is the bonus paid in cash during the cur-
rency of the policy. Whereas in Australia the
man who doesn’t let his bonuses accumulate is
looked on as rather a bad, shiftless lot, the American
idea is rather to encourage the drawing of the an-
nual “dividend.” Some, of course, is not drawn,
and is lumped in the end with “claims.” Hence no
fair comparison can be made between the “cash
bonuses” of the offices on the two sides of the Paci-
fic. The past year’s profit apportioned for division
this year was £3,157,241, which was equal to about
19 p.c. of the premiums. This, though fair, is less
than two-thirds of the Australian best. No compari-
son of bonuses is, however, of much consequence
without a comparison of premiums. What the in-
surer really wants to know is the net cost of his
insurance, or the net cost of what is offered him
compared with what others can offer him. The
bonus that is made by merely adding a trifle on to
the premium is just the same small fake that you
see in the tailor’s shop with the human spider in the
doorway—the small fake of crossing 60s. off the
ticket and writing 375. 6d. alongside it.
But security is a greater thing than net cost. It
is the first thing in life insurance. When a man’s
policy falls due for payment he is mostly too dead
to argue points with anybody ; also, he is mostly too
dead to go out and make further provision for his
widow. Ife pays in to-day and trusts his savings—
often his only savings—to the administration of other
people, not till to-morrow or next year, but perhaps
for 40 years. And mistakes are serious because, if
he is 30 now, he can never be 30 again, and he can-
not pull out five or 10 years hence except at a sacri-
fice. Perhaps, indeed, by that time he may not be
able to get any insurance at all, by reason of disease.
The security of an office depends mainly—in fact,
almost wholly—on the quality of the assets; and
this is a matter in which the American practice
seems better than ours. Their assets may be no bet-
ter ; our misfortune is that we can’t be sure how
good ours are. Also, we can’t be sure how bad they
are. There is no valuation of assets either every
year or every seven years. When the Citizens’ took
over the assets of one of the most conservative old
Australian offices a few years ago there was a valua-
tion of assets, and things like this happened:—
Value
Book value, agreed upon.
Freehold No. 1 .. £115,000 £45,000
„ No. 2 .. 34,821 16,000
„ No. 3 .. 35,000 17,000
Foreclosures . . 69,998 48,400
Sworn annual valuations, such as the American
offices have to submit, ought to prevent the inflation
of book values in that way ; and the further Ameri-
can practice of publishing a complete list of securi-
ties, other than mortgages, along with the values set
upon them, is another safeguard.

This is an amalgamated undertakery. It was origin-
ally formed, as mentioned above, to give as decent
burial as possible to three Melbourne land-boom
banks, and subsequently the remains of the Mer-
cantile Bank were removed to the same mortuary.
At the start 1,366,666 shares of 4s. each and
£392,485 worth of debentures were issued to the
old shareholders and creditors. As the latter were
mostly English and Scotch the office was fixed in
London, where it still is; hence the long wait for
the year’s figures. There is, however, a Melbourne
Board ; and, having the best idea of how the cat was
jumping, Australian investors have mopped up very
many of the shares. Things were pretty bad in Aus-
tralia in 1903, and the valuations on which the new
capital, etc., were based were very low. Values,
however,, improved rapidly, and large profits were
made on sales. These profits were put aside, by
arrangement, to protect the debentures, in case of
another slump. When the last of the debentures
were paid off, some of this reserve was divided in
1909. Then, when the Mercantile Bank Assets Co.
was absorbed, a new assessment was made, and
£173,987 was added to the book value of the pro-
perty still on hand. To get rid of this wealth,
though much of it still existed only on paper,
£246,559 was distributed either in cash or in 4 p.c.
debentures. This was equal to 3s. 4d. per 4s. share.
The absorption of the Mercantile involved also the
increase of the shares to 1,479,355. Since then the
old plan of buying up debentures with the proceeds
of sales has been followed. The next table gives a
summary of the work done in that way:—

That £12,644 for the past year was net. Actually
the surplus on realisations was £26,938, but £14,294
was applied to writing down various securities. And
the real profit may have been larger because “when
only a portion of a security has been realised at a
price equal to or in excess of the book-value of the
whole, the directors have continued their former
policy of placing only a nominal value upon the un-
sold portion.” That means, of course, that profit-
taking has been deferred —it will only show up when
the at present unsold portion comes to be realised.
But whether this will be worth much or little to the
co. nobody can say. For one thing, there is no tell-
ing from the published accounts how much these un-
sold portions amount to. In ordinary circumstances
one would not expect much pro tit on sales in future,
remembering that the valuation was made in 1910,
when price levels were high. Yet there is this fact:
on sales of properties standing in the books appar-
ently at about £84,000 there was last year a profit
of nearly £27,000. The property wasn’t, however,
all suburban land; the co. owned sheep stations (it
sold one recently) and a sugar plantation in Fiji,
and fine prices have been paid for such things lately.
But, though profits may still be made on sales, the
revenue from rent, farming, etc., will be cut into
steadily. In 1911 the ordinary trading profit was
£29,447; last year it was only £24,922. Assets
on December 31 consisted of £327,558 of properties
and investments, £135,043 of purchasers’ balances,
and £50,511 in cash. Liabilities (including deben-
tures) were £112,156, exclusive of the year’s divi-
dend. Deducting the dividend, and assuming the
properties, etc., are worth just book value, the
assets are equal to ss. Id. per share. But in view
of the result of recent sales, is it a fair thing to
assume that the £327,558 of properties, etc., are
worth only that much? It would, however, take an
average realisation of p.c. in excess of the book-
value to add another Is. each to the shares. Also, it
has to be remembered that, as the properties are
realised (assuming that it is intended to go on
realising them), it will scarcely be possible to get
more than 4 p.c. from the gilt-edged investments the
money is put into; and you can’t pay an 8 J p.c.
div. on that.

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*
Mine managers and engineers contemplating Re-
inforced Concrete construction should write for par-

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. Sd. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, Vs. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. —a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [2

Messrs. Mephan Ferguson Proprietary, Limited, of
Footscray, Victoria, have secured the contract for
the supply of steel pipes required in connection with
the duplication of the Bombay Water Supply. The
contract amounts to over £226,000, and the pipes
to be used are of the locking bar type, of which Mr.
Mephan Ferguson is the inventor and patentee.
Engineers will be interested to note that this Aus-
tralian invention has been adopted in preference to
other classes of piping, after critical and searching
enquiries as to the merits of the various types of
water pipes made in the world. It is worthy of
note that all machinery required to carry out this
work has been designed and manufactured at the
firm’s Footscray Engineering Works, and will be
shipped to Bombay, where the pipes will be manu-
factured.*
Messrs. Babcock k Wilcox, Ltd., have received
the order for the supply of all the steam generating
plant and accessories for the electrification of the
Victorian Government Railways. The acceptance of
this firm’s tender was strongly recommended by Mr.
Merz, the English expert. The contract amounts to
£258,000.*

probable that the 1908-9-10 figures are nearer the

So The Bulletin welcomes the ambitious stranger,
and wishes it well, and believes that it ought to do
well ; and, incidentally, it remarks that whoever
drafted the prospectus produced one of the clearest
and most complete documents of the sort issued in
Australia for many a day. The directorate is also of
the substantial sort.
The Melbourne Motor ’Bus Co. has begun
to get a grip on Melbourne’s congested traffic. Seven
Daimler ’buses, two deckers, each carrying 34 persons,
are running regularly between St. Kilda Esplanade
and East Brunswick, and this number will be in-
creased gradually. Each vehicle is alleged to be
earning £5OO net per annum, after making heavy
allowances for wear and tear and repairs. The life
of a ’bus is estimated at seven years, but the co. is
reckoning it five years and writing off 20 p.c. yearly.
The manager’s office is a room in the Vacuum Oil
Co.’s offices, so it looks as though the Standard Oil
Trust is taking a hand in solving Melbourne’s traffic
problems. Meanwhile shareholders in the Melbourne
Tram and Omnibus Co. are asking one another (but
not the directors ; they are afraid of them) why the
Tram Co. does not start motor-buses and get a footing
on the roads before June 30, 1916, when its conces-
sion expires
W. H. Soul, Pattinson & Co., Ltd., have,
it is understood, changed their mind. Instead of
issuing some shares at par to pay for new premises
in George-street, Sydney, they will, it is said, send
out bonus shares (probably one in two), and get the
money for the building either from their own
well-filled stocking, or from the bank, or a little
from both.
The liquidators of a little N.S.W. coal co.
that deserved a lot better luck, the Heddon Greta,
are making good progress with their doleful job. The
most saleable asset, the coal waggons, went
promptly, and brought in £15,600; and the £7600
received on account, plus the hire of other plant,
just wiped out the liabilities. The mine and unsold
plant and about £9lOO in cash and good bills (the
last payable in June, 1914) will be available for
distribution among the holders of the 20,844 pref.
and the 42,573 ordinary shares.
Questions having been raised as to the
respective value of the British Broken Hill’s ordinary
and privileged shares, the directors have had their
say in the last report. The ordinaries (300,000) are
paid to £1 ; the privileged (60,000) are paid to
8s For dividend purposes the shares rank alike;
but on liquidation “the amounts actually paid on
all issued shares would first have to be reimbursed
and any surplus would be distributed ratably in pro-
portion to the nominal value of the shares ” This
doesn’t seem right. If there is more than enough
to return the capital, then it must come out of pro-
fits ; and if those profits were divided prior to liqui-
dation, the shares would rank alike. Why should
it be otherwise if the division of the profits is de-
ferred until liquidation ? The right method seems
to be—(l) to pay privileged shares their Bs.; (2)
to pay ordinary shares their 20s. ; (3) to divide any
surplus equally among the 360,000 shares. Certainly
the point hasn’t much present practical importance,
though in the event of, say, a sale it might be
pushed into awkward prominence at any moment ■
and if there is any question about it, it would be
best to remove the doubt at once.
* * * *
British Broken Hill sends out its leisurelv
report for the half-year to the end of
December last. Profit was £61,837 after writing
s °"'ething, but not enough, off plant, etc. ; and
£12,822 spent on development was not charged to
working exes. This means that present profits are
inflated at the expense of future profits. While the
profits for the half-year were £61,837, the divs.
were £108,207 (7s. per share). Therefore, while
£81,324 was brought forward, only £34,954 re-
mained to carry forward to the current half-year;
and that amount was speedily cut into by declaring
another Is. div. No doubt the successful issue of
new capital late last year greatly improved the
financial outlook, and iustified the directors in let-
ting go some of the accumulated profits; still share-
holders mustn’t think that what they have got lately
has been earned in the period.
The £61,837 profit (assuming that the true profit
•"as £61,837, about which this paper has the
doubts before-mentioned) was made by the British
on an output of 103,150 tons. That and a few
tons more were sent to the lead mill, and the result-
ing concentrates contained 363,0430 z. silver and
9423 tons lead. The profit was therefore approxi-
mately £6 11s. per ton of recoverable lead. That
is to say, a fall of a trifle over £6 10s. for lead
would wipe out this co.’s profit, taking last half-
year’s figures as a basis. The plant has been eating
up about 17,000 tons of ore a month. Rearrange-
ments and additions in progress are expected to
bring up the capacity to 20,000 tons a month,
though the mine isn’t yet in a position to supply
that quantity of a consistent grade. With the arri-
val of George Klug, 8., M. and Co.’s manager, as
consulting engineer, it was, of course, certain that
the Elmore zinc process would go out. The Minerals
Separation plant, as used by the Zinc Corporation,
also in the B.M. stable, is being put in. Calculat-
ing on £23 SDelter and £l5 lead, Klug estimates
that the alterations will put £30,000 a year on to
the profits. That sounds well. It would sound bet-
ter if Klug could guarantee £23 snelter and £l5
lead. The £l5 for lead may be safe, but £23 for
spelter begins to look very risky. In 22 half-years
down to the end of 1910 spelter only six times
averaged £23 or more; and there has been a very
uncomfortable drop of nearly £2 in the past month.
This and the uncertainties of lead (which averaged
as little as £l2 Ixs. Bd. in one half of 1910) con-
stitute the big end of the Broken Hill gamble.
The ore will last longer than the present high prices
of metal. It will no doubt even outlast the next
slump in metals. The British certainly doesn’t say
what its reserves are: but the mill couldn’t exhaust
them in six years, and they are being added to splen-
didly. » » *
The Melbourne people behind the Lambing
Flat (N.S.W.) dredging proposition have lost no
time. H. C. Armstrong’s option on the leases runs
till November, but the option co. knows that, if the
gold is there, the sooner it is out the better, so a
boring plant has been set to work this week. The
deepest ground to be tested will be about 75ft. ;
most of it is from 45ft. to 50ft. Frank Powell, who
reported, likes the look of it; but it’s the values that
have to be determined—if they’re right, the show is
right, because no other difficulties are in sight. The
option co. is in 700 £lO shares, of which the public
took 500 at one unsatisfied gulp.
Pine Hill South (W.A.) has passed away.
It first sustained financial injury at Golden Valley ;
then it got a bump at Meekatharra ; finally the
Manxman South Extended, near Bullfinch, put it
out of its misery. For a time things boomed at this
last show. Splendid ore was alleged to exist in
“both ends” ; then it was reported that “exceed-
ingly rich stuff” was still bagged, and the mana-
ger wired to the directors that he was building a
house for the safe storage of the bagged ore. Later
on it was stated that the rich shoot had disap-
peared, and, later still, 30 tons were crushed for
60oz. After that, next to nothing was heard of the
co., so the Westralian mining public wasn’t sur-
prised to hear of its death.
For the four months ended April Wes-
tralia exported £79,749 worth of minerals other
than gold, of which £18,362 represented copper,
£22,348 tin, £BB7O silver, £11,133 silver-lead ore,
and £19,036 miscellaneous. Total exports to date
are valued at £3,229,760, of which two-thirds were
supplied by copper and tin.
* * * *
Paringa’s latest find looks like providing
more than is necessary to keep officials in comfort.
On the western side of its long-held Kalgoorlie pro-
perty a shoot of ore averaging 84s. has been proved
for a length of 80ft. and a width of 51in., and
hopes are entertained that it will keep on keeping
on for some distance further. The south shoot in
the same ore channel yielded £12,500 from 4100
tons, but it was only 125 ft. in length. Up to the
present the mine has only been a source of income
to miners, tributors, officials and firms who supply
mining requisites. Shareholders have received no-
thing, and probably never will. There have already
been two reconstructions.
Princess Royal’s fate hangs in the balance.
Some months ago State Government aid in the shape
of a £2OOO subsidy ( £ for £) was obtained, and
a prospecting winze was sunk from the bottom level
of the co.’s Norseman derelict. It was continued
down until the ore gave out, when it had to be
stopped for want of funds. As the Government
wouldn’t give the co. any more money, and as its
chances of getting any elsewhere are not brilliant,
the Board recently called shareholders together to
consider the position. It was mentioned that ex-
emption had been obtained, and that there was a
possibility of an outside co. lending a hand. In
view of this, it was decided to adjourn for a month
on the chance of something turning up. Past ex-
perience, however, has proved that there is an un-
profitable time ahead of those who expend capital
in looking for wealth in Norseman deep ground.
* * * *
Kyarra (W.A.) claims that developments
justify the belief that its Meekatharra property
shows a profit on actual and probable reserves of
£60,000. Within the next few months the work now
in hand should, it avers, be advanced to a “point of
finality” whatever that may mean; and as only a
year has elapsed since the co. was formed, it is of
opinion that shareholders should be well satisfied
with past results and future prospects. The accounts
showed a credit balance on April 30 of £3096. At
present about £lOOO a month is being spent on de-
velopment and plant, consequently profits are on the
small side and are likely to remain so for some
time; but everybody should benefit in the long run
by this bold policy.
Managing-director Shallcross thus ex-
plains the Westralian Bullrush’s position, financial
and otherwise. It is the sole owner of the Royal
Standard, which should become a revenue-producer
in July. It is the holder, also, of 31,705 out of
60,000 shares in the Kyarra, whose appearance war-
rants the belief that it will start paying dividends
next October. Excellent arrangements, Shallcross
says, have been made for financing the Bullrush Co.,
and so far as he can see there is no reason to antici-
pate that shareholders will be stirred up for any
more money after payment of the 14th call in July.
Probable ore reserves are estimated at 65,000 tons,
worth 365. per ton. So far the gold recovery by
ordinary battery amalgamation has averaged 31s.
per ton; but, as a result of recent experiments, a
much closer extraction is expected in the future.
Costs, excluding development and office items, should
not, it is reckoned, exceed 15s. per ton when crush-
ing is resumed.
Marvel Loch (W.A.) is exploiting the
Comet option for all it is worth, and a trial crush-
ing, “estimated to yield not less than 2oz. per ton,”
is to be put through immediately. Local holders
who, knowing the weaknesses of the Comet, bought
for a rise, are to get out when the return is an-
nounced. It is the same right through with eastern
cos. operating in Westralia. The market is the most
important thing.
Mountain Queen (W.A.) has obtained
partial exemption on its second string—the Trans-
vaal leases—in order to make financial arrangements
for the erection of a £40,000 plant. Extensive ex-
periments made at Broken Hill and Ballarat have
shown that the iron, sulphur and arsenic associ-
ated with the gold in the ore are not going to inter-
fere very much with its treatment, and when every-
thing is in full swing the co. hopes to make monev
out of its arsenic as well as out of its gold. It is
a great thing to be hopeful.
Manager Morgan’s estimate for year
ended December 31 last credits Bullfinch Prop.
(W.A.) with 158,313 tons, of a gross value of
£409,661. Take off production costs, and a profit of
approximately 10s. per share is left. In a supple-
mentary statement to May 31, Morgan reports a num-
ber of extensions in good ore at the 1, 2 and 3
levels, and adds that a vigorous mining policy is
now in full swing, and that the tonnage, will be
increased in July.
* * * *
In some respects the management of
Mount Morgan isn’t likely to improve as the result
of the sale of the late Walter Russell Hall’s shares in
a block. A circular has just been issued by the new
secretary (almost the whole Mount Morgan outfit is
new) intimating that, among other things, it is pro-
posed to have not less than seven nor more than 10
directors, besides a London Board, and that, in the
interests of continuity of policy, it is proposed that
seven of the directors and four members of the
London Board shall be fixtures until 1918. This
“continuity of policy” pretext is too thin. If the
shareholders are satisfied that the policy which is
being pursued is a good one, directors will not be
interfered with. It is only when a policy is in-
fernally bad that shareholders can be roused to
action; and then they should have the power to
throw out the directors guilty of that policy. So long
as a policy is reasonably sane and honest, it isn’t
necessary to fix the directors in their seats for five
years certain in order to secure continuity. That
course is only necessary in order to secure contin-
uity of a policy which isn’t reasonably sane and
honest. If the Mount Morgan management thinks
that its policy, or the policy of any of its directors,
during the next few years may not be reasonably
sane and honest, it will, of course, push the proposed
amendment of the articles through. If not, it isn't
worth while.
Chillagoe is putting up a five-head battery
on the Percy goldfield (N.Q.) to crush stone from
the Union and other shows. There have been bat-
teries on the Percy before, but in those days equip-
ments were primitive, and the mineralised stone met
with at depth was too hard a nut to crack. What
gold the battery and tables can’t save will now go
to the smelters in the shape of concentrates.
* * * *
The Moonstone line at Charters Towers
(Q.) will get a lot of prospecting. The Brilliant and
St. George has started to sink, and expects to strike
it at 600 ft. As there are numerous leaders in the
vicinity, something of value may be met before the
Moonstone is reached. Having nearly exhausted its
capital, the Brilliant and St. George is reconstruct-
ing. There will be 144,000 shares, half of them
contributing, and the other half (paid-ups) will be
given to contribs. as a bonus. Old shareholders get
36,000 paid-up shares (one for every four they now
hold). Kelly’s Queen Block will purchase the old
Welcome and Union leases on the Moonstone, and will
have to raise fresh capital unless the workings on
the St. George reef and the Brilliant cross reef turn
out more. Tom Mills, who is taking so much in-
terest in deep-sinking at the Towers, is chairman of
the London Board of Mills’ United. He favors sink-
ing a circular shaft at Lissner Park to a depth of
4000 ft. if necessary, but it is quite on the cards that
the Brilliant would be struck before reaching that
depth.
* # * *
Brilliant Extended, Charters Towers,
made a profit of £1249 for a run of four weeks, and
the directors, encouraged by the improvement, pay
a sixpence ( £6250). This dividend is the first
this year. It is, however, worth remembering that the
profit for the half-year amounted to only £5104,
although 12,120 tons of stone were crushed for
£24,019, and 10,700 tons of tailings cyanided for
£8195. The cash credit at May 16 was £17,864,
of which £lO,OOO is on fixed deposit. With regard
to development work, the most hopeful feature is the
extension of eastern levels. Ventilation in these
deep mines is greatly improved in cool weather, and
the better flow of air through the workings has
enabled the manager to open a block of ground at
No. 15 level.
* * * *
New Specimen Hill, Daylesford (Vic.),
was one of the shows that found favor during the
North Nuggetty boomlet; but a thud followed when
it was proved that the stone didn’t average better
than that worked by the old gone-bung English co.
Then, when all seemed blue, a strike at the 430 ft.
livened things up. A flat make was also found, and
on these particulars the market moved too fast for
Daylesford to get a decent cut in. Now there is a
lot of talk about a dividend ; but, like the gold in
the mine, it will be there when it is there. The co.
is poking down another lift, and it will be good
news if it hits rich stone; but this is a gambling
risk.
Ballarat knows Daylesford, but Melbourne
speculators won’t remember that to hustle out
quartz means to exhaust reserves, although bringing
dividends along more rapidly. And it is particularly
unsafe to forget this concerning Central Ajax. The
best development there has been in the Western lode
both at No. 6 and No. 7 levels. Any turn-up happen-
ing in that quarter would lead to a lively movement
among a heavy lot of bears. Out of the heap of
guessing over the Central ore reserves, one may
pick 265. per share as probably somewhere near the
mark. If more, the world doesn’t know the fact
officially ; but it is a dead cert, that once big crush-
ing is attempted, the trouble will be to hold the
grade up.
Toora Tin, South Gippsland, has Colonel
Hughes to do the amiable and Tom Birkbeck and
W. A. Mercer to do the talk. The hydraulic-sluicing
plant is to be an eye-opener in power, but that
doesn’t say that the claim is to be an eye-opener in
tin. The crowd that is putting up the cash for the
plant is supposed to embrace the Goldfields Consoli-
dated fatmen, who have piloted many a nice South
African deal through in London. But that doesn't
impress the cockies of those parts ; they know that
a hydraulic sluice can make a big mess of streams,
and they threaten blue ruin if the sludge gets into the
Franklin River from the claim. It has taken all
Tom Birkbeck’s suavity to defer an explosion till
the plant is eating up the gravel, and as he is mak-
ing an honest attempt to dam back all the sludge as
well as the bad language, the chances are he will get a
fair run. Seemingly, others than Mercer and Birk-
beck think Toora will boom, for every acre of drift
in the country-side is surrounded by pegs.
South German, Maldon (Vie.), trudging
along like an over-tired giant, got a pint of vitality
infused into its veins last week, when all the for-
feited shares went off like hot cakes. If this stimu-
lant fails, it will need a lot of faith to prop the old
claim up again. The one ray of sunshine is pro-
vided by the shoot which the tributors are working
at 1750 ft., and calls and cash from the forfeited
shares will go to prove whether it still lives in the
tight country at 1900 ft. Failing this, why should
not the South German have a final flutter over the
shallow ground? Nearly all the best gold in Mal-
don —-and that wasn’t a trifle —was got out of shal-
low shoots.
J.H.C.: Still very highly speculative....
M. 8.: The Bulletin’s statement that the Common-
wealth has a debt of £6,371,847 tallies with
Knibbs’s official figures. See “Finance Bulletin No.
6, Commonwealth Bureau of Census and Statistics,”
page 14: “Amount raised under Loan Act, 1911,
£700,000; balance of loans taken over from S. Aus.
on account of the N.T., £3,431,836; ditto on ac-
count of Port Augusta railway, £2,240,011: total,
£6,371,847.”... .St. K.: No, it doesn’t. The fact
that Commonwealth notes are legal tender doesn’t
oblige anybody, banks included, to pay in that paper.
It only obliges people to accept proffered payment in
it. And there’s a mighty difference between the
two things.
An Adelaide crowd which tried to boom
Binti Binti (W.A.) has now fastened on to an
option at Ora Banda, in the same State, and the
same yarns are being told. Listen to its expert:
“Have inspected the bottom level of the Victorious
and seen the mine plans....lf lode continues, it
must go into our property. .. .Their No. 3 winze,
showing high values, is not far off from east end.”
It would be interesting to hear what the Victorious
people have to say on the subject.
Sydney is having a chance to inspect
Ormiston metal, the new aluminium alloy whose
ponding production was mentioned here a while ago.
Hitherto aluminium, in spite of its virtues, has been
comparatively little used, mainly because (1) it
could stand no strain, and (2) it could not be
welded or soldered or brazed. The Sydney invention
which bears the inventor’s name is as light as
aluminium, and as it can be soldered, brazed or
welded, and also has much greater tensile strength,
it ought to make a stir on the earth if the price is
right.
Henry Jones, the Tasmanian who, with
E. T. Miles, brought Tongkah to the front, writes,
re a reference to his connection with Debook, a new
Miles venture in the same part, that he has nothing
to do with it, directly or indirectly.

A GRAND SPIRIT

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, June IG, 1913.
My Dear Arini, —
The cable says that the christening skirt
in which the infant Nelson was carried to
the font has been “knocked down” for £BO.
Its pretensions to be the genuine article are
probably as solid as those of many other
historical and unhistorical relics. Taking
them all the way down, from the fresh young
tree at Cooktown to which Cook tied his
ship before the shrub was born, to the bottle
containing some of the breath of Joseph of
Arimathea, relics are an uncertain sort of
business. The splash of red paint still
pointed out to gaping tourists as the blood
of David Rizzio on the wormy floor of Holy-
rood, is obviously paint and dust. The
Cockney tripper who felt he hadn’t had his
tip’s worth of Scottish bloodstain said so
to the guide, who naively admitted that it
hadn’t had a lick of the brush “for mair
years than he could count.” Returning to
the shirt in which Nelson may have howled
at his own christening, George W. E. Rus-
sell, in some reminiscences, mentions the
Nelsonian church that fell into disrepair
some years ago. “As a combination of
pietism with money-making which critics of
our national character tell us is so peculiarly
British,” he quotes the following advt.: —
The man who first taught Englishmen their duty,
And fenced with wooden walls his native isle,
Now asks One Shilling to preserve in beauty,
The Church that brooded o’er his infant smile.
A new and glittering profession has been
evolved in England. I found it in the report
of the funeral of Miss Davison, the suffra-
gette lady who tried to eat the King’s horse
while that animal was busy losing the Derby,
and received fatal injuries in her scuffle with
the animal’s feet. This is how the cablegram
reads :
Two carriages, hidden with white flowers, pre-
ceded the open hearse, escorted on either side by a
guard of honor with lilies, followed by relatives,
Miss Kenney, Miss Sylvia Pankhurst, Mrs. Despard,
Miss Sanders and a few men, including Ben
Tillett, MANY HUNGER-STRIKERS, several sur-
pliced clergymen, numerous society ladies, shop
girls, factory hands, nurses in uniform, women doc-
tors and graduates in their academic gowns.
So apparently hunger-striking is a profes-
sion nowadays, like being a shop-girl, fac-
tory-hand, Pankhurst, nurse, doctor, or Ben
Tillett, or even a sweet girl graduate in her
academic gown. Possibly the business will
spread and climb to higher grades, and in
time we will see brass plates in Macquarie-
street or Collins-street, with some such in-
scription as “Mary Bones, M.D., Professional
Skeleton.” Then you who are stout and
scant of breath and defy Puck to put a
girdle round you in 40 minutes will hasten
in an overloaded dray to see Dr. Mary Bones.
And while the wind blows coldly through
her she will tell you how to live, entirely
without food, and thus in time attain to the
higher and more spiritual framework.
Whereupon you will drop your guinea be-
tween her ribs and depart sorrowfully, feel-
ing that the Higher Life is beyond you.
And being of what medical works call a
“full habit,” you will probably get jammed
in the doorway as you go out, and have to
be pushed forth.
Sad to say, there wasn’t one new-bonnet
striker in the procession which followed the
poor Davison girl to her grave.
The late Arthur Guinness, who used to
Speak in the M.L. House of Representa-
tives, was famous for his silence. He had
enough of it about him to furnish the draw-
ing-room of a catacomb. During his last
holiday trip to this State, he glided over
. our scenic attractions, leaving fewer tracks
„of his presence, in printers’ ink, than any
...other person with a handle to his name who
Las ever been earmarked for interviews. In
spite of this oyster-like retreat into his
-shell when he laid aside his Speaker’s wig,
the late Arthur Guinness, Knight, was bet-
ter liked than many babbling brooks in his
home waters.
The veteran Mrs. Sophie Corrie (80 not
(.out) is skipping off to Queensland for a six
• months’ holiday tour. A tea party, to
1 farewell the blithe traveller, met together
\last week.
Solicitor Matthew Harris, a son of the
knight of Etliam (Darling Point), was
married the other day to Miss Lizzie Camp-
bell, who used to be a Nightingale in Sydney
Hospital. About 80 guests wished the bridal
pair the usual good luck with the help of
an old slipper. Mrs. Carty Salmon, who is
a sister of the groom, came from Melbourne
to be present at the wedding march.
The recently-returned W. A. Holmans put
in Saturday afternoon at the Symphony
Orchestral concert in the Town Hall. In
the evening they hurried off to the same
bleak address to get a farewell snack of the
Bispham baritone. The ascetic Holman
figure hasn’t added to its circumference since
it left these shores. The festive British
oyster is apparently no more fattening to the
lean kind than is the gigantic Stewart
Island species, for thin men rush round the
wind squalls of Invercargill in legions. The
Australian shell-fish is a small, tame beast
compared to its heavily-bearded brother from
Stewart Isle, which is an entire oyster trust
about the size of a saucer and not to bp
trifled with. The cables were so persistent
in reporting the Holman meals abroad tnat
one forgot that a politician generally eats
one dinner a day, wherever he is. Those
who expected to see the N.S.W. Attorney-
General-Premier come home the size of an
elephant remarked in scandalised whispers
the other night that he was “just as thin as
ever.” Mrs. Ada Holman looks very spruce
after her scamper across Europe.
The low-lying sward on the Rose Bay
links was mostly water on Saturday after-
noon. The casual players who were out for
a quiet practice splashed through the wettest
end of the wet, and made toy geysers when
a hearty smack caused a ball to break up
the happy frog home in a water-logged hol-
low. Part of the links are, apparently,
below sea-level, hence the spongy quality of
the golf that follows a winter deluge. A
long, thin figure, that was probably owned
by Prof. Fawcett, banged at a ball on com-
paratively dry ground, under the eye of
instructor Clark. Across the dryest alti-
tudes of the links 200 or 300 enthusiasts
pursued the struggle for the championship
between Sturrock and Apperly. These
goffers must have cast-iron nerves. The
leaps, rushes and splashings of the wet-
footed crowd that lined up like the spectators
at a country funeral, would have been dis-
tracting to shaky players. A mighty cheer
blew the mud up to the welkin when Stur-
rock or Apperly made an extra-special
drive, and 10 minutes later there would be
a rain of slush, owing to the welkin coming
down again. The finish, with Sturrock as
victor, occurred over the horizon of a very
green hillock covered with goloshes.
While the battle raged between Sturrock
and Apperly on one part of the Rose Bay
links, mixed foursomes was paddling through
the swamps on the other edge of the goff
paddocks. The Ladies’ Golf Union of
N.S.W., which is having a week of combat,
was up to its ankles in cold water on
Saturday. That smart little lady cham-
pion, Miss Duret, who can smack a ball
whatsoever way she listeth, hails from the
long, everyday wash of Manly’s waves. She
was out for first place with J. S. Marks,
the Rose Bay goffer, in the mixed four-
somes championship on Saturday. This was
a popular victory for the Manly girl, who
is a good sport. Mrs. Triggs and Oswald
Sealy; the indomitable Una Clift, a mighty
woman on the links, and her goff partner,
C. W. Rundle; the brisk Miss Trevor Jones
and her gentleman help, W. Blaxland,
stamped round on the trail of the conquering
Duret and Marks.
Some of the short goff coats worn by the
lady goffers at Saturday’s campaign were as
dazzling as tropical flowers. They made
luscious splashes of red, purple and blue on
a wintry day. Mrs. M. A. O’Callaghan had
a gorgeous dash of Tyrian purple about her
handsome appearance, as she hurried across
the links with the O’Callaghan in her wake.
The Norman Pilchers followed the game,
Mrs. Norman walking with a swift, swinging
stride through no end of very wet water.
Her hair, a generous auburn, curls of its
own accord on a damp day, and looks its
best when the straight-haired tribe are
covered with wisps like the leavings of a
bag of straw 7 .
The Manly links sent two first-rate goffers
in Mr. and Mrs. Wright, who won the mixed
foursomes handicap on Saturday. “The
Village” wastes little time over the frills
that make the Rose Bay goff house a place
where idle women loiter over teacups.
They’ve got to loiter, anyhow. It takes
from half an hour to an hour and a quarter
to drag a teapot out of some secret strong-
hold, where the kitchen range always sulks
on a big goff day. President Ted Simpson
tramped round as a spectator the other after-
noon. A gorgeous sunset flamed in the west
at schedule time. “Banjo” Paterson’s
muse, framed in a sac suit, was seated on the
upper terrace, with the charming Mrs.
“Banjo” at his side. Society, which was
present in considerable numbers, wore its
worst expression, owing to the fact that it
had to sit with its feet on wet grass for
quite 60 minutes, waiting for the kettle to
boil.
After the show the goffers were counted,
to see if any had got drowned on the links.
I don’t know how many were missing, but
I believe all the pools are being searched for
lost mothers and sisters and daughters, and
vanished bread-w'inners.
The Women’s Hospital (Crown-street)
wants all the help, and a bit over, it can
get. A Special Appeal is fishing for funds
with the fresh bait of shilling collection
cards. Hon. sec. Amy Mowle and hon.
treasurer Reynolds Denniston are casting
their nets in any likely stream. On July 5
the Little Theatre management will give a
matinee for the Cause. This will be
attractively garnished with tea and muffins,
sweets, and other toothsome temptations that
represent the Joys of Satan and send you
weeping to the dentist and the doctor.
Horticulture for women probably started
when Eve dug down the first potato from
the tree-top, to the everlasting sorrow of her
sex. We’ve been learning how to cook it
ever since. In the report of the recent
meeting at the Queen Victoria Club there
is no mention of the lady of the forbidden
apple, but I take it she was the lirst woman
gardener on record. Mrs. Laura Bogue
Huffman presided at the latest effort to poke
up horticulture for females in this State.
Theo Cowan, an Australian woman
sculptor of considerable note, has stepped
ashore here after nine or 10 years spent in
the pursuit of Art on the other side.
The Sydney Stage Society met the wei
tail of the recent rainfall for its ambitious
effort to produce Besier’s play ‘‘Don,” with-
out condescending to learn the lines. The
voice of the prompter frequently drowned
the crunch of chocolates in the stalls and
the rattle of paper bags in the gallery. Miss
Gavin Brown, who seemed to know more
about her business than any other amateur
present, got away with a large helping of
tiowers, and two boxes of sweets about the
size of cabin trunks. The Palace Theatre
was the scene of this performance. The
profits are to go to the Shakspeare memorial
fund. Owing to the inclemency of the mud
on the evening of the play the Bard is un-
likely to get more than a new shroud or so
out of ‘‘Don.”
The Bispham farewell concert in the
Town Hall was a triumph of Bispham over
popular prejudice. The one-man show in
Sydney has yet to leap into large financial
profits. Melba knows her Australian public
too well to give them a one-woman song
recital —granting that a lyric soprano
wouldn’t get a frog in her throat if she sang
continuously for two hours several times a
week. However, the American baritone,
after battling against flooded skies and
drowned highways, shattered the local tradi-
tion that asks for a concert company even
though some of its members be old-age pen-
sioners and the star singer is as fiat as
yesterday’s pancakes. Saturday night’s
audience was a record for a one-voice per-
formance.
David the singer surpassed his previous
efforts. He tuned his lay to the masterful
strains of Wagner, Mozart, Mendelssohn,
Lizst, Verdi, and other big guns. The ex-
tracts of Wagner as interpreted by Bispham
have set a standard that is likely to be
hurled at the Quinlan Opera Co. when its
male soloists plunge into “The Ring.” A
cyclone of applause stormed across the muni-
cipal echoes after Alberich’s Curse (“Rhein-
gold”) had cursed its cussedest. Pianist
Gilbert was in his best form, also. He made
a terrific attack on the difficult Liebestod
(“Tristan and Isolde”) and routed some
mountainous octaves with great slaughter.
Our best suburbs sent a large supply of our
worst-mannered and most expensive people.
They tumbled into the wrong seats, quarter
of an hour after the concert was in full
blast. Bispham closed down till the scuffle
was over. When the next breather occurred
the Always Late Contingent was on its feet
again and stamped into its own compound.
Hazon, by the way, was the only musician
who put the Lost Tribe of Potts Point in its
place, by shutting it out till the mid-pro-
gramme interval.
The little Jessica, with the large con-
tralto voice, achieved one of the few finan-
cially successful concerts of the recent
deluge. Mrs. Noah and all the little Noahs,
and all Ham’s wife’s relatives, and Shem’s
uncle by his father’s sister’s second mar-
riage, left the domestic ark, heavily shod
in rubber and clothed in umbrellas, and
paddled to the Town Hall for the occasion.
Besides the big audience present, hundreds
of absent ticket-holders, who were presum-
ably washed away from their moorings, had
contributed to the treasury box. Mrs. A.
N. Jonsen, president of the committee
which worked this affair to a full-house
climax, ought to shake herself violently by
the hand. The web-footed weather would
have blotted out the receipts of a less skil-
fully organised attack on the public purse.
It is up to the committee to keep a tight grip
on the cash, and lock it in a burglar-proof
money-box. When the time is ripe to pack
up Jessica and her unusual voice for for-
eign climes, another nest-egg may be added
to the present deposit.
Mrs. Alex. Gordon and the Gramophone
Co. are likely to have a duet together, while
the sweet-voiced Welsh-Australian warbler
is in London. This will give the Gordon
youngsters, when they cease to be young-
sters, the chance of hearing their mother’s
vocal art as it is to-day.
The council of the Actors’ Association
held a meeting of the cult on Sunday even-
ing. Walter Bentley’s studio was the pl'ace.
This mild entertainment was not an alarm-
ing feat of Sabbath breaking. A block away
the evening service in St. Andrew’s Cathe-
dral was undisturbed by anything more up-
roarious than the shrill screams of the
latest motor horns. One of these infernal
machines is exactly like a pig squealing in
the final agony that precedes pork,
which goes ahead of bacon. Some day the
Society for the Prevention of Cruelty to
Animals may arrest this petrol horn, also
another that bawls like a sick cow when it
is bogged in a mud-hole.

Someone or other said to someone or
other that Governor Strickland never went
out after sunset. Then Such-and-Such re
marked to Tweedle-dee that it would be no
use asking his Ex. to the llospice liall
which it would be inconvenient to hold
before the vice-regal chickens went to roost
When it was too late, a few intelligent per
sons recalled the fact that various reports
of the Strickland appearance, outside his
family circle, quite late at night, had
trickled through the press. At the eleven-
and-three-quarterth minute, a cast-iron ap-
pointment gripped the vice-regal presence
Unable to accept the belated invitation, his
Ex. will be represented by A.D.C. Forbes
and his pretty missis—before this meets
your eye.
The new Dutch boat Tasman was at
home to a crowd of visitors the other after-
noon. In its upper lounge, which has a
large hole in the middle threaded by a stair-
case, there is a bold bas-relief of the warm
reception that met the original Tasman in
1642 when he first peered at the Maori. The
chief impression of this marine display of
furiously indignant natives paddling across
a bronze sea, is, that most of those present
have either left their hair at home, or wear
it standing straight up on end, bristling
with fury and grease and whale oil. Man-
ager J. 11. C. Stuart and Skipper Goedhuis
were waiting to clutch the hands of all
coiners. Careful souls slithered up the
slippery gangway, keeping a firm grip on
the rail and one eye on the streak of inky
water that lapped the Dutchman’s side
below. Barefooted Javanese stewards stood
about like small brown images. The im-
movable countenances of these fragments of
humanity was atoned for by the different
phases of expression in every set of toes.
I suppose a Javanese still wears the visible
signs of his emotion in the place where a
higher civilisation grows its corns. Minister
for Works Griffith and his wife came along
the Dutchman’s deck while the company
was filling itself with tea. The Spencer
Bruntons ; Mrs. Sep Levy (chiefly clad in a
long silky black fur coat, cuffed and col-
lared with a small snowstorm of ermine);
the M.L. Tourist authority, E. H. Mont-
gomery. in the glad rags that include a
shiny top hat; Mrs. E. H. Montgomery,
very snne in mouse-colored furs and wintry
gown; Mrs. Jack Connack, and a hundred
or so more also ran.
Leila Wiburd, a youthful soprano, raised
in this town, will give a concert on June
26. This young songstress belongs to a
well-known sporting family, which is much
addicted to swimming and singing. It pur-
sues these recreations at different times o'
day.
Rebe Kussmann, a girl fiddler who used
to be one of the veteran Kretschmann’s in-
fant phenomena, has roused the London
Telegraph to a mild chortle of
praise. Re a recent recital given by stu-
dents at the Guildhall School of Music, that
organ deliberately remarked: “Miss Kuss-
mann, a violinist, obviously has musical tal
ent” (this sounds dull, doesn’t it?). “Her
interpretation of the allegro and andante
movements, etc were characterised by a
decision and a wealth of well-baianeed feel-
ing which showed that she is not only very
well grounded in the technique of her art.
but possesses temperament.” After this
kindly, elephantine effort, the critic, presum-
ably, took a well-earned rest, and retired in
good order on an aerated bun shop.
Eva Gauthier, the little French-Canadian
skylark, flew off on Saturday by a merely
mundane Brisbane express. The Tasman
had threatened not to budge an inch from
its wharf till the Symphony concert was
over, and the singer and her saucy I rencli
hats were on board. But the Dutchman un-
gallantly changed his mind and fled some
hours before the lady could keep her tryst.
She raced after the departed liner and beat
it on the road. So she vcas able to heave a
reproachful smile at her fellow-passenger.
Manager J. 11. C. Stuart, when his craft
bustled up to Brisbane, and ask him wna
or. earth had detained him.
Yours affectionatelv.

HERE is never any wondering "what shall I do to kill the time” in homes
where there is a STECIv PIANOLA PIANO.
From the moment it is installed you will find it hard to*resist its fas-
cination.
At first you will want to be playing it all the time —perhaps you will start
out with a collection of popular melodies, operatic hits, and lively cakewalks.
Then, as you become expert in playing it, you will want to dip into some of
the more difficult music, you will want to find out something about the great
composers, try over pieces you never even heard the names of, increase your
store of general information by a closer acquaintance with such famous names
as Mozart, Mendelssohn, Haydn, Schumann, Chopin and so on.
The money spent for a STECK PIANOLA PIANO is something more
than money spent for passing pleasure.
The Pianola Piano stands for EDUCATION, for RECREATION, for
RELAXATION from the cares of the day, for a broadening of the mind, for an
insight into the wonderful art of music.
It is something the whole family can enjoy and will profit by.
