MELBO URNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, June 16, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Marie Hall's coming-of-age dance was
easily the event of the month. It
happened on Wednesday of last week —
the night before the golden twenty-
first birthday which was bringing her
Grandpa Watson’s £16,000,000,000. (Per-
haps it isn’t as much as that; but I
always think a fish-tail of ciphers sets off a
pretty figure.) Marie herself wanted to
postpone the whirl on account of the serious
illness of her friend and travelling chaperone,
Mrs. Goodall. But all her girl friends had
their frocks ready, and their programmes
were already half-filled, and grief unutter-
able would have devastated the countryside
at the mere mention of nostponement. The
girls looked lovely ; in fact, there was not an
ordinary-looking maiden among the crowd.
(Gentle Reader ! if you mumble doubtfully
like that, 1 will move the suspension of the
Standing Orders and hit you with my um-
brella !. . . . Well, don’t interrupt!) Nina
McLeod, a special girl-chum invited from the
distant West for the frolic, was garbed in
gold, with a purple osprey. Marjorie Dunn’s
clothes were Deacock blue, and she risked
peacock’s feathers in her hair. The little
French Gomans daughter put a pinky-red
feather in her hair, and wore white. Mutiie
Huon scored simply with an all-black cos-
tume. Joan Innes-Noad, from Sydney, ap-
peared in a dream of blue and mauve, with
her baby-fair hair trickily dressed. Marie
Hall, hostess for this glorious night, was
wound in a scarf of silver and pink roses,
with a peep of purple here, and an edge of
sable fur there, and mamma’s present of
pearl and diamond jewellery glittering every-
where, and grandpa Watson’s gold shining out
of her eyes. Mamma looked resplendent in rose
brocade. The clinging sheath was gossamer
and plush, and it snaked faithfully after her
across the floor. Not once did she have to
stop and whistle to it to come on. Half a
dozen Naval Depot men struck beautiful
attitudes amongst the mere men civilians.
Jeff Stuart, a delightful young sailor, is the
greatest Society exponent of the Turkey Trot
and imported dances. (I suppose he stands
a good chance of being made an admiral if
he does his hornpipes well at sea!) “All
the girls are crazy to dance with him !” mur-
mured a chaperone. “They say he holds
them so beautifully!” (Happy reminiscent
sighs like summer zephyrs from all the con-
tented matrons. Bleak sniffs from the other
sort.) Muriel Starr, from the Theatre
Royal, came along after the show and
Trotted with grace. She shed an adorable
cloak of mole-color in order to rotate with
a youth or two. Mrs. Leslie Stuart upheld
the frocky reputation of our smartest
matrons in rose-embroidered black and gold.
Mrs. Murray Morton showed off a blue dress
that had been embroidered in Japan. Mrs.
Hewlett, another chaperone, brought a pretty
niece-in-law. Lesly Madden wore a grey
dress, and danced with young medical stu-
dent McGillicuddy, who saw to her Glaci-
arium injury, the other day. Two Frank
Madden misses enjoyed themselves hugely.
One fluttered about in spangled black, and
the other wore a blue frock with a cut-off
train like a telephone conversation. Sup-
per was a set midnight affair —practically
a good dinner transposed to an unnatural
hour. After supper was done with, the
guests took a fresh interest in life and began
a second dance, which lasted till the milk-
man, that wild harbinger of dawn, crowed
thrice and proclaimed that day was come.
The homing motors fled by ones and twos
through dreaming Chapel-street towards
town, and momentarily disturbed the sleep
of prostrate suburbanites with the hurry
music of their humming engines.
One sure sign of careless opulence, at
heiress Marie Hall’s coming-of-age dance,
was seen in the cloak room arrangements.
Real half-a-guinea-a-box powder, my dear!
—the kind that actually sticks to your nose,
and gives you the proper whited sepulchre
look. And it wasn’t doled out either, but
was available by the sackful, while a small
mountain of powder puffs rested softiy
alongside. High-class functions. from
Government House parties down, have de-
sisted altogether from the old, lavish way
of putting out powder; for the ordinary
laws of morality don't seem to apply to
nose-paint, any more than they do to um-
brellas, or books. The stuff is just lifted,
much as lead is lifted from the roofs of
unoccupied houses by persons of a much
lower caste. Why, even shoehorns and
buttonhooks are now chained to the waist of
the cloak-room dragon-in-waiting. Sweetly
wise girls who go forearmed to the soiree
with puffs and “shammies” secreted in their
stockings, almost fainted when they saw the
half-guinea powder sticks lying round. It
was like leaving a keg of money handy in
order that guests might not want for large
change.
The Alfred Hospital dance shovers have
secured everything in the way of vice-
regalitv for their whirl. A committee meet-
ing at'the Town Hall (in the Gaud Mayor-
ess’s inner shrine)'put things on a good
financial basis. Mrs. llennessy is president,
and the Gaud Mayor treasurer. Most of
Toorak’s illustrious women are actively con-
cerned. and if someone could only seize and
auction the ostrich feathers that wave opu-
lently at the committee meetings, the funds
would have a comfortable sum to go on
with.
The Law Students, a set of serious young
tome-chewers (Memo, for compositor: Not
tomb- chewers, please!), have invited their
friends to give their minds a rest and hop
with them, on Wednesday, July 2, at the St.
Kilda Town Hall. For that evening at
least, the study of the law will positively
cease in this vicinity
The South Yarra Skating Kink, which
draws the Best People from their Toorak
fastnesses, has seized on an American idea,
and is busily making ready for something
called “A Tombola Night.” Fancy dress and
fancy skating are on the programme; like-
wise plain dress and plain skating the sort
that so often ends with a dull bump.
“I say,” chirped a girl Climber to a
maiden who is one of the Clumb; "you
didn’t go to that invitation hunt club meet-
ing at So-and-So’s place in the country! You
were invited, of course! Why” (bubbling
with enthusiasm), “there was a special guest
train and dinner on board! Fancy you miss-
ing a thing like that!” “No, I didn't go,”
responded curtly the hunting girl who had
Clumb (or Clomb), “and I saved two pounds
by it! At that outing you paid your own
fare, and your own dinner, and your own
horse-box! I don’t mind telling you I could
not afford it.” Exit dazedly the Climber.
She knows now how deftly the socially ex-
perienced manage their hospitable splashes.
On Saturday the hosts of society rolled
along to the Princess’s matinee, which was
generally referred to briefly as “Lesly Mad-
den’s show.” Charitable amateurs (all
amateurs are charitable —they have to be)
presented, or shoved forward “His Excel-
lency the Governor.” The vice-regalities
attended in bulk—perhaps because of the
sonorously flattering title. G.-G. Denman
beamed from the Manager’s box, and the
Barttelot lady, at his distinguished elbow,
supported a purple hat and sable furs above
the plush edge. Husband Barttelot towered
in the shadowy background, and gazed
across at Governor Fuller, who was buried
in the depths of an astrachan coat —an
enormous thing that almost filled the box.
Another mountainous garment wore the
curls of local Help Crespin above the crater,
which was the collar. Mrs. Stuart Murray
and Mrs. George Chirnside commanded a
herd of loyal followers in a box lower down.
Mrs. Pursey Chirnside was penned with the
Fenners. John Pickwick Madden sat in the
orchestra stalls and beamed on leading
daughter Lesly. Sister Mrs. Jack Baillieu
was indicated by a white osprey. All the
social following of the rich Mrs. Sanderson
(starring for the afternoon) threw in their
six shillings apiece to watch the show ; while
hundreds of rich boys let the uproarious
football matches rip and paraded duti-
fullv and beautifully. The pitiful
bleat of lost society sheep was heard
thinly in the wilderness of the pit.
“Ai simply couldn’t get better seats, my
deah! Isn’t it a feahful crush ?” Rubbing
elbows with quite clean people they haven’t
been introduced to has a remarkable effect
on some folk —makes them gaze wildly
around, as if they expected to be mortally
bitten, and talk in strained, high voices
that carry for miles. There were two
Helps on the boards, one from each Govern-
ment House. Burnham, of the G.-G.’s
household, masqueraded as a governor.
“Tommy” Curwen, of the State staff, had to
perform the sort of duties on the stage that
he (presumably) has to struggle with in real
life —if Helps are in real life. As might
have been expected, he made an excellent
job of it. If there was a bell to pull, or
lights to switch, or cigarettes to pass, Cur-
wen attended with neatness and despatch.
(“Governors’ families waited on for orders.”)
Pretty little Maie Ryan had the ingenue
part. She aired some pretty dresses, and
spoke her lines in a thin, shrill voice. Lesly
Madden, very plump and sedate, was billed
as Stella de Gex —a French-English society
girl who has defied convention and arrived
on the music-hall stage. Lesly pranced
somewhat heavily through the (allegedly)
heart-breaking pirouettes of the part. The
amphitheatre held a number of cheerful bar-
barians, who had evidently been snared by an
outside placard announcing “Princess Mati-
nee To-Day.” Roars of savage laughter
greeted the unfortunate leading lady as she
made her first “entrance.” She appeared
in a ’rickshaw, and, in spite of weeks > of
careful practice, she tripped on something
while getting out, and tumbled in a tangle
of high heels, shoe-buckles and frills from
the Asiatic truck to the stage. It was dis-
tinctly hard luck, and I hope you will help
me "to suppress all mention of the
accident. The Madden gowns were
very fine. One, an evening dress, was
accompanied by an osprey that looked
like a burst water-main. Mrs. Sand-
erson showed off a rose chiffon costume that
made the house gasp with envy. Judge
Holroyd’s son wore the pink lapels of the
stage governor’s help. Fred and Norman
Steele, good-looking young men of these
parts who hold commissions m the loy
Scouts or something, tramped on under hel-
mets as mere supers. Amongst the vege-
tables that were handed up from the stage
boxes, some practical or embittered soul in-
cluded a hot-water bottle, unwrapped and
unashamed in its imitation leopard-skm
cover. I sincerely hope that whoever do-
nated it did not mean to insinuate that it
might be of use in mitigating the effects of
a frost. You know these dastardly things
are done sometimes. A w r ell-timed hot-water
bottle might easily assassinate a theatrical
reputation—stab it to the heart, so to speak.
Lesly fluttered to the footlights after the
last curtain, and thanked “Everybody with
coy blushes and a voice tremulous with ner-
vousness. . »
Edith Hope Campbell and Nina Bagot,
both of whom decorate the Best Set, got
snowed up for three whole days at Mount
Buffalo. When rescued, the tale they had
to tell was anything but woeful. During
their term of isolation they ski-ed and tobog-
ganed. A young Dublin cousin, who
was there for geological or other
scientific purposes, accompanied the girls
during part of the snow-country trip. The
young man has a delicious brogue which
would be a great handicap if he were trying
to disguise himself in a strange country.
Miss Hope Campbell, by the way, disclaims
the Scotch flavor of her last name, and has
no great love for the bagpipes. She belongs
to the only Irish branch of the mighty Camp-
bell clan, and so inclines her shell-pink ear
to the harp.
The youngest of the Pinschof daughters
(a great horse and dog enthusiast) has just
been on a riding tour with the Stuyvesants
and a few others. She wanted to show these
visitors some Victorian scener^; so they
trained it to Tallangatta, and from there
rode across the mountains, taking some
breathlessly steep tracks. They trotted
through Mansfield, and passed towns with
cheerful names like Tumbleup and Merrijig.
They eventually came down through Bright
to the plains on the other side of the range.
Then the ladies once more devoted some at-
tention to their ill-used complexions, and got
busy with pretty frocks for the rush of pri-
vate dances now following one another in
this frosty town.
A Toorak wedding has just been cele-
brated in whispers. The small bride w T as
booked for matrimony once before to another
man; but, when the wedding guests were
almost at the door, and the presents were
being arranged to the best advantage, the
ceremony was declared “off.” That sort of
experience is calculated to jar a person ; so
all hands united in keeping this venture just
as quiet as was possible.
Last week Oswald Hoad, son of the late
General Hoad, married pretty Sheila Mac-
Donald, one of the prettiest girls of the
Caulfield district. Neither of them wanted
fuss and trumpeting. (Many poor young
things have that laudable aspiration; but it
is often overruled on the grounds that Aunt
Mary or Uncle Ebenezer “would never for-
give us.” And, most of the time, the sup-
posedly relentless relatives are muttering
angrily that they don’t know what all the
fuss is about, but they suppose it means
another present, though Heaven knows liv-
ing is expensive enough without, etc., etc.)
The small wedding party—for this really
was a quiet wedding—having seen the job
well and truly done at St. Peter’s, packed
itself into a couple of motors and ran down
to the Oriental for some refreshments. The
hotel spread its bright blue pavement carpet
for the bride’s white shoes. She wore extra-
special tailorings of white cloth, and carried
a huge muff of chiffon and ermine, with
water lilies pinned on the outside. When
it was time to “go away,” she ran upstairs
and rapidly disguised herself by changing
shoes and stockings and enveloping the
dazzling whiteness of her suit in a dark
travelling cloak. Thus attired, she defied
anybody to recognise her as a newly-wed.
The Best twins endured their christening
the other day, and made a great vocal to-do
about it. Mrs. Isaacs, wife of the High
Court cjignitary, stood sponsor for at least
one "of tbe youngsters. The pretty young
mother was overwhelmed by the grand united
yell of the family; but a kindly matron
patted her encouragingly on the cheek and
cooed: “Tut, tut, my dear! You can’t ex-
pect them to have perfect manners at nine
months old.” A very wise and proper obser-
vation. I have made a careful note of it,
and, when I find the tea label I wrote it on,
I shall paste it up somewhere.
The Urquharts are taking Margaret Niall
abroad with them, thus leaving her widowed
dad to his own devices and without the
orders usually imposed or issued by a
motherless daughter. They all had a good-
bye dinner, at the Oriental, last Thursday,
when the table was crowded with violets
and champagne—also some food. By the
way, the Urquharts, who hail from the Wes-
ern District, now number Charlie Wallace
amongst the family, that well-known
yachtsman having married their eldest
daughter. The second girl, who has just
budded from school and flapperdom is
fine, fair sporting amazon. At the'dinner
she wore an enviable blue frock and th
wide-eyed wonder-look of expectant youth.
Victor Hood, who is putting in a few
weeks with the Fullers, has been on the sick
list for a day or two, and intends shortly
to go North and convalesce where the white
man is unable to live, or die, or drink, I for-
get which.
A spinster of (?) summers, who runs a
large motor round town, is slipping quietly
away to England to marry a wool man who
has won her heart, hand and fortune. The
lady is one of a large family ; but when Dad
died he left all his money to her. Disap-
pointed sisters, brothers, cousins and aunts
tried to upset the will; but the impartial
Law declared, after examination, that it
was too well rooted for any such operation
The favored one is throwing round handfuls
of largesse to the others before she departs.
Nellie Wilson, of the J.C.W. forces, is
off to the U.S. with a sprightly back-kick
A New York theatrical engagement looms
ahead <4 her.
Ruby Roberts, the girl billiardist who was
beaten—but not by a gigantic margin--b.v
Walter Lindrum, has hopes of persuading
her sex to take up the cue as a profession.
“It is rather monotonous always playing
with men,” says Ruby. “The only women
I have played with in public lately were
Madame Sterborn and Miss Collins.” She
has discovered that most girls are content
just to learn the game passably. During the
match at Alcock’s rooms, the fair cueist wore
a lilac gown decorated with a bunch of vio-
lets. The mixed audience followed her play
with some enthusiasm. It is over six years
since she took to billiards, at the instigation
of her uncle.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
A simply delirious week, my dear. Two days' rac-
ing! Clara Butt!! “Bella Donna’’!!! a Victoria
League reception !! ! ! and about 675 teas!! !! | p 0
you wonder that the local Culture is simply sizzling
in the community’s collective skull? The Asehes,
inimitable advertisers that they are, have decided to
give another “Kismet” Ball here. Wherever “Kis-
met” is played there is a “Kismet” Ball. This is an
imperishable rule framed by the business manager.
The cash will go to the Adelaide Children's Hospital.
The Exhibition Building will be the site of the revel.
The Victoria League entertained Mrs. Clara Butt,
Mr. Kennerley Rumford and the members of the
Julius Knight co. at the Cafe Rubeo on Tuesday.
There were about a hundred guests all told—a num-
ber of them, strange to say, men. The question is:
Did they leave their jobs and swallow inferior tea
and soggy cakes for the mere love of such viands, or
was the presence of alluring young theatrical females
to blame? I leave it to you with full confidence
that you will arrive at a correct decision. Mrs.
Way, who is just recovering from influenza, helped
to receive the guests. She was in blue taffeta, with a
black beplumed hat. Mrs. Richard Hawker discovered
to the community a graceful frock of white crepe d*
chine, with beautiful lace and a picturesque black
hat. Mrs. J. R. Baker habited herself in black
moire, and there were no audible complaints on the
subject. The stupendous Clara wore a chinchilla
coat and a beaming smile—a fine combination. Lady
Bosanquet was in blue, and her daughter in a shade
that hovered between cerise and strawberry. Mrs.
T. J. Fisher wore grey ninon with touches of silver.
The decorations were scarlet —geranium and salvia.
For further particulars apply by letter enclosing
stamped and addressed envelope.
The A.R.C. had fine weather for the Birthday
Cup meeting on both days. Battle Bridge and
Charlatan paid £l3 odd per medium of the tote,
and Broadsword, in the Cup, paid £39. A party
from Government House turned up both on the Satur-
day and the Monday, likewise the McFarlanes, Hawk-
ers and Bowmans. Mrs. A. E. Martin exhibited a
smart purple coat and skirt. Mrs. Julian Ayers,
Mrs. Frank Fisher and Mrs. Kyffin Thomas wore
more or less ordinary clothing; while Mrs. John
Corbin sported an exquisite sealskin coat —the sort
that the bellow-dramatic villainess dons in Act I,:
that is to say, before the comic friend of the hero
has had time to hit her on the head with a hatchet
and cause the garment to be distrained on by her
creditors. In addition to those mentioned above
there were several thousand mere human beings.
The Butt-Rumford concerts have drawn good
houses, and on Wednesday the Exhibition was
crowded. Mrs. Butt’s frock —of rose and silver and
fur —was a wonderful creation, and she fairly shone
with diamonds. The main features of the evening
were encores and burst gloves.
The Bach Society tackled “The Messiah” at its
last concert, aided and abetted —in fact, actually
directed—by Dr. Davies. Mr. and Mrs. Chappie.
Canon and Mrs. Girdlestow, Mr. W. Duffleld, the
Gaven Gardners, and Mr. and Mrs. Chapman all
looked as if they liked it.
Miss Ethel Haute, one of the Bakewell clan, got
back from England on Saturday.
The Henniker Heatons lunched with the Ways, at
the Queen Adelaide Club, on Saturday.
T Burgoyne, M.P., who had his 86th birthday on
Tuesday, has been in the State Legislature ever since
'B4. In his time he has been in a Ministry or two
—under John Cockburn and Vabien Louis Solomon,
but mostly he toils as a mere private member.
The S.P.C.A. has been giving its attention to sale-
yards, and doing it to some purpose. Owners or
broken-down horses have got into a way of sending
the poor brutes to a sale-yard, where they are
knocked down for 2s. 6d. or so to whoever wi
for them. That means they may either go as wo
for pigs or be worked. A day or so ago the a -
purchased three grandfather horses at a sale-yard tor
225. 6d. All were almost starved, and one in sue
evident pain that it had to be destroyed. The i Sock*
means to interfere with Private Enterprise sofara.
to force owners to treat their horses decently—or pm
them out of their misery when their work is do •
But why a horse which has toiled all its -en ~
life without a penny of wages shouldn t g 5 ,
age pension the same as a fairly well-paid human
am unable to say.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Patrick Jennings is the only N.S. W. Pre-
mier who can be compared in personal
political Miefliciency with the outgoing Pre-
mier McGowen. Both were good fellows,
both were honest and decently behaved, but
neither had any initiative. In the beginning
of his career McGowen found it difficult to
discriminate between Freetrade and Protec-
tion, but. on an emergency, he settled down
to one of them, though it is doubtful if he is
THE AFTERMATH.
The Political Cartoonist meets the Trust
Dragon, the Socialistic Tiger and a few other
friends in a nightmare.
yet quite sure which. He was delighted
when the Australian Parliament took over
the darned trouble. Still, he and Jennings
nominally headed Ministi-ies that did things
—even if they weren’t always wise things.
As a matter of fact, neither could be bothered
to repi'ess their colleagues. They preferred
to cultivate their own hobbies —the one
Sunday-school teaching, the other music. As
“Jim” flies to his Sunday-school. “Pat” fled
to his songs in A Flat.
The ignorance of the average Englishman
who writes about Australian politics is amaz-
ing. The latest to make an exhibition of
himself is Archibald Hurd, a journalist in
the employ of London Telegraph, and a
sort of a*nateur naval expert. Dr. Arthur,
M.L.A.. proudly published a letter from
Hurd last week, in which the whole of the
oft-exploded objections to Australian defence
were resuscitated. An extract: —
Australia offered a Dreadnought in 1909 to
strengthen the British arm where it must be bared
for the whole Empire; she is now taking the ship
away into the Pacific.
Of course Australia never ‘‘offered a Dread-
nought in 190!) to strengthen the British
arm.” A few Australians talked of some-
thing of the sort about this time. Ulti-
mately they tried to raise the money for
the vessel by private subscription, but failed.
The 1909 Dreadnought is not now being
taken away into the Pacific, for the simple
reason that it never came into being. The
Foster Frasers, Hurds, and so on have no
cause to feel lonely: In a recent issue of
London Daily Mail it is seriously stated
that Weinholt, Fisher’s late opponent in
Wide Bay (Q.) was a pugilist, who never
addressed meetings, but conducted his candi-
dature by knocking people out on the plat-
form. How is*it possible to take the people
who are responsible for such uninformed
nonsense seriously?

“Melb” : Ex-Agent-General Tav-
erner brought with him from Eng-
land biograph films of the railless
electi’ic trams at work in Rother-
ham (Eng.), and showed them at
the Star Theatre, in the presence
of the leading Melbourne experts
in Electi’ics and Trams, including
representatives of our monopolist
company. The railless trams work
from overhead lines, with a trolley
stick such as the rail-trams carry.
The question at once arises, “Why
a wire? Why not run with petrol,
like motor ’buses?” The answer
is that the overhead line gives a
far longer life and reduces ex-
penses. Also, the railless car sur-
mounts steep gradients that would
stop a petrol ’bus unless the en-
gines were altogether too powerful
for levels and ordinary gradients.
As compared with tramway trac-
tion, the cost of construction is
about one-fifth, and thei-e is no in-
terference with the ordinary traffic.
The trolley pole, much longer than
for a rail-car, sways, so that
the car need not keep under the
wires. It is easy to urge theoreti-
cal objections, but the Rotherham
cinematograph shows the system in
actual working, with the cars
dodging in and out, and going to the
side of the road to pick up or let
down passengers. Altogether it is
a revelation. The motor ’bus •will slay the
tram, and the railless will slay the motor
’bus. What after that?
Some day a smart organiser will work a
point on his political opponents, and thereby
stress a weak spot in the existing electoral
machinery. In Gippsland, for instance,
several presiding officers, clerks and scruti-
neers, were unable to reach their booths or
May 31, through superabundance of snow,
and the voting was postponed till the follow-
ing Saturday. By that date a swift agent,
working for Wise, could have rounded up
500 supporters who had not yet voted, and
l’ushed them to the booths in question, and
thus ousted Bennett. The performance would
be quite legal, as things are.
[F. C. Jones, Elgar-road, Burwood, Victoria.]
Senator Clemons l:as answered the polit-
ical riddle. There should, this Tas-
manian statesman thinks, be a rest from
party government —with this proviso . lhe
Liberal party should decide what measures
may not be passed. Incidentally, Clemons
thinks the Labor Government might be per-
mitted to remain in office during good be-
havior. also with this proviso, that party
Government being temporarily abolished as
aforesaid, the Liberal party should be the
judges of what constitutes good behavior.
On the same principle, washing-up being a
nuisance and tradesmen’s bills an infliction,
this writer is going to allow Senator
Clemons to abolish his meals —with the pro-
viso that this chronic eater reserves to him-
self the right to turn up at his old haunts
as usual and take food three times a day.
[S. H. Prior, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
No sooner do the Liberals get a majority
in the House of Reps, than dreadful things
happen. Two deck-hands fell off a Sydney
Harbor boat in one day. Rats were reported
to be increasing in Sydney. A schooner was
reported sunk off Coogee. Australia lost the
Davis Tennis Cup. The Turkish Vizier was
shot. The worst frosts on record happened
in S. Aus. Anthrax broke out at Moama
(N.S.W.). A child in Westralia died after
chewing a bougainvillea bloom, which bad
hitherto been regarded as quite a harmless
vegetable. Butter rose l|d. The creditors
in a Melbourne Jewish trader’s estate got a
shock —die offered them 19s. lid. in the f.
Cold feet were prevalent in Brisbane. _ A
man named Smith was fined ss. for being
drunk and disorderly. Heaven only knows
what these things have to do with the Lib-
eral party; but as they are the sort
of things that were fastened on to the Labor
party and the Labor party lost its majority,
the public is evidently impressed by them.
[S. H. Prior, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
Purple patch hewn from the letter of one
James Young, Liberal candidate for
Ryde (N.S.W.), to Sydney Telegraph
(i2/5/’l3) :
I much appreciate Mr. Wade’s kindly-meant advice
for me to keep out of politics. My own professional
advancement, no doubt, would be greater without
the political incubus of Parliamentary duties to re-
tard me. Still, as the electors of Ryde electorate
have services of value to them placed by me for their
acceptance or refusal at the ballot-box, I am pre-
pared to make the necessary private and professional
sacrifices, should the electors, as I have every reason
to hope and expect, return me to Parliament as their
representative by a large majority.
One of the drawbacks of Liberalism is its
habit of putting 59 words into a sentence.
If some one would present the party
with a pint of commas and a bag of full
stops he might start the Wade crowd on a
new career.
When the Liberals were fighting. the Re-
ferenda proposals, their stock arguments
against giving the Commonwealth power to
deal with trusts, etc., were that (1) there
weren’t any combines; (2) if there were
any they weren’t injurious; (3) even if
they existed and were injurious existing
legislation was quite strong enough to cope
with them. Now, though those yarns were
told less than a month ago, the Victorian
“Liberal’’ Government has already decided
t» introduce next session an anti-combine
law r , on the lines of Maoriland’s Commercial
Trusts Act, 1910, to prevent persons and
firms forming compacts tending to discrimi-
nate unfairly against persons or firms out-
side the compact, or to stop supplies to those
persons or firms —as the Melbourne Brick
Combine did to the Glen Iris Co. and other
intending customers recently, according to
the sworn evidence given to the Vic. Brick
Commission.
[F. C. Jones, Elgar-road, Burwood, Victoria.]

“F.C.,” from the Victorian side: —
There was an election the other day in my dis-
trict for a Legislative Councillor or Affluent Land-
lord. There hadn’t been a contest for years or cen-
turies, or some other old multitude of things of the
sort, and I wouldn’t have known there was one on
now but for one of the candidates condescending to
drop me a circular presenting his views and his por-
trait. His face was against him, and so was his
occupation—he was a grazier—but the other man
hadn’t troubled to notify me of his existence.
Neither of them had spoken within four miles of my
residence. In fact, I don't think the other man
spoke anywhere at all; he was the retiring fossil,
and his views could be assumed accord.nglv. He
called himself a “gentleman.” Painful though it
was, I was compelled to vote for the less reactionary
grazier. At all events, he had energy enough to
graze, which was in his favor. I was on the verge
of raving lunacy when he lost by 900 votes in a
poll of 8000, out of a total enrolment of 18,000.
You will observe the figures, and when you recollect
that there were contests in only five of the 17 elec-
torates, you will perceive what a live, up-to-date,
vigorous, representative institution this House of
Review is!
It is already hinted that two things which
almost alive two weeks ago iS
IVS.V. are practically dead again-.*)-,”
North Shore bridge and Minister GHfliiic'
wonderful scheme for raising the wind

TORY PRESS: " No, Mr. Cock! It's the dirty

If Victoria’s State Ministry could cook
half as well as it can write a menu, that
State’s Parliament might be worth keeping
in existence a w-hile longer. The bill of fare
for next session comprises no less than 51
items, chiefest of which are the Greater
Melb. Council, Price of Gas Regula-
tion, Workmen’s Compensation, Redistribu-
tion of Seats, Public Health. Hospitals and
Charities, Milk and Dairy Supervision, and
Crimes Act Amending Bills. It would take
John Weary two sessions to pass those few,
and then the Rich Landlords would put half
of them on the shelf for time to ripen—or
rot. But by starting with half a hundred
proposals, the Ministry can pass a dozen or
so trifling measures, and crow over the num-
ber of its achievements, though one Act to
constitute an effective Greater Melbourne
Council would be worth the whole boiling.
From The Bulletin’s Old Soldier:—
Archibald Hurd, of London, talked some
wild rot in his letter to Arthur, M.L.A. —I
mean the letter that the Sydney Telegraph
reprinted last week. Consider this: —
If Australia insists in attempting to “police the
Pacific” the British people “may respond by claim-
ing that ships paid for with money raised from the
people of the United Kingdom shall be kept in Euro-
pean waters to police European waters.”
It will be interesting to know how Britain's
food supply (a good deal of which, by the
way, comes from Australia) is to be pro-
tected if the British navy is kept in Euro-
pean waters? The food supply will be
Britain’s chief naval problem in its next
great war. Furthermore, it will be abso-
lutely absorbing to hear the remarks directed
by the British investor, who has many mil-
lions sterling sunk in this country, to the
Government, which by way of punishing
Australia for starting a navy of its own,
takes steps designed to place the country at
the mercy of an invader.
The present N.S.W. State Government
never does things quite as any other Govern-
ment would, and it is living up *:o its reputa-
tion. At time of writing McGowen has re-
signed half of his Premiership, or he luu
sent in half his resignation of the whole
Premiership, and the other half is to be sent
in a few weeks hence. So at present he is
half in office and half out. and if the door
slams he will probably be hit about the waist.
And Holman is half the new Premier and
half the old Attorney-General, so if the door
slams he will probably be smitten on the
ear. Holman, as the Premier who has partly
come in, is supposed to be forming a new
Cabinet, and McGowen, as the Premier who
has only partly gone out. hasn't yet given up
his hold on the old Cabinet. McGowen. it is
understood, isn’t to do any more work m his
old position, but he wants to hang on to
the hollow shell of the dignity till a certain
date, when he is booked to lav the founda-
tion stone of a fish-stall or something of tne
sort in his own constituency of Kedtern.
The idea is that that will be his last public
appearance as head of the Ministry. fnc
dentally he has half decided to accept a subor-
dinate portfolio under Holman. as .j ur j* r
of Victoria, when he abdicated, did under
Watt. Also, he has half decided not to. And
if any calamitous circumstance arises ne
tween the first half of McGowen s resig
tion and the second half of Holman > a
sion the Governor will have to decide ' ,
two out of the four halves to send for- an
he will probably have half a mind to
up his own job in despair.
FORBIDDEN GROUND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In alleging a breach of municipal by-laws, which
tell you you mustn’t walk on the grass, a poetic
Balmain policeman said that he saw two youths “on
the forbidden ground, talking to two girls.”
Which makes the bard break into reminiscent
rhyme:—
When the thin moon no longer hung
Her lamp, hut throbbing planets swung
Gold censers in the heavens round,
You turned, with eyes so true and young,
And faltering tongue,
To whisper, “ 'Tis forbidden ground.”
But silence followed on vour sigh ;
We two alone beneath the sky,
Where no voice fell, nor any s und,
We stood and watched the stars swing by,
Just you* and I,
My dear, upon forbidden ground.
They say, that on the garden sward,
Where Eve and Adam praised the Lord,
One tree, one plot of grass was found,
Where One from out the hosts adored,
With flaming sword,
Kept guard on the forbidden ground.
No flaming sword its blinding light
Swept balefully across our sight,
There stood no angel to astound ;
Y T et trembling were your lips, and white
Your face that night,
Because it was forbidden ground.
We would have dared the sword of flame,
We would have made a jest of fame
While in the blaze we leapt and drowned.
But aren't you glad that all the same
No bobby came
To us, in the forbidden ground.
N.S. W
A. M.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
A little brother of Jack Johnson has made
a somewhat sudden appearance in the ring, having
just sailed to fill an international engagement. He
is to box a big German pug in Berlin. Andreas
Johnson is the name of the new colored hope. He
is not as big as Jack; in fact, American critics
declare that, as a fighter, he is not ace high. But,
then, American critics are not feeling proud of
the Johnson family just now.
The smashing of Joe Russell, of Brisbane,
by Herbert McCoy, at Sydney Stadium on Saturday
night, was one of the swiftest fights seen in that en-
closure as far as speed is concerned. It is certain
that the purse he bagged is about the most quickly
earned money McCoy ever made. A minute and a
half subsequent to shaking hands with his opponent
McCoy had landed the glory and the profits in his
dressing-room, so to speak. When they met McCoy
sent two towards Russell’s head, but Russell inclined
his head, so to speak, and as the wallops flew past
he placed right and left on McCoy’s body. McCoy
telegraphed a left wallop, but instead crossed the
right and dazed Russell badly. The Queenslander
rushed into a clinch and as he came in was chipped
on the jaw. Then he gripped. but McCoy threw him
off like St George repudiating the dragon, and with
an uppercut sent hint down and out. It was over
two minutes before Russell got a proper hang of
what had happened. The real contest of the evening
was the struggle of the people who had seen the
fight to get out through the raging crowds of late
comers who were charging in full blast to see it.
That was a great pushing match.
On Saturday last the Rugby League re-
sumed its competition on Sydney’s sodden grounds,
where brilliancy was impossible, and strenuousness
necessary. Eastern Suburbs was the sole remaining
undefeated team, and South dreamed golden dreams,
even though Messenger himself led the champion
team. But the dreams did not materialise. On a
bog tactics are hard to carry out. It was a ground
on which the battle was to the weighty, and to
those with large feet, and East Sydney had the
surplus poundage and the abundant feet. It
started to use them from the first, and by sheer
bullocking heaved South back on to its line, where it
was kept busy pulling down attempts to get over.
South had the misfortune to lose its captain early
in the game, and he happens to be one of the
captains that do matter. He is a tactician as well
as a 'bailer, and a general as well as a booter; and
without him to direct its efforts, South felt that it
had no great hope. And it hadn’t. It was defeated,
by 12 points to nil. The play didn’t look so uneven
in the field: it was the heavy artillery of the
younger Messenger's boot that made the difference.
At half-time East lead by four to nil, thanks to
a couple of penaltv goals kicked by the gentleman
aforementioned. East started to press from the
moment of resumption, and though South fended
well, it was evident that East was the attacking
party. The attack worked closer and closer and
when within striking distance there was a sudden,
savage combined rush and the ball was bullocked
over the line. This time IV. Messenger took the
kick and added the extras. South attempted to re-
trieve this disaster with what was really the charge
of the day, but the temporary campaign came to
grief. All the same, South pressed hard. It kicked
at the door but could not burst it open. Then
South lost another man injured, and the team, out-
weighted and manned down, could not stand the
charge, which was carried home by “Dally” Mes-
senger. The kick failed, but South was beaten by
12 points to nil.
* * * *
Newtown continued the triumphant
career that commenced when it lowered South Syd-
ney’s colors for the first rime. This time it was
Glebe’s turn to provide the chopping block and it
did it with bad grace, struggling like the Devil. Had
its struggles been less devilish and more scientific
it would probably have done better. The ball was
hard to handle, but Newtown managed to keep its
grip of it sufficiently to put up a good exhibition
ot combined play. Glebe dropped it all over the
place; and the result was victory for Newtown by
seven points to three. Annandale put up its usual
bright game against Western Suburbs. This plucky
team, the baby of the crowd, always plays a good
sterling game in spite of the persistent bad luck
that has brooded over its efforts. West Sydney
won by 11 to six points. Balmain’s struggle with
North Sydney was splashed out on the mud flat
known as Birehgrove Ova 1. Both teams were sports
and hauled one another out impartially when bogged.
Each declined to cross the line, and each kicked
two penalty goals. They made a drawn game with
four points each. So the result is that East Sydney,
the only unbeaten team of the bunch, is far ahead
in the competition with 12 points to the eight of
South Sydney and Glebe. Newtown is creeping up
with seven and North Sydney has six. It is. not
necessary to search below the six level. Any mining
man will tell you that deep sinking seldom pays.
If the League players had a bad time the
Union men had a worse, on account of having to
play on inferior grounds. The result was that the
games were mostly scrambles and insane mixtures.
Tactics and passing charges were just about im-
possible. The chief contest of the afternoon was
that between East Sydney and Newtown. As a
rule Newtown walks over East Sydney under Union
rules; but on this day the advantage was with
the heavier Eastern team, wffiich won out by 10
points to three. Balmain found itself stamped flat
by University to the tune of 24 points to three, a
truly awful event under Union rules. Glebe had a
great struggle at Manly, where it managed to climb
over the Village team by six to nil. South Sydney’s
defeat of St. George was a nice, quietly decisive
victory by 10 to six; and North Sydney just man-
aged to upset West Sydney by three points to nil.
As in the League Competition, East Sydney is the
only unbeaten club, and heads the Union list with
14 points. Newtown and North Sydney have 10
each. University is holding its aching head with
only eight, and so is South Sydney, while Glebe has
but six. Glebe’s grief is tempered not by the re-
flection that there are four clubs below' it with less.
It isn’t w'orth their while to hold their aching heads.
Their heads aren’t worth holding.
In the Australian Rules division North
Sydney got Paddington’s head under the slush and
swam to the surface of things with 62 points to Pad-
dington’s 47. It was an interesting struggle, as.both
teams were commanded by tacticians, but the North
Sydney G.O.C. proved to have the better ideas of the
tw'o. At Hampden Oval, which, contrary to general
ideas, was in better condition than most of the battle
grounds, owing to the fall of the country and the
artificial drainage, South Sydney went for Sydney
District with bald-headed fury and its mouth open,
and its tail up, and its false teeth in its pocket for
fear of accidents. But the Southern organisation
w-as far from perfect, and the attack failed for want
of systematic support. Sydney repulsed it, and then
let loose its own system and overwhelmed
the wild individualists from the South by 49 to 18.
East Sydney had dead and buried luck. It w r as to
meet New 7 town at the big new' ground at Mascot.
New'town got there early, and so did a portion of
the Eastern team. The rest were held up for an hour
by a derailed tram, and the fragment on the ground
refused to face the goal-hungry-looking Newtown
crowd, which hung out its tongue as it gazed raven-
ously on the Eastern fragment. The umpire bounced
the ball, Newtown kicked a goal, and the game was
won, six to nil.
“BOX ON"—“BANG”—“OUT."
The fight that lasted 35 seconds at Sydney
Stadium. Herb McCoy (Vic.) settles Joe
Russell (Q.).
The Soccer crowd in Sydney went slow
on Saturday. Most of the grounds on which its
matches are held were in a state of quagmire, said
by some authorities to be liable to swallow up a
whole team and yell for more. So only two matches
of the Rawson Cup Competition were played off,
between Sydney and Granville and Newtown and
Canterbury. The first match took place at Epping,
and was a stubborn contest with plenty of excite-
ment, out of which Sydney climbed laboriously to
the position of top dog with five goals to three.
Newtown journeyed out to Sydenham, where it
found Canterbury awaiting it in battle array and
its feet in the water, and fell upon it, got it down,
and drowned it by seven goals to one. The body has
not yet been recovered.
* * * *
Melbourne’s League hoofball last week
was decisive, in several ways. Fresh from its vic-
tory over South, ’Roy went across to Collingwood
on King's Birthday and returned with the scalps of
the hitherto undefeated Magpies, scoring a 10-point
victory; and on Saturday it took Carlton by the
forelock and handed it the worst walloping of the
lot, 99 points to 64 being the measure thereof.
Simultaneously, South avenged itself on ’Wood for
its defeat by 'Roy. Taking full charge in the last
quarter, it piled on four superfluous goals, or there-
abouts—South 10 goals 15 behinds, to ’Wood’s 7
goals 9 behinds. South's marking and kicking are
faulty, but the team loves itself like a brother, and
works together in unity—at present. St. Kilda put
up a mighty scrap with Geelong, and by bogging in
managed to win a fair game by seven points—7B to
Geelong's 71. Essendon got a grip on Melbourne, and
piled up 71 to 30; but as ’Don has to win every
game left in order to reach the finals, it will pro-
bably burst before the effort is completed. Rich-
mond notched 83 points to the Students’ 40, but
that match hardly mattered. The Students seem to
suffer from the unwholesome tripe served to them
by certain press barrackers—that they are a great
team afflicted with bad luck. With the first half
of the round of home-and-home matches now com-
pleted, ’Rov, South, 'Wood and Carlton stand on the
top rungs.' with half a game between each. St.
Kilda, a game and a half worse off than Carlton,
still has a chance. The rest are practically outed.
The position in the Ass. game is a shade more
open, inasmuch as six clubs still have chances.
'Scray is on top. North and P'ran stand each half
a game lower. ’Don has one win less; then Bruns-
wick and ’Town follow with half a game to debit.
'Cote, Brighton, Port and Melbourne City are Mud.
On Saturday ’Scray pleased its supporters by keep-
ing within cooee of P’ran throughout the argument,
and then galloping away in the last term. It fin-
ished with 61 points to 47. The Brunswick Brickies
surprised their barrackers by keeping the whip hand
of Essendon all the afternoon, 55 to 40. Wick has
evidently been holding deep converse with itself these
last few weeks, and the result now shows up.
North made an unsanctified show of Port, 95 to 2b.
’Cote spilled the Citizens in the soup once more 60
to 35; and Town utterly drowned Brighton—9l to
21. ,
During the week the various tribunals sharp-
ened their axes and got busy. For mistaking the hoof-
ball field for the boxing 'ring, Carlton’s captain was
passed out till Aug. 1, and a North Melbourne
player for the rest of the season. But official leni-
ency has previously been so marked that the law
has at last begun to speak. Last week its remarks
ran thus: £3 and costs to a Port player for assault;
£2 and costs to a ’Town youth for insulting an
umpire: £5 and costs to a Melbourne player for
assaulting a Carlton bogger-in. Thus is the Ul-
regulated youth discouraged and the revenue in-
creased, which is always a desirable circumstance.
* * * *
The arrival of Maoriland sportsman Dr.
Thacker in Sydnev in charge of the Rugby League
footballers has had an electrifying effect on Richard
Arnst. The doctor has been one of Arnst’s financiers
throughout his sculling career, and as a result of h.s
call to Sydnev, Richard is on the war-trail once
more. The winner of the Barry-Pearce match has
already been challenged, and Jim Paddon has been
marked for slaughter, provided satisfactory terms
can be arranged. The doctor is firm on the proviso
that Richard must place himself unreservedly in the
hands of a competent mentor, and train like the
Devil; a thing he hates even as Satan is understood
to hate a certain fluid found about churches.
Sydney lacrosse put on a fresh garment
last Saturday. Sydney and Eastern Suburbs, both
of which had wallowed only in victory' hitherto,
met. The softness of the underfootage hampered
the freedom of the play and bogged the chances of
Sydney, the members of which seemed more anxious
not to spoil their beautiful clothes than to win the
game. So the mudlarks of Eastern Suburbs overran
them and shed their collars in the mire by eight
goals to nil. Burwood surprised Birehgrove, and
outed it bv three to two, and Mosman bashed Manly
by eight to nil, a favorite score on Saturday. Peter-
sham scoffed at University by five to nil. So the
first section of the competition ends with Eastern
Suburbs undefeated, well in advance of everything.
The nearest are Sydney, Petersham and Birehgrove
—the rest nowhere.
* * * *
Lane Cove, hitherto undefeated in the
baseball competition, fell over its own feet, or some-
thing on Saturday. It struck Waverley, which had
already suffered defeat three times, and could do
no better than make a draw of it with three runs.
Petersham and University produced the same sort
of animal with four runs apiece. Mosman gave
Leichhardt food for remorse in the shape of a score
of nine runs to three. And Leichhardt was premier
club last year. Paddington and Burwood met at
Rydalmere, but as it would have been a swimming
contest, the teams stay'ed in the pavilion and told
stories about dogs. In the competition Lane Cove
leads with 11 points. Mosman looks over its shoulder
with 10. Petersham and Leichhardt reasonably
handy with seven each. The rest is mere tail.
* * * *
C.R.L.H., the man who started the
rescue, writes: “Concerning the capsize of the 14-
footer Dart in Sydney Harbor Sunday week, I hap-
pen to be the bloque fortunate enough to note the
capsize. I also admit to being mad enough to have
been fishing in the rain without getting a bite. In
last Bulletin the two men in the motor-boat were
referred to in uncomplimentary terms. I think they
should be poleaxed. The motor-boat was painted
white, was about 25ft. long, and apparently name-
less, as well it might be. When I told the men in
it of the capsize they mumbled that their engine
was not equal to the strain. So we went out in the
skiff. When we had grot two of the men from the
wreck the motor-boat came tearing along, and we
indicated to the occupants that they had better take
off the remaining and heaviest of the crew, as our
skiff had shipped a lot of water. The skunks merely
laughed and kept on the way towards Manly. I
think this sort of thing should be punishable; but
as it apparently is not, I hereby pronounce on those
motor-boat men the fishermen’s curse, “May their
gills turn green and their dying bed be made of a
hundred-thousand up-turned fish-hooks.”

• * * *
Fred Merton has won a race in Adelaide
at last, and his bad luck had been so notorious that
there was much cheering In 1912. visiting Adelaide
for the Cup and Birthday meetings, he got second
money five times. This year, Penza was second three
times at Morphettville, and once more on the Old
Course. Merton’s horses ran third in the Adelaide
Cup both years, and this year Aurifer was third in
both Cups. So a win outright in the Alderman Cup
(worth £440) was about “up to” both horse and
owner.
* * * *
The V.R.C. Committee was lately asked
to decide a novel question. At Kyneton on March
17, Ma Gosse had a walk-over in the Pastoria Handi-
cap. With a 71b. penalty she came out for, and
won. the next race, the Drummond Welter, starting
at 10 to 1 against Now, Ma Gosse had been backed
for that meeting “first start,” and there was argu-
ment as to whether Abraham Shortodds should pay
out 10 to 1 or nothing. The club committee put it
that, had Ma Gosse lost the second race, the
backers, in the light of the 71b. penalty, would in-
fallibly have denied their intention to back her for
that race, wherefore her first start was the “walk-
over.” Verdict accordingly, money to be returned.

N.S.W. Novice cross-country champion-
ship at Sydney on Saturday was over roadways and
sandhills, through marsh and mud—a most horrible
business that could have been more pleasantly H
on horseback save for the discomfort to the' hrm”*
The winner won fame and praise; but the other an
starters deserve a halo for the weariness of fle s
which they endured in pursuit of the bubble
transient glory. It was an easy win for Ken Fnsto
who finished 28sec. ahead of A. C. Elliot, strueirW
and ploughing through the slush and soil in
30sec. The “teams” championship was a eakpuJi!;
for East Sydney with 16 points against Hornshv
Church's 39 and Marrickville’s 79. Ken Foster th
winner, is a lad of 16, and belongs to a tribe which
has made the pace in novice cross-countrv event,
C. C. Foster won in 1911, Ken Foster ran 16th in
1911, fourth in 1912 and landed the millions n
1913. Sure and not too slow, considering what a
stripling he is.
The Continental Road Race from Goul-
burn to Ashfield (131 miles) is to be run on Mon
day, 23rd inst. The co. has made all arrangements
to supply the cyclists with proper food during the
race, and heads the prize list with open orders for
20 guineas. There are 11 other prizes of various
sorts. The event is an amateur one, and is one of
the events which will serve to indicate a retirement,,,
tive for the next Olympiad.
“K.’s” post-mortem: The Australian
tennis trio had bad enough luck to strike America in
the first preliminary round for the Davis Cup, but thev
had worse luck in permitting A. B. Jones to catch
a severe cold a week before the match. If there'*
not already a law making ill-timed colds illegal
there ought to be. That cold meant a lot—so much
that the Australians were able to lay their hand*
on only one rubber (the doubles) to America’s four
Doust (captain) selected himself and Rice for the
singles, and Jones and himself for the doubles, re-
serving the right to change his mind at the eleventh
hour and play Jones in the singles, if the cold per
m tted. Evidently it didn’t. It’s a pitv, for A.B
is, without a doubt, Rice’s superior in both singles
and doubles, and the fact that the cable regards him
as “easily the star” in the “most sensational tennis
ever seen in America” shows that he was in good
nick. However, it is doubtful whether he could hav«
won both singles, and nothing less would have pulled
Australia’s chariot out of the fire, or dragged the
tennis ashes from the mud. U.S.A. relied on
McLoughlin and Williams for the singles and
Hackett and McLoughlin for the doubles; and its
confidence wasn’t misplaced, for McLoughlin won
both his singles in three straight sets, and Williams
beat Doust three sets to one, and Rice three to
two. The Rice-Williams rubber, which couldn’t have
any effect on the result of the tie, Australia being
already hopelessly out of it, was the only decent
show the Australians made in the singles,' and the
final figures were I—6, 4 —6, 6—l, 9— 7', 6—2 in
favor of the American. The doubles went to Aus-
tralia, 2—6, 6—2, 5 —7, 6—2, 9—7, making 52
games in all. McLoughlin, in • particular, seems to
have provided the Australians with more than thev
could chew, his service and smash being particularly
tough morsels; and as Williams also owns a well-
lubricated smash which worried Doust and co.,
therein lies the cause of the defeat.
As to the other ties, the Germans came out of the
bag and beat the French badly, and are a day’s
march nearer the Cup. The Canadian-African match
has been held up for a while. The Australian trio
now move on to England in search of scalps, and
anyone who mentions Davis Cup or cough mixture tn
them during the next few months will be regarded
as unfriendly.
* * * *
The newest find in Maoriland sport is a
North Canterbury giant, alleged to be the founda-
tion of a world top-notcher. He is 19 years of age.
kicks the beam at 12st. 101 b. ; measures 39in. round
the chest, and is an all-round sport. The only thing
known against him is that he has an unbeaten
record as a pigeon murderer; but being young, he
may live that down.
LINES TO A DEAD NIGGER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Apparently Johnson is finished, down and out. The
last batch of Yankee papers to hand never mention
his name once.—B. June 5/’IS.
Brudder, you are dead and done for!
Though you had a fairish run for
Your spondulicks, which you won for
Battering the white pugs, who
Metaphoric’ly you've “eaten” —
(Prowess even death should sweeten !),
Now, at length, by whites you’re beaten—
Mutton’s not so dead as you !
Life to you was paragraphic,
Now no more reporters traffic
In your doings—your seraphic,
Gold-stopped smile is valueless!
Dead men tell no tales w'orth reading;
Brudder, not a soul is heeding
You henceforth. You’re dead and needing
No more puffing in the press.
Brudder, now you’re gone, no glory
Hangs around your dusky story:
V And so long have you been mori-
Bund, your end no one surprised.
Moral—which can not be doubted —-
Take this from the world you flouted:
E’en a- champion boxer's “outed,”
When he’s no more advertised!
VICTORIA.
EARDLEY TURNER.

“Billy T.” : The lady picapocket is pros-
pering in Melbourne, and John Hop is mak-
ing poor progress with her. She is often of
smart rig, and treats her victim with such
subtle arts that, after it is all over, the poor
fellow can scarcely believe he has been
touched. It is like having one’s purse pulled
with gas. The other night a nice little
thing, not more than fiye-foot-one, with brown
hair and eyes, fashionably coated and skirted,
asked respectful questions of a tall gentle-
man in dark grey near the corner of Eliza-
beth and Little Collins streets. It seemed
that she had mislaid her address, or it may
have been her religion, and wanted advice
and guidance from a trustworthy person with
kind eyes. Mutual exchanges had only just
begun, when a third party intervened, with
some show of excitement, and a clatter of loud
remarks. “I seen yeh!” he said. “I seen
yeh!” and seized the lady rudely. The lady
beheld him with lofty scorn and calm dis-
gust and asked him how he dared. The tall
gent, in dark grey was a cavalier quick in
action. Ilis neat left caught the intruder
on the point, and the intruder crumpled up
on the roadside. “Dear me, this is very
dreadful!’’ wailed the nice little thing. “I
really must not be mixed up in a street
brawl.” She left. It took the tall gent,
two or three minutes to restore the man
who was down and out, and another five
minutes to get an explanation of his shame-
ful treatment of the lady. “It wasn’t no
business of mine,” said the man with the
sore jaw. “I saw she was goin’ through you,
that’s all.” Investigation demonstrated to
the tall gentleman’s satisfaction that he had
been gone through. He had only seven shil-
lings and a silver matchbox left, and it took
more than that to repair the sore jaw.

THE WINTER WEATHER IS LOVELY
AT MT. VICTORIA.
THERE, 3424 ft. above the sea, you can really enjoy living. Forget that
there ever was such a place as an Office; cast aside all worries and live
the life of the Mountains.
Even if only for a week-end, you will notice a wonderful change. The
pure, crisp air reinvigorates you, and prepares you for business the following week.
THE IMPERIAL HOTEL
the leading Hotel at Mt. Victoria —offers you unequalled advantages,
;tay there will greatly increase the pleasure of your visit.
and
The Hotel is magnificently furnished throughout, is electrically
lighted, and has its own sanitary system. Also a modern Motor
Garage. Service and Cuisine equal to the best City Hotels.
a
TARIFF: £3 3s. per Week; 10s. per Day.
Special Personally Conducted Tour to Jenolan
Caves Over Prince of Wales Birthday.
This will afford an unequalled opportunity of viewing the famed
jenolan Caves at small expense.
You travel First-Class by Rail, and from Mt. Victoria or
Katoomba to the Caves by the luxurious Itala Cars. You have a day
and a half at the Caves, and stay at the Caves House. In short you
have every comfort and convenience throughout the trip.

Rickets
not only affects the bones,
it renders the child liable
to croup, bronchitis, nerve
troubles and acid indiges-

ESTABLISHED 30
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. ho
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Nanilla: We know that sort of English-
man by heart. He is not a remittance-man for the
reason only that he has no friends or relations who
are able or willing to send him remittances. And
so he works. But being a congenital ass he does
no particular good for himself ; and being a dreary
bore he is avoided socially with a good deal of zest;
and that embitters him with the country and with
its inhabitants. Take the advice of our Religious
Editor —counsel which is based on a lengthy experi-
ence of this sort of cit ; zen—and edge off your
British acquaintance before he begins to borrow
money from y0u.... Scar: The man who thinks that
“the present wild and unsettled state of the North-
ern parts of the continent constitute their best safe-
guard” can’t be argued with here. He had better
hunt up the ghost of the last Tasmanian black
and ask him what he thinks of the proposition. His
land was wild and unsettled enough for anything....
F. : You’ll have to give us some idea of the
date.... Dumper: Yes, and if the same sub. had got
hold of “Hamer’s” yarn it would have gone where
yours went, he having previously handled about 50
variants of it and traced the original to America. . . .
Jeddough: Too young and green.... Billy Bucket:
No, “Wun Hop” does neither —he merely enjoys the
cartoonist’s privilege of pulling your leg. And it
looks as though the job was easy. .. .B R.: The Bul-
letin can’t undertake to return the MS. of unused
paragraphs Julia L. (Q.) : It is not Bulletin stuff,
but the market for it is comfortably steady. Think
you should be able to place other stories with the
same paper —and other weeklies of the family type.
The official prophet is at present insolvent through
backing his own fancies at the races; consequently,
we are unable to sav whether you will ever do better
work R.M.V.: It is a rather untidy avalanche.
Cannot discover anything of value to us. Try else-
where, and next time either hire a typewriter, or en-
slave a young person who writes a legible fist. . . .
H.N.: Hilda, our dear, you have versified one of the
Past Grand Chestnuts —one of the dear old ones that
linger fondly in the memory and are never, never
forgotten, the love verses are very nice in a pretty,
imitative way. Thanks for neat manuscript
D.L.T.L.: Bit like diluted Jacobs, isn’t it?
Bellerive: Herewith the second instalment of your
serial, “Dosser’s Paradise.” Synopsis of previous
chapter: Parson Jim, who is looked up to by all the
other dossers, is confronted by the indignant brother
of the lady he is supposed to have ruined. It will
be remembered that, as the last chapter closed. Par
son Jim, who was being made to feel thoroughly un-
comfortable, emitted a cry of terror and endeavored
to escape. Chapter 2 —When his enemy held him with
an iron grip and resumed when you were a gay bird
Don you ruined my unfortunate sister’s Life under
the pretense of marriage Left a mother without the
honor of the Name of wife she became demented And
poisoned herself with arsenic in the Bloom of her
womanhood and I pledged an oath oyer her grave to
wreak vengeance upon the man who’d betrayed Her
your the man and by the Saints above you’ll pay
the forfeit Dennis appeal’ed the victim I’m waning
fast from want and misery Give me time to repent
for my sin To my Maker alas his pathetic anpeal
Was answered with a fiendish laugh A knife flashed
o’er his wretched form And he fell crone to the
mouldy floor Stabbed to the core of the pluck I’ve
settled him muttered the assassin When the door
was thrown open And he was surrounded by the
paupers Whom seized him like wolves and Chimed
Murder in Dosser’s paradise I done him in cried the
captive Hand me over to Justice I’ve revenged My
sister’s fate and the law can Swing me. (THE
END.) .K.P.: “The Average” was posted back to
you on 10/6/’l3. It was sent home because it was
below its name. .. . Jimipy B. : “Boys" just about
1800 words too long. It wouldn’t matter much in a
novel, but it’s a fairly serious fault in an 1800-word
story. T.8.D.: No value here. .. .William O’C.:
It may seem ungrateful of us, but we’re not going
to send you anvthing for your illuminated address
to Little Lord Fauntleroy. As you are a new arrival
on the Inky Way, we will not charge you anything
for reading it. That’s the best we can do
Trier: Your humor reminds us somewhat of heavy
weight-lifting turns we have endured at music halls,
having all the time a miserable suspicion that the
weights were less than they were supposed to be.
and knowing quite well that it didn’t matter, any-
how, weight-lifting being a hopelessly dull stunt or
job, besides being out of place in the modern world
of power. .. .S.S.H.: Sorry “Reply” unsuitable....
G. : Sentiment is very pleasant, but the verses
require the cosy firelit setting of a children’s page,
with large-hearted Aunt Martha, as stipendiary
female relative to the whole wide world, doing her
knitting under the heading, and the salaried cat pur-
ring on the top margin of a' cocoa advertisement....
Ess K.: After Bret Harte a long way. The trouble
about big men’s footprints is that relatively they
are bottomless pits to very small followers. Alwavs
better to try and make your own track, and thereby
ayoid the comparison that is odious, if not abso-
lutely fatal. (P.S. —Just struck your second contri-
bution. It moves us to say that it would be better
if you could stop. Do vou think you could ston if
you tried?) Miggles: Not Bulletin stuff.
Whether it is any other paper’s stuff rests with that
paper and with the cloyen pen of the dey'l-
ish sub-editor.... Offsider: Sorry. Not worth
print... . W.8.J.: Well-meaning and monotonous....
W.A.S.: Find space for them? We will find illimi-
table space for them ! At the verv first opportunity,
they shall be hitched to a balloon and sent up
where there is no niggling sub-editor to trouble. . . .
Norman: Oh. it’s spontaneous enough. We can see
quite plainly that it is one of those poems that just
burst out and let it go at that. .. .M.A.G. :
No, we don’t like, and you won't tell
us all about it—at least not while we
have the strength to resist.... S.K.: Uninteresting
moan. Try a shriek... .8.Y.: Sorry, no value to us.
You’re a beginner, aren’t you ?. .. . Darlarmi: Verv
true and very du11... .H.W.D.: A little more skill
required.
TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)
CORRESPONDENTS.
E. Schneider: Now. did you expect that this paper,
or any paper, would buy the thing? Wil C.:
Quite useless. To begin with, draw your pictures
about ten times the size in order to allow for re-
duction. and don’t draw them on a flabby paper sur-
face. Also, don’t double the paper up, and don’t
employ the cheap device by which words are shown
issuing from the mouths of the various characters.
Furthermore, draw better, and don’t use faint pencil
scratches. Lastly, shinine brother, when you send a
peremptory demand for the return of your hopeless
communication, enclose a stamp. In conclusion, and
finally and by way of P.S . the thing was torn in
three pieces and dropped into our old and valued
friend, the basket K.M.L.: “Please criticise.”
Well, it isn’t worth a solitary old whoop in Galilee.
....Baal: If your characters looked a little more
human, and weren’t so short in the legs and so in-
sane about the heads and stomachs and whiskers
and hands and clothes, and the beer-glasses were
more like the genuine articles, and there was some
nrobability and perspective and composition about
the picture it would be pretty g00d... .G.P.G.: A
horrible collection, and the picture of the hen-em-
bezzler is the worst.
Points from a recent Sydney lectnre on
“The iS-uth about Germany"’:—
The German does not ask why he should be a sol-
dier ; it is natural to him. He is a soldier because
he is a German. Some who steal away to oversea
countries awaken the idea that military service is
unpopular at home. On the same ground we mieht
conclude that it is unpopular to earn an honest live-
lihood because occasionally someone disappears with
other people’s money.
The Germans love Peace; they love it so much
that they want to make sure of it; therefore their
efficient army and navy.

rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them—the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. 'They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.

“Bookware” on Austin’s successor (if
any) :
Kipling has more genius than any other Laureate
candidate, but really he is not a poet. Macaulay
admired Virgil perhaps more than any other writer,
yet doubted whether Virgil was a poet—regarded him
more as the unequalled prosateur. I think that will
be the verdict on Kipling. There is as much poetry
in the prose of Ruskin, and more in the prose of De
Quincey. For poets, go to Shelley and lveats, with
Byron on a lower plane, as he himself recognised, like
Schiller quailing before Goethe. Noyes, author of
the worthy Drake epic, is in the Virgil class. \\ at-
son is the best intrinsic poet going "for the Laureate-
ship, and Masefield the next best. Vi atson writes
little, while Masefield is coming on. I scratch New-
holt and Bridges. There is evidently competition
for the job. All because of thej American market.
The Laureate-ship goes for little in England, but it
has a large money value in the United States.
“Addleliead” : “Nemo” (B. ,22/5/ 13) is
wrong in alleging that Arclibishop O Iteily,
of Adelaide, has only one suffragan, the
Bishop of Port Augusta. The “province” of
Adelaide includes Westralia and the North-
ern Territory—the largest, territorially, in
Australasia. In Westralia alone are
three suffragan bishops—their lordships of
Perth, Geraldton and New Norcia. The
Territory pulls through at present with an
“administrator apostolic.” By the way, it
isn’t necessary for an archbish. to have any
suffragan bishops. The arch, of Hobart has
none. Archbishop OTleily, by the way, was
parish priest of Fremantle (W.A.) when the
rescue of the six Irish patriots by Capt.
Anthony was effected. We’ve heard a lot
about it lately ; but O’Reily could tell more.
Chief Justice Way (S.A.), still full of
work and keen of intellect, is about three
months older than settlement in the Wheat
State. He was born in April, 1836, and
the first ship of the S.A. Co., which financed
the Colonisation Commissioners, arrived at
Kangaroo Island in July of the same year.
Way’s father, a Bible Christian minister,
arrived in Adelaide with his son in 1850.
The son, who happened to be Attorney-
General 25 years later when the Chief Jus-
tice’s chair became vacant, thought he was
the best man for the job, and his colleagues
agreed with him ; and for 37 years past he
has been proving that both he and they were
good judges.
“0.K.” writes re a valuable Australian: —
Claude W. Barker, the Sydney High School boy
who went to Britain eight years ago to team the art
of warship-building, is back in Australia as a qualified
naval architect, with letters after his name. He
took a B.Sc. degree in naval architecture at Glas-
gow University, and is an associate member of the
I.N.A. Melbourne Herald interviewed him on his
arrival last week, and got some interesting informa-
tion from the pushful young man. Conditions of
life being vastly better in Australia than in the
land where he learned his business, Claude Barker
will stay here if the Commonwealth Govt, can make
use of his non-imported brains. Otherwise he will
go back to Glasgow anon. There’s a lot doing on
the Clyde, in his line. “All the yards that cater
for warship work are exceedingly busy. Besides
home orders, there are many ships being built to
the order of fore’gn Governments.” Contractors are
not affected materially by labor troubles just now,
“but the construction of merchant vessels is, to a
certain extent, tied up by the high price of steel.”
The new naval base at Rosvth is a gigantic undertaking,
nevertheless the contractors are a year ahead of their
contract, and it has recently been expanded. The
three great dry- docks in course of construction will
take far larger boats than the Dreadnoughts of to-
day, but any longer and wider boats will not have a
deeper draught than 271 feet, or thereabouts, because
they would have no ports they could conven entlv
enter. The British submarines have been greatly
improved in design, on the first attempts, and the
Admiralty is going in largely for them for harbor
defence. It is a significant fact that the Admiralty
within the last four years has prohibited the inspec-
tion of the naval dockyards by even members of the
IN A. Claude Barker recently got a letter from the
Institute to that effect. And he speaks of it in an
uninjured tone of voice, as though he thought the
Admiralty had need ..of all its caution.
There is dire monotony about that fre-
quent cablegram heading, “L’lster Prepares.”
Possibly a few people in the eastern section
of Ulster “prepare,” but one-half of Ulster
votes Home Rule as a good steady thing.
And if the bit of Ulster that is making a
dizzy ass of itself with rifles and midnight
drills goes preparing too much the other
half may get angry, and throw a potato at
it, and knock it over. When even Belfast
returns a Home Ruler, and the Tories hold
Derry—where the famous Walls were—by
a microscopic majority, the Ulster tradition
becomes thin. The Ulster myth recalls the
long-suffering legend about the old Irish
Parliament, and its corruption and in-
capacity, and the, evidence it supplied of the
Irishman’s unfitness for self-government. Of
course, as no Catholic could either be a
voter or a member the real Irish were the
people who were least represented in that
remarkable gathering which met at College
Green. There were present the descendants
of old imported Scots from Ulster, and de-
i scendants of old imported Englishmen from
Dublin and thereabouts, and nominees sent
over from England to see what they could
pick up. And because this Tory crowed
showed its utter incapacity to govern wfith
even a semblance of intelligence it has been
putting on airs ever since. .
“N. or M.,” from Tasmania: —
Another genuine Turner has come to light, in
Hobart, and Melbourne experts are agreed that the
I “genuine” may, for the present, go without in-
verted commas, though Christie’s, in London, will
give the final decision. The enthusiastic owner ex-
pects to seU at £5OOO, which, seeing that he picked
up the canvas at an auction mart for £l, leaves a
very fair margin of profit. The picture, a seascape,
disguised in cracked varnish, was formerly the pro-
perty of Mrs. Tom Giblin, and was sold at her death.
A clean-up revealed the artist’s signature, and the
rest remains with Christie. There has also appeared
a smaller oil suspected of being Turner’s work, but
of this I can only say, darkly, that more will be
heard anon. Tasmania is a happy hunting-ground
for collectors. It is not so long since a Melbourne
dealer paid a record price for a pair of silver-gilt
communion cups dating back to Stuart times. The
precious things were decorating a kitchen mantel-
piece, and the none-too-affluent ladies who owned
them thought they were brass. They had been the
property of a bishop’s grandfather. Tassy holds a
lot of fine old wood-carving, too, much of it dusted
by owners who would as soon th nk of selling the
roof as of parting with the furniture. There is a
“grandfather” clock of my acquaintance that bears
distressful witness to the methods of Judge Jefferies,
of the Sanguinary Assize. After the snufflng-out of
Monmouth’s rebellion, the carver had his right hand
chopped off as a hint not to go a-treasoning any
' more. Being a sturdy rebel, he instead of dying of
lockjaw, went home and painfully and left-handedly
hacked out a clock-case in memory of the event.
“My heart is pierced. Salve me, O Lord!” is carved
on the front panel. The words are alleged to be the
last spoken bv James 11. A slip of paper pasted
inside bears faded reference to the King’s deposition
and the evils brought about by him “cleaving to
Popery” ; and hang : ng on a nail is the bony hand
that struck so disastrously in a lost cause. Taken
all round, it is rather a grim possession.

WORMS— THE CHILDREN’S ENEMY.
A Safe, Sure and Reliable Specific for WORMS in CHILDREN and ADULTS.
THE symptoms of the presence of worms are very pronounced; but should the indications prove incorrect, a course of COMSTOCK’S “DEAD SHOT”
WORM PELLETS will do no harm whatever. An examination of the stools is the surest way to determine the presence of worms, and when found
treatment should start at once to rid the system of the parasites.
The Long Thread Worm measures from one inch to two inches in length, the upper portion being thin and the lower two-thirds about twice as
thick. This variety also infests the large intestines and the rectum.
The common Thread Worm derives its name from its threadlike appearance. The average length is about one-third of an inch, this description
applying to the female worm. The male worms are much smaller, and do not exhibit half the length of the females. The favorite situation of the
Thread Worm is the large bowel which forms the terminal portion of the intestines. These worms cause severe itching at the lower passage and picking
at the nose. Ansemia, restlessness, irritability, and loss of appetite are also frequent symptoms.
The Round Worm in its appearance, save in color, of course, somewhat resembles the earth-worm in shape. As in the case of the Thread Worms,
the female worm very much exceeds the male worm in size. Whilst the Thread Worm inhabits the large intestine of children, the Round Worm is found
in the small bowel, or that portion of the intestine which immediately comes off from the stomach and makes up the succeeding 20 feet in length of the
digestive tube. Adults, of course, are frequently also affected with Round Worms, and it may be added that they are also subject to be troubled with
Thread Worms.
COMSTOCK’S “DEAD SHOT” WORM PELLETS are a purely vegetable medicinal preparation, compounded on a scientific principle for the
eradication of worms which infest the intestines. They contain specific properties which destroy the worms, but cause no injury to the stomach or
bowels and do not weaken the system. The pellets are prepared in the form of a confection or lolly, have no disagreeable taste or smell; but, on the
contrary, inspire children to take them as they would an ordinary lozenge. Obtainable from all chemists and stores, or direct from The W. H. Comstock
Co., Ltd!, 23 Lang Street, Church Hill, Sydney; and Parish Street, Wellington, N.Z. Post free on receipt of price, 2s. 9d. per packet; six packets, 15s.
