IN BANANA LAND
In a recent case at Rockhampton the
accused, charged under the new Liquor Act
with being upon licensed premises on a
Sunday, pleaded that he visited the pubbery
for the purpose of obtaining a yardman’s
job. It might have been a good defence if
the proprietress hadn’t stated in court that
she wanted no yardman. That man was
fined; but Bung took the hint with enthu-
siasm. In the next Saturday’s paper pretty
nearly a third of the “Wanted” column was
taken up by pubberies beseeching the ser-
vices of yardmen.
There was a painful misprint in a Bris-
bane daily paper last week, when the cable
column burst out into this: —
Thirty thousand athletes passed before the
Kaiser crying “Hell!”
Still, one can say this of the erring comp.
He knew human nature even if he was a bit
weak in his German.
“P.F.”: Mormon missionaries have lately
invaded the Rockhampton district, and
Bishop Halford, Presbyterian Dr. Hay and
sundry other divines have deemed it neces-
sary to devote a special Sunday sermon to
warning their flocks against the blandish-
ments of the Saints from Utah. After
which solemn warning the Eternal Feminine
may be safely trusted to “just want to see”,
what the exhortations of the Mormon elders
really amount to.
Bananaland has just decided to do some-
thing towards growing the stoppers for its
beer bottles. At present most of the corks
come from the countries around the Medi-
terranean. Those that aren’t born there-
abouts come from any other old place except
Australia. Early this year the Queensland
Acclimatization Society, in a fit of energy,
dropped a line to Portugal for some seeds of
the cork-oak. To-day there are over 700
young plants in Brisbane awaiting distri-
bution in the spring. The Society proposes
to send one or two to the head teachers of
schools throughout the State. All the
pedagogue is asked to do with them is to
plant them and write once a year saying
how the young beer cork is thriving, and as
he has only 25.000 other things to do this
is easy. A school playground may not be
the best place in the world for a tree-nursery,
but it’s a fine place for slaughtering the
“Won’t grow in Australia” prejudice.
Practically all the clergy of Brisbane —
Anglican, R.C., Presbyterian and Methodist
—-united the other Sunday to bless Aus-
tralia’s Defence Force and bid it go on
learning to shoot in the way that is straight
and to fight the good fight that will some
day be necessary to keep Australia white
and Christian. Somehow the nearer a par-
son lives to the Equator the easier it seems
for him to be also a man.
Temple Clark, of the Lands Department,
has just produced a pamphlet on the prickly
pear question. Clark, who is in a position
to get the most authentic information, says
that thirty million acres are already in-
fested, and that the pest is spreading at the
rate of a million acres per annum. He
laughs at the proffered cochineal cure, which
has failed in India and S. Africa, and points
out that the insect would need to destroy
200 million tons per annum to be effective.
Yankee chemist Roberts estimates that he
can destroy, by means of his gas, 100,000
acres of pear in 10 years—a mere bagatelle,
says Clark. Yet for his own part Clark
can’t think of anything better to recommend
than the employment of 1000 Chinese, who
could, he estimates, clear 156,000 acres per
annum. Perhaps 100,000 new white settlers
would serve the purpose better, and serve
the country a good deal better also.
PROOF CONCLUSIVE.
BILL (after interminable argument): “ Yer mad , Bob. Yer mad as a snake.”
SAWNY BOB : “I’m not. I'm as sane as you or any other bloke. I've got certificates
from two doctors saying I’m sane.”
“Condamine”: I would not advise The
Bulletin to rob its infant’s money box to
invest in Queensland pear country on the
strength of Roberts’s experiments in destroy-
ing the vegetable porcupine with poisonous
gas. The Yankee chemist has not yet rea-
lised the virility of our national plant. The
pear makes its growth from September to
January, and any check that does not
destroy it only encourages it to show what
it really can do. Roberts's first work was
done in the growing season, and the pear
promptly put on new shoots. The second
attempt has been lately made, and the
branches and leaves are apparently dead.
If they stay dead until next Christmas and
no more arrive from the roots, -writer will
say “Hallelujah!” in a loud voice; also
“Well done, good and faithful servant.” As
to the other Yank who is going to wipe pear
off the landscape, his stated methods are —-
(1) To convert the plant into paper pulp.
As there is only about 6 per cent, of fibre
this is declared by experts to be not nayable.
(2) To make and export jam from the fruit.
Owing to its acidity and the difficulty of
gathering it, this does not sound promising.
(3) To use the fruit for coloring matter
Boiling water destroys all the coloring at
once. Don’t rob anybody’s money-box to
back this method either.
Wowsers Again!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When voices of dole on the breezes
Come down, and the Sabbath is here;
When weird and astonishing wheezes
Inform us the Righteous are near,
From out a grey hedging of whiskers
Proceedeth a voice as in pain,
“Oh, Shame on these wicked young
friskers !”
’Tis Wowsers again!
When pubs are agape on the Monday,
And citizens moisten the lip,
By way of forgetting that Sunday
Was void of the signs of a sip,
The brethren go by with a mutter,
“This Drink of our land is the Bane;
It placeth the Just oh the shutter!” —
’Tis Wowsers again!
When Youth has gone crazy on cricket,
And wild at the sight of the ball.
You hear, “Lo! the bat and the wicket
Are spread as a snare for you all!”
And if, in a game that is fistic,
You show that you’ve muscle and brain,
“Oh, barbarous bouts pugilistic!”—
’Tis Wowsers again !
If Darby should walk with his Mary
An hour after sun has gone down,
Although she be modest and chary,
And prim be the cut of her gown,
Next morning that innocent lisper
Will find her repute’s got a stain,
Because of a mischievous whisper—
’Tis Wowsers again!
If Tom should go backing his fancy,
When colors are gay on the turf;
If frolicsome Netty or Nancy
Should show a neat foot in the surf;
If Jack, when his bosom is mellow,
Meet Phyllis down town in the rain,
And offer to share his umbrella—
Oh! Wowsers again!
They buzz like a horde of mosquitoes,
They bite like a legion of fleas.
They’d shut out the sun with their vetoes,
They’d spy on the mites in the cheese;
When ‘ libels' are breathed at the altar,
And slanders are hatched in the fane
That merit the axe and the halter —
’Tis Wowsers again!
QUEENSLAND.
A. WOWSERITE.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, June 14, 1913.
My Dear Tiiargomindah,—
The local delegate of the Amalgamated
Union of Surplus Governors, Wm. Mac-
gregor, has been rushing at a bit of extra
piece-work during the past few days. On
Wednesday afternoon at the University,
prior to a meeting of the Senate, he un-
loaded degrees on five students. In the
evening he put in an hour or so at the Rail-
way Ball, from 9 o’clock. Lady Mac, the
ex-Miss Mac. Mrs. Cecil Palmer, Mrs. and
Miss Coldham (of Melbourne), Mrs. Pagan
and Miss Evans shared the stage with him.
Palms and four porters in uniform were the
other decorations.
After the ball his Ex. and the Foxton gen-
tleman usher beat it to Childers per railway
train. There the local show was duly
opened.
On Friday the Macgregor split a bottle of
ginger vinegar with the Methodist Confer-
ence. And this afternoon he togged him-
self up as a chancellor the University,
formally hurled open King’s College and
made a long explanation about it. Dr.
Brown, of Sydney, who is occupying a perch
at the vice-regal abode, the Master of King’s
and Professor Steele also threw in a gallon
or two of There was a huge
crowd. The Vision was there, in grey
ninon; Mrs. Scott Fletcher looked well in a
striped brown garment, with Bulgarian em-
broidery on the bodice; Mrs. Digby Denham
and Mrs. W. H. Barnes both wore grey;
and Mrs. Kingsbury was in royal blue.
While his Ex. was speechifying an alarum
clock- —one of the insistent beasts that re-
peat themselves, particularly on cold morn-
ings—began its fell work, and not until
Mrs. Scott Fletcher, with a fine color in her
cheeks, made for the scene of action, did it
stop.
A well-buttressed rumor reaches me that
Capt. Forbes, A.D.C. to the N. S. Wales
Governor, is coming over here for our
August festivities, together with his prettj
wife and Mrs. Sep Levy.
The first Private Assembly of this season
cavorted at the South Brisbane Technical
College last Thursday. There were about
180 cavorters, to the delight of the caterer,
who had been told to expect 250, and had
cooked according to scale. A few hundred-
weight more palms had been hacked out of
the adjacent scenery to decorate the stamp-
ing ground, and poinsettia and scarlet salvia
brightened up the bare expanses on the
supper table. Six first-offenders made their
appearance. They were Emily Hayman,
pretty Agatha Fitzgerald, a Miss Gilles,
Blaine Love, one of Harry McConnel’s
daughters, and the little Ridgway girl.
Noticeable among our younger matrons
were Mrs. George Webster, Mrs. Wilkinson
(she was Marian Darvall), Mrs. Mayo, and
Mrs. Doug Corrie. Mrs. J. T. Bell, Mrs.
McConnel and Mrs. Cecil Palmer were
apparently on the committee. I say
apparently, my dear, for with an almost in-
credible coyness, that hard-working body re-
fuses to see its names in cold print,
u hether this is caused by an earnest desire
to blush unseen, or an equallv ardent long-
ing to avoid explanations with the many
whose toes (according to that large, footless,
colorless animal Rumor) have been tramped
on I know not. Amongst the guests who
trailed fetching draperies was Mrs. Gilbert
W ilson, our home-made songstress. Hers
were black, and liable to set Envy up in the
old line of business.

Monday night was a much more friendly
affair. Jean Dyson-Holland—in a prettv

ranks of the dancing girl. Everybody knew

Suffragettes are about as consistent
other females. An English friend ends

South Brisbane’s Mayoress, Mrs Nevlan
gave a coin tea at her home this afterim™
to swell the funds for the Charity Ball'
Even the humble “tray,” when collected in
bulk or mass, is useful to these charities.
The Women’s Club gave a very successful
coin evening this week in aid of the R es i
dential College for Women. President
Martha Burns saw to it that the plucked
were made happy ; also she saw that quite a
bulky sum was commandeered.
Another deserving show that got a boost

On Thursday Mrs. Crase gave a tea and
to-day Mrs. Arthur Morgan opened a bazaar
to assist the faded exchequer.
Have you read “Veiled Women” yet my
dear? I haven’t. Our Early Victorian
Government decided it was immoral or some-
thing, and sent all the Brisbane shipments
back to the place of abominations whence
they came. Whatever vou do. Thargo-
mindah, don’t forward a copy. Nothing on
earth would now induce us to even peep at
it. Sooner would we be boiled at the stake
on top of the Walls of Derry. Yours in a
swirl of blushes.
AUSTRALIA JANE.
"Oldest Inhabitant”: Arrangements are
being made to remove the remains of Wil-
liam Landsborough, explorer, from Caloundra
to Toowong (Brisbane) Cemetery. Lands-
borough began a career of private exblora-
tion in 1856, and discovered Mount Neb- and
Fort Cooper. He explored the Peak Downs
and Xagoa in 1859. discovered the head of
the Thomson in 1860, and traced the
Gregory and Herbert Rivers to their sources
in 1861. In that year he was engaged by
the Royal Society of Victoria to head an
expedition to the Albert River in search of
Burke and Wills. After many false starts
the party left the depot on February 10,
1862, and journeyed southward, discovering
rich pastoral lands extending along the
wrnters of the Flinders to the Dividing
Range, thence along the Thomson from its
source to the Victoria or Barcoo River, and
next to the Warrego. They travelled along
the Darling to Menindie, and from there
they cut across to Melbourne. For this
achievement Landsborough was presented in
Melbourne with a piece of plate valued at
£5OO. and on subseouently visiting London
the Royal Geographical Society gave him a
gold watch with a few brief remarks cut
into it. After a tour of India and Europe he
returned to Queensland, was elected to the
Legislative Ass., but resigned his seat in 1865,
and took the position of Government Resi-
dent in the Burke district. Subsequently, in
conjunction with G. Phillips, he discovered
the Western River and followed the course
or the Diamantina —named after the Greek
wife of the first Governor of Queensland,
George Bowen. In 1868 he became In-
spector of Brands for East Moreton. He
died in 18S6.

The season continued dry during the first half of last veals resulting in loss of stock and, consequently,
some reduction in wool shorn, but fortunately the advance in the price of wool will,, to a material extent,
make up for the shortage in quantity. The grain harvest was a good one. So far owing to fairly good
autumn rains, the pastoral and agricultural outlook for the winter is on the whole favorable. Trade
continued very active, but in common with the rest of the world, Australia has experienced a tightening
money market, and it has become necessary to impose restrictions in various directions to check undue
expansion, but the trade position is believed' to be sound.
It is with deep regret that the Directors have to announce the death of their esteemed colleagues the
late Mr. David Quixano HenriqueS, and the Rt. Hon. Lord Ilkeston, P.C.

TOBACCO QUALITIES
IT is an admitted fact that present tobacco
crops are eminently unsatisfactory, and
manufacturers who are compelled to
purchase at the present time cannot obtain
so good a tobacco as hitherto. It is owing
to this deterioration that many cigarettes
are degenerating in quality.
THE manufacturers of “Winfred”
Cigarettes are in a unique position.
They hold an enormous stock of the
finest selected tobacco purchased during the
past three or four years, so that they are able
to maintain, for some years to come, the
present high-class quality of their cigarettes
without increasing the price.

Those who are troubled with falling hair, dandruff,
baldness, itching scalp, etc., know full well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-
ever, of such conditions. These troubles can. be
quickly overcome by my True Hair Grower.
I am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
I reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirtj -five the top of my head was quite bald.
I had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here let me
sav that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time —yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, I fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. 1 jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This lead him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later. I
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair.
Since then the hair on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world—l am
not interested in decrying others —but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest.

Going bail for an arrested person is said
to be the merest matter of form in many
Melbourne cases. A friend of the person
in trouble introduces himself to a Jay Pay
as being good for the required guarantee,
and the bond is signed and the prisoner is
free to disappear. Next week, when the
vanished fugitive is marked “absent” on
the charge sheet it appears that the man
who bailed him out is also missing, or else
he turns up with the information that he is
“stone, motherless,” and would never have
incurred a penalty if he could have paid it.
Then the majesty of the law reckons itself
outraged and indignantly blows its nose, or
the Crown Solicitor’s nose, or the Attorney-
General’s nose.
“Vic.”: There are two J. W. Flemings in
Melbourne —one the well-known Anarchist;
the other, J. W. Fleming, of Brunswick, the
oldest Melbourne native. The latter Flem-
ing was born in 1837. His father was a
butcher; Flemington was named after him.
This Fleming remembers Fitzroy when it
had only half a dozen houses and Colling-
wood and Richmond Were wild, uninhabited
bush. He was lost on Collingwood Flat.
Afterwards he became first president of the
Australian Natives’ Association, being
elected at a meeting in an Elizabeth-street
hotel. Apart from that the tnost notable
thing about him is his §4 years in municipal
life, ih the course of Which he has been
seven times Mayor of bricky Brunswick.
From our Tame Court Correspondent •
A cable message of last week mentioned thn fhe
Duchesse d’Orleans (Who was an Austrian •n-
-duchess) is seeking a judicial separation and my
from her husband, the head of the Bourbo i-ans
House, on the score of desertion. If Frr turns
royalist again the warring pair should ha st re-
fusal of the position of king and que Their
chances of getting any such offer are s’ • at the
best, however ; and a matrimonial row die will
do them no good. The Due and Duck .uive been
notoriously bad friends for years r Like so
many of his illustrious ancestors he ... been quick
off the mark all his life, and has m .sed few chances
in the way of frivolity. The ekpl. nation of why a
judicial separation, and hot a divorce, is being
asked for lies, Of course, in the fact that the parties
are Catholics. The Due is a queer contradictory
sort of Character iff divers ways. He took a Commis-
sion in ah, English regiment, and he has all his life
been an advocate of the divine right of monarchy to'
do what it_ dashed well. likes—especially in regard to
a debased institution like a Republic. Yet when the
Boer war broke but he was ail for the Itepublic and
dead against the. hatioh uhder whose flag he had
served. Carari D’Ache, the famous French cartoon-
ist, was producing amuSing, but highly libellous and
occasionally obscene, drawings of Chamberlain, Lord
Roberts, Kitchener, the Queen and others at the
time. The Due wrote a letter of warm congratula-
tion to Caran D’Ache. Simultaneously with its pub-
lication appeared art absolutely hair-raising picture
of Queen Victoria which set half Europe laughing.
The Due was kicked but Of the bachelors’ Club and
cut by English society. At Wood Norton 1 , his coun-
try place ih England, he kept up precisely the same
royal state as though he were King of France.
Within the confines of this Comparatively small
estate the whole mummery of royalty was gone
through, year in, year Out. This indicates that he
possesses at least one essential as a monarch—
namely, absence of a Sehse Of the ridiculous.
The person who objects to compulsory
training for the defence of the white women
and children in Australasia and the purity
of the race’s blood is a weird and padded-
room variety of crank. The other day he
was the author of a communication to Dr.
McArthur, S.M., Wellington (M.L.), urging
that popular Beak to throw up his billet
rathef than carry oht the law of the country
which fines those who refuse to drill. Said
this remarkable individual:—»
The compulsory military training is a notorious
outrage upon British freedom. It is an abomination,
a monstrosity and a damnable hobby ; and those who
enforce it are deserving of the utmost abuse. I
wish you would show this letter to the Minister of
Justice. Those who want to serve the Devil may
do so, but they have no right to compel others.
(Please keep your eye upon God’s judgment, nigh
at hand.)
The difficulty is to know exactly what form
God’s judgment will take. The Koreans
Teckon it came their way in the shape of a
Japanese horde that set fire to their Queen
and enslaved the country because they had
no army to defend themselves with. You
never can tell.
As soon as they get a few minutes’ breath-
ing time Commonwealth Ministers ought to
dip into the Federal land tax tangle, and
see if they can’t straighten things out.
What sort of a tangle it is can best be
judged by the fact that the staff is still at
work on the 1910 tax, making all sorts of
adjustments. The principal trouble is over
the land held by companies in which people
are interested who are taxpayers in respect
of other land. Their microscopic interest in
the co.’s land sometimes puts them up in
another class, and makes them liable for
another farthing or so; and the digging out
of this extra farthing seems to be costing a
heap of money. The whole business is in
need of simplification, and the officers ought
to have enough experience now to be able
to suggest where the simplification should
come in. Tax-gathering from people is a
good deal like metal-extracting from ore.
The metallurgist is always aiming at a 100
per cent, recovery, which means theoretical
perfection ; but his bosses know better than
to let him run things on theoretical-perfec-
tion lines. They want profits, and there is
no profit in nosing around at pounds of ex-
pense after the elusive, ultimate ha’penny.
[S. H. Prior, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
“Torres” speaks a literary
Cabled that Field-Marshal Roberts (of Course)
gives his enthusiastic approval to a vague scheme
for colonising Melville Island with old English sol-
diers. For 26 years the residents have been lessee
Robinson, two whites and a number of blacks, work-
ing the buffalo-slaughtering “industry.” The grand-
iloquent English newspapers, peppering Australia
with high-flown language, as usual, dubs Robinson
the Buffalo King; so the Cattle King, Wool King,
Silver King, Cotton King, and so forth, have an-
other mate. Why isn’t there a Turnip King? Mel-
ville Island, from 20 to 30 miles off Darwin, is 18
times bigger than the Isle of Wight, and has
1,536,000 acres. Robinson has been in Melbourne
lately. Through the agency of one Lloyd, he has
been negotiating for years with the Australian Gov-
ernment. He obtained a freehold grant of the island
in 1873, when he landed there, but got it under
conditions. He was, however, able to comply with
the conditions in respect to only 5000 acres,
which he got at 2s. 6d. per acre, payable in 14
years. This he still holds. He thinks grants of
freehold would be the wise thing, and doubts whether
the right men will be attracted otherwise. With re-
gard to the future he recalls that Malta carries
216,000 people, and it is only one-twentieth the size
of Melville, and hasn’t as rich soil or as equable a
climate. The old-soldier proposal, to be bossed by
an English co., provides for Javanese or Papuan
labor, with the redcoats as just fighting cocks.
Robinson says no to this. It is not generally known
that an unsuccessful attempt was made long ago to
colonise Melville and the mainland with soldiers.
Give us men who can work and intend to work, says
the old hand; no others will do for the North.

“ONLY A COLD!”
A LITTLE TALK TO BUSINESS MEN.
“ONLY A COLD.” but a man's head is not clear for business. He may not be
able to grasp the opportunity that he lias waited for so long. His appearance is
against him, too. With swollen face and reddened nose he cannot meet his employer
or business friend with that look of confidence which inspires confidence in return.
Socially he feels himself a nuisance—almost an outcast. Hardly ill enough to compel
him to abandon his engagements, he moves about, the embodiment of physical distress,
and only awakens the feeling of pity or disgust in his acquaintances.
“ONLY A COLD,” to which a man must not give in, but must run the risk of
more serious ills by braving the weather, no matter how inclement it may be. How
many cases of Pneumonia and other serious illnesses, causing the deaths of fathers
of families, result yearly from “ONLY A COLD”?

The Silent Retinue.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Broken Hill Proprietary Company is erecting
steel works at Newcastle at a cost of three million
pounds.— News item.
Messrs. Noske Bros, will despatch to the Port
Melbourne railway pier this week 200 tons of flour,
which will be sent per cargo steamer to Jaffa, on
the coast of Syria, 35 miles north-west of the Citv
of Jerusalem. Formerly the flour for consumption
by the white population of the Holy Land was ob-
tained from Egypt, the ancient granary of Southern
Asia, but of late years Australian flour has found a
market in this part of Turkish territory. This is
the first occasion on which Nhill wheat has been for-
warded to Jerusalem.— Nhill Free Press.
long Express from Adelaide a
I moment paused at Nhill—
At midnight in the Wimmera I saw
the gleaming Mill!
A blaze of light symbolical, a square, deter-
mined tower —-
A strong Australian arsenal of living force
and power!
The sleepers in their carriages saw not these
windowed stars
That flashed beside their cavalcade of east-
ward-speeding cars !
They neither heard nor dreamed of it, yet
in the rolling train
I heard this Psalm Antiphonal—the Anthem
of the Grain!
First sang the Mill exultantly: “Know ye
the Holy Land?
’Tis here ! Not in Jerusalem ’midst barren
rocks and sand !
This is the soil immaculate, this is the sacred
earth ;
Praise ye the mighty Wimmera with songs
of triple worth!
Up from this soil primordial I draw the
golden wheat —
Mine engines dance a saraband with swift,
ecstatic feet!
Hear ye each valve and cylinder lift up its
proud refrain —
‘We are Australia’s acolytes, the servants of
the Grain!”
Now sang the cars swift answering: “He
link the cities twain —
We carry the ambassadors of mighty Flin-
ders Lane !
Yea, not alone to Adelaide bind we the Mel-
bourne mart —-
We reach the cities luminous that lead in
Trade and Art!
We roll by Yass and Albury, we cross the
Murray’s stream—
For us and our ambassadors the lights of
Sydney gleam !
Nay, more than that! —from Broken Hill
we roll with calm disdain
Towards Longreach and to Jericho! Cease
now your Psalm of Grain!”
Then laughed the Mill right scornfully : “I
know the Path ye go—
A Path now wide, now narrowing—a fig for
Jericho!
I know your City Luminous —-that spend-
thrift by the sea
Whose merciless steel tentacles have stran-
gled Industry !
I know yon distant Albury—’tis there the
narrowed Way
Hangs like a sword Damoclean above this
Land to-day!
Ye boast of proud ambassadors —a fig for
Flinders Lane;
I am the Mill omnipotent, and Jaffa knows
my Grain !”
Next mocked the planished cylinders that
drew the swift Express:
“We bring besides ambassadors the Great
who rule the Less !
Hear now yon loud diapason—'tis Wealth’s
united snore ;
The rich men sleep complacently, with boots
outside each door!
This is their Caravanserai —their fast hotel
on wheels ;
Whilst valve and cam throb rhythmical, they
eat their lordly meals !
Ours is the splendid retinue of wealth, and
in Wealth’s train
Our waiters haste obsequious, so prate no
more of Grain !”
Up from the Mill’s vast diaphragm I heard
the challenge peal:
“Who are the Rich but pensioners upon the
Workers’ zeal?
Bring ye the Lords of Dividends from dust-
stormed Broken 11 ill—
Bring ye your herd of millionaires —yea,
whilst the coffins fill!
Hark to the Car Invisible that rolls behind
them all —-
Filled with the SiDnt Retinue who wait the
Trumpet’s Call !
Hark to the sad antiphony sent from the
Car of Pain —
Theirs is the Rich Man’s requiem, mine is
the Psalm of Grain !”
Then from the Car Invisible I heard the
answer break :
“We are the Last Lean Dividend that these,
forsooth, shall take!
We died upon the Barrier —yea, for the rich
man’s sins,
We bled in crimson sacrifice in crosscut,
stope and winze!
The fracteur-fumes our altar reek —on swift-
revolving wheels
We follow Greed and Callousness until the
Trumpet peals !
Then shall the Last Lean Dividend be drawn
by staggered Cain”—
The Answer ceased, and rolling dread I saw
the Car of Pain !
Then sang the Mill compassionate: “The
ancient Empire’s pass —
I feed the Copt beneath the Cross raised by
this Ruling Class !
Whilst Christian and Mahommedan are
locked in grips of death,
I succor far Jerusalem and also Nazareth.
Mine is the Path the nation treads in earnest
dungarees,
Whilst Cain and co. on flying wheels pass
like proud Pharisees!
I am the heir of Raineses, who ruled oer
Egypt’s plain— . . _ ,
My Broken Hills lie underneath the fields of
yellow grain !”
A blaze of light symbolical, a square, deter-
mined tower —- ,
I saw Australia’s arsenal of living strength
and power! , , , ...
I saw the Silent Retinue, and heard this
song till dawn —
This song that thrilled till jarred by some
ambassadorial yawn !
Till waiters came with morning tea, I heard
this distant Mill
That answered Cain with energy and chal-
lenged Broken Hill!
But Cain is proud— he does not hear this
undertone of Pain ,
The Barrier’s antiphonal hlent with the
Psalm of Grain!
N.s.w.
GRANT HERVEY.
Elder Man’s Lane.
V.—THE KIDS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OTHER vehicles go down Blue’s Point-
road to the Horse Ferry and the Gov-
ernment Wharf. They are made of
boxes, and have two little cast-iron wheels
fastened on underneath ; and roughly shaped
strips of packing-case nailed to the sides and
slanting well upwards, for shafts. But some
have a board projection in front from under-
neath, with other little wheels, the bar at-
tached with a sort of swivel, or king-pin—
or pivot; a sort of fore under carriage—
they might be called driving wheels. The
latter build, with clothes-line traces re-
versed and used as tiller-ropes (only the
tiller is for’ard) are admirably adapted to
“coasting.” Going dowm they invariably
contain a “kid”; and, when not in actual
legitimate use by their serious-minded and
practical proprietors, they may be seen
ciuising round, in charge of a still younger
brother, or sister, maybe—with the family
baby aboard; said baby always clean and
contented, and often asleep. The careful
conductor’s attention, on such occasions, is
divided between guarding against a run-
away, and pushing up baby’s ridiculous little
white woollen cap, which is always getting
down over its eyes.
But in workin’ hours the carts are pushed
or dragged by little gentlemen of two-foot-
odd and upwards; aud in times of extra
wreckage and spoil they often go up hill
tandem ; a mate, who is not the proprietor
of a cart, being sometimes pressed into ser-
vice and paid in shares. The carts are
dragged up the steep hill with surprising
loads of lumps of coal and odds gnd ends
of lumber. And I have seen several large
pumpkins—a mighty load for a little man—
that were jolted off a produce cart going
on to the landing. My friend, Benno, the
bottle merchant, and others, often give the
early youngsters a hitch on coming up in
the morning before school hours. Every-
thing is extremely business-like, and pre-
ternaturally solemn.
Besides road, wharf, jetty and landing
place, there is a little sandy beach, round
by the shipsmith’s shop, where is driftwood
on a tiny scale; and flotsam in the shape of
butter boxes, fruit cases, occasional bottles,
etc., from ocean-going craft. This is acces-
sible by means of a tiny punt and ditto
dingy, belonging to the shipsmith’s boys.
There is co-operation, and Socialism, and a
system of barter in kind and for services
rendered.
But for the chartering of the aforesaid
craft, belonging to the “shipsmith’s kids,”
there must be “browns” or “coppers,” of
which more hereafter. Of course, there is
a bloated capitalist and financier amongst
my youngsters. And, for the rest, the stiand
is fairly portioned, and spoils are fairly sold.
Anything that comes ashore on the strip of
sand between the floating jetty and a line
projected from the further wall of the ship-
smith’s shop belongs to my friend and
neighbor, “Georgie,” and his immediate
friends ; anything cast up between that line
and the line of the first row of the piles of
the Fresh Food Co.’s jetty is the exclusive
property of my acquaintance, “Stinker,” and
his pals. Stinker, by the way, was nick-
named thusly on account of a mistaken
maternal parent having christened him
Cecil Percival Reginald. The expanse be-
tween the niles is the happy hunting-ground
of one “Bugs,” Stinker’s protegd, a boy
with a club foot. He was so called in a
hurry, and for the want of a more expres-
sive nickname, on the occasion of the un-
premeditated arrival in the lane of Bugs’
family, with their worldly belongings in a
hired bottle-o’s cart. “By Gee!” exclaimed
Stinker, to the Lords in Convention, on that
occasion—and with a flash of inspiration
during a hurried conference on the question
of appropriately naming the newcomer: “By
Gee, we’ll call him Bugs!” And Bugs he
became. Disputed floatage, that is on the
lines of demarcation, is tossed for. The strip
of sand is called Drowned Pup Beach.
Speaking of flotsam and drift, I remem-
ber, in bygone years, a younger cousin of
mine who had a canvas dingy. His mother
seldom, if ever, had to buy wood or coal,
summer and winter; and, besides, Harry
kept himself well supplied in pocket money
from the proceeds of the sale of bits of
brass, and copper, and copper nails, and
other strange flotsam. Strikes me that tides
were far more erratic along the water front-
ages in those old days, than they are now;
and the water of far greater floating power.
The youngsters recognise a woman’s
rights—in their proper place and under cer-
tain proper circumstances —to a small ex-
tent. There are little ladies, neat and clean
but sometimes barefoot, who humn surpris-
ing armfuls of soft wood scraps up that road
and round into by-lanes, for mother’s cop-
per, pretty much as they hump big baby
brothers round. Stinker and his brethren
will, on special occasions, and with a sort
of good-natured contempt, throw out a few
sticks from their loads for the girls to pick
up. (Contributions received disdainfully,
and after a due, and nose-in-the-air allow-
ance of time for “them fellers” to get past
—but picked up, all the same.)
Round in the by-ways and lanes from
Blue’s Point-road, and above Berry’s Bay—
though blind for the most part, unfortu-
nately, and so not overlooking the bay and
the surprisingly wild bit of bush and cliff
scenery of Ball’s Head beyond—round up
there, I say, in an unexplored region, are
rows of the usual mean and shabby little
weatherboard boxes that depress, dishearten
and disgrace our further suburbs nearly as
much as the horrid little new, brick two-

FAIRBANKS-MORSE VERTICAL
Geared Kerosene Hoisting Engine.
The main parts of the engine are enclosed in a dustproof crank case. This keeps them free
from dust and grit, and protects them from undue wear. Inside the crank case is a bath of
lubricating oil. The main parts are in this way self-lubricating.
